Huduth: Temporal and Essential Origination
The author defines huduth and distinguishes temporal from essential origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of huduth and its two types: temporal (preceded by non-existence) and essential (preceded by a cause).

Core Idea
Huduth is precededness—either by non-existence or by a cause.

This lesson explores the core kalam concept of huduth (origination). The author begins by explaining that huduth is not a simple notion but requires a preceding something. He then distinguishes two kinds of precedence, giving rise to two distinct types of huduth: temporal and essential. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later discussions on the eternity of the world and the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a 'nugget' — a concise, dense statement.
2
Grasp the definition of huduth
The author defines huduth as 'mashqiyyat wujud al-shay' (the precededness of the existence of a thing). Make sure you understand that huduth is a relational concept.
It is not the thing itself but a property of the thing relative to a prior.
3
Identify the two kinds of precedence
The preceding something can be either non-existence (which cannot coexist with the later) or another thing (the cause) which can coexist. Note the coexistence condition.
Temporal huduth requires that the prior does not coexist; essential huduth allows coexistence.
4
Connect precedence to types of huduth
If the prior is non-existence, huduth is temporal. If the prior is the cause, huduth is essential. See how the author derives these two types.
Essential huduth applies to all possible things because they are all caused.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
The passage concludes that essential eternity (qidam dhati) is unique to the Necessary Existent (Allah). Ask yourself: Why is that? Because if something were essentially eternal, it would have no cause.
This ties into the proof for God's existence from contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نكتة) الحدوث لا يعقل إلا بسبق أمر عليه ) أي على الحادث لأن الحدوث عبارة عن مسبوقية وجود الشيء فلا يعقل إلا بأمر سابق عليه ( فهو ) أي ذلك السابق (إما عدمه ) الذي يمتنع مع اللاحق، أو أمر آخر يمكن اجتماعه معه . ( وإنما اختلف تفسيره نظرا إليه ) أي إلى ذلك الأمر، فإذا اعتبر تقدم العدم كان الحدوث زمانياً، لامتناع اجتماع المتقدم والمتأخر. وإذا اعتبر تقدم غير العدم وهو العلة كان الحدوث ذاتياً شاملاً للممكنات بأسرها اتفاقاً؛ لأن كل ممكن مسبوق بعلته سبقاً يجامع فيه السابق اللاحق فيكون القدم الذاتي مختصاً بالواجب تعالى
A nugget: Huduth cannot be conceived except by the precedence of something over it, i.e., over the originated thing, because huduth is the expression of the precededness of the existence of a thing, so it cannot be conceived except by a thing prior to it. So that prior is either its non-existence, which cannot coexist with the later, or another thing which can coexist with it. Its interpretation differs according to that thing: if the precedence of non-existence is considered, the huduth is temporal, due to the impossibility of the coexistence of the prior and posterior; and if the precedence of other than non-existence, i.e., the cause, is considered, the huduth is essential, including all possibles by agreement, because every possible is preceded by its cause with a precedence in which the prior coexists with the later. Thus essential eternity is specific to the Necessary Exalted.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of huduth (origination) and distinguishes its two types. The author begins by stating that huduth cannot be conceived except as being preceded by something. He then explains that the preceding thing is either the non-existence of the thing (which cannot coexist with the later existence) or another thing (the cause) that can coexist. He then ties this to the two types: if the priority of non-existence is considered, it is temporal huduth because the prior and posterior cannot coexist; if the priority of the cause is considered, it is essential huduth, which applies to all possible things by agreement, since every possible is preceded by its cause, and this precedence allows coexistence of the predecessor and successor. Hence essential eternity is specific to the Necessary Existent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That huduth only means temporal origination (coming into existence in time).
Correction: Huduth can also be essential, meaning preceded by a cause, which does not involve a gap in time. Essential huduth applies to all possible beings.

Checkpoint
What are the two types of huduth according to the author?
A) Temporal and essential
B) Possible and impossible
C) Causal and effectual
D) Eternal and originated

Key Takeaway
Huduth is fundamentally precededness, and its two types—temporal and essential—are distinguished by whether the prior is non-existence or the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You and everything you own are preceded by non-existence—you were nothing. How does that truth change the way you view your accomplishments today? Will you now thank the One who brought you from non-existence to existence?
?
If every possible thing (including you) is essentially originated, meaning it depends on a cause at every moment, then you are never self-sufficient. When was the last time you felt your utter dependence on Allah? What would it take for you to live in that awareness right now?
?
Essential huduth means your existence is always preceded by Allah's will and power—not just at the moment of creation, but constantly. Does your daily life reflect this, or do you act as if you are independent? What one habit can you change to remind yourself of this continuous dependence?

The Requirements of Temporal Huduth: Matter and Time
Philosophers' claim that temporal huduth requires matter and time, with threefold classification of matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal huduth necessarily requires both matter and time according to the philosophers, and how matter is defined differently for accidents, forms, and souls.

Core Idea
Temporal huduth demands a substratum—matter—and a temporal duration.

This passage introduces the second investigation of huduth, where the philosophers argue that any event in time (temporal origination) must have a prior substratum (matter) and occur within a time frame. The concept of matter is nuanced: it can be a subject, hyle, or body depending on what originates. Understanding this foundational claim is crucial for grasping the Islamic philosophical position on creation and change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is part of a larger discussion on huduth.
2
Identify the three types of matter
The passage describes three kinds of mater (المادة) depending on the type of originated entity. Find each one: subject (موضوع) for accidents, hyle (هيولى) for forms, and body (جسم) for souls.
The passage also notes that 'matter' can be interpreted as hyle alone.
3
Reflect on the need for time
The passage states that temporal huduth also requires time (مدة/زمان). Think about why: because the originated thing before its existence is possible, and possibility is an existential attribute that implies a temporal dimension.
This connects to earlier discussions on possibility (إمكان).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها) أي ثاني أبحاث الحدوث (أنه قال الحكماء : الحدوث بمعنى المسبوقية بالعدم وهو الحدوث الزماني يستدعي مادة ) أي محلاً، إما موضوعاً إن كان الحادث عرضاً وإما هيولى إن كان الحادث صورةً، وإما جسماً يتعلق به الحادث إن كان الحادث نفساً. وقد تفسر المادة بالهيولى وحدها؛ لأن الموضوع والمتعلق مشتملان عليها . ( ومدةً) أي زماناً (أما المادة فلانه ) أي الحادث قبل وجوده ،(ممكن، وهو ظاهر . ) والإمكان ( أمر ( وجودي ) ؛ لما مر من
And the second of them, i.e., the second investigation of huduth: that the philosophers said: huduth in the sense of being preceded by non-existence, which is temporal huduth, requires a matter, i.e., a locus: either a subject if the originated thing is an accident, or hyle if the originated thing is a form, or a body to which the originated thing attaches if the originated thing is a soul. And matter is sometimes interpreted as hyle alone, because the subject and the attached thing both include it. And a duration, i.e., time. As for matter, it is because the originated thing before its existence is possible, and that is evident. And possibility is an existential attribute, as has passed.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the second investigation of huduth, stating the claim of the philosophers that temporal huduth (being preceded by non-existence) requires both matter (madda) and time (zaman). The author explains that matter is a substratum (mahall) which takes different forms: a subject (mawdu') if the originated thing is an accident, hyle (hayula) if it is a form, or a body (jism) if it is a soul (nafs). It is also permissible to interpret matter as hyle alone, since both the subject and the body that the soul attaches to contain hyle. As for time, it is required because the originated thing before its existence is possible, and possibility is an existential attribute (as previously established).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal huduth requires matter only for physical bodies.
Correction: The philosophers extend matter to all temporal things: accidents require a subject, forms require hyle, and souls require a body. The concept of matter is broadened beyond mere physicality.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what kind of matter does a soul require?
A) A subject (موضوع)
B) Hyle (هيولى)
C) A body (جسم)
D) No matter

Key Takeaway
Temporal huduth necessarily requires both a material substratum (with varying definitions) and time, because the originated thing is possible and possibility is an existential attribute implying temporal duration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers assert that your very soul requires a body as its substratum. Do you treat your body as a mere vessel for your soul, or as a trust from Allah? In light of this dependency, how should you care for it?
?
You were once nothing, preceded by non-existence. This lesson forces you to confront that your existence is not self-grounded. When you feel self-sufficient, does remembering your pure possibility humble you? What one change would you make today to live in constant awareness of your dependence on the Creator who brought you from non-existence into existence?

Why Matter (Madda) is Interpreted as Hylic Matter (Hayula)
The commentator explains why madda is interpreted as hayula alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars interpret madda as hayula alone when discussing origination, and how the terms subject (mawdu) and related (muta'alliq) each encompass hayula in different ways.

Core Idea
Madda means hayula only, because both subject and related imply hayula.

When investigating the origination of accidents and forms, scholars use the term 'madda' (matter). This lesson explores a key commentarial clarification: 'madda' is interpreted solely as hylic matter (hayula), not as a body or other substrate. The commentator explains why by showing that both the 'subject' (mawdu) and the 'related' (muta'alliq) necessarily include hayula, either as a part or as a necessary concomitant. Understanding this distinction prevents common objections and clarifies the foundational vocabulary of kalam cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the literal translation. Let the text’s own terms sink in before analysis.
The passage is dense; take your time with the translation.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence stating that madda is interpreted as hayula alone, and note the reason given: 'because the subject and the related both encompass it.'
This is the main thesis of the passage.
3
Understand the definitions of 'subject' and 'related'
The commentator defines 'subject' (mawdu) as a locus that sustains a state (hal), and 'related' (muta'alliq) as a locus to which the occuring thing is related. Notice that both can include body, hylic matter, or soul depending on context.
These definitions are crucial for the argument.
4
Follow the objections and answers
The commentator raises two potential objections to the claim and resolves them by clarifying the senses of 'subject'. This shows how the interpretation holds up under scrutiny.
The objections are about higher principles and hylic matter itself.
5
Grasp the twofold inclusion
The passage concludes by explaining that 'encompassment' can mean whole-to-part (body or related includes hayula as a part) or necessitating-to-necessary (soul entails body, which entails hayula).
This completes the justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما جسماً يتعلق به الحادث إن كان الحادث نفساً. وقد تفسر المادة بالهيولى وحدها؛ لأن الموضوع والمتعلق مشتملان عليها. قوله: (أي محلاً) لإمكان الحادث، أو محلاً للحادث، بأن يراد بالمحل أعم من أن يكون محله حقيقة أو شبيهاً به، ليدخل الجسم بالقياس إلى النفس. قوله: (إما موضوعاً) أي محلاً يقوم الحال، سواء كان جسماً أو صورة أو هيولى أو نفساً بالقياس إلى أعراضها. قوله: (إن كان الحادث عرضاً) لأن الحال المتقوم بالمحل عرض. قوله: (وإما هيولى) أي محلاً متقوماً بالحال، سواء كانت هيولى أولى، أو ثانية كالعناصر بالقياس إلى صور المركبات. قوله: (إن كان الحادث صورة) لأن الحال المقوم للمحل صورة. قوله: (لأن الموضوع) أي الموضوع الذي قصد بتعميم المادة إدخاله أعني الجسم بالقياس إلى أعراضها الحالة فيه والنفس الناطقة بالقياس إلى صفاتها النفسانية المتجددة، كالألم واللذة والسرور والغم، فلا يرد أنه لو أريد به الموضوع مطلقاً انتقض بالمبادئ العالية، فإنها موضوعات لأعراضها مع عدم اشتمالها على المادة. وإن أريد به موضوع الحادث انتقض بالهيولى بالقياس إلى أعراضها؛ لعدم اشتمالها على المادة. قوله: (مشتملان عليها) اشتمال الكل على الجزء، كما في الجسم بالقياس إلى أعراضه والمتعلق بالقياس إلى النفس، أو اشتمال الملزوم على اللازم كما في النفس الناطقة بالقياس إلى الأعراض الحادثة فيها فإنها لاستلزامها البدن مستلزمة للهيولى.
And also a body to which the occuring thing is related if the occuring thing is a soul. And it (madda) has been interpreted as hylic matter alone because the subject and the related both encompass it. His saying: (i.e., a locus) for possibility of the occuring thing, or a locus for the occuring thing, so that by 'locus' is meant what is more general, being either its locus in reality or similar to it, so that the body is included with respect to the soul. His saying: (either as a subject) i.e., a locus that sustains the state, whether it be a body or an image or hylic matter or a soul with respect to its accidents. His saying: (if the occuring thing is an accident) because the state subsisting by the subject is an accident. His saying: (or as hylic matter) i.e., a locus that subsists through the state, whether it be primary hylic matter, or secondary like the elements with respect to forms of composites. His saying: (if the occuring thing is a form) because the state that constitutes the locus is a form. His saying: (because the subject) i.e., the subject intended by generalizing matter to include it, namely the body with respect to its accidents that inhere in it, and the rational soul with respect to its psychical adventitious attributes, like pain, pleasure, joy, and grief, so that it is not objected that if by 'subject' is meant absolutely, it would be refuted by the higher principles, for they are subjects for their accidents while not containing matter. And if by it is meant the subject of the occuring thing, it would be refuted by hylic matter with respect to its accidents, for it does not contain matter. His saying: (both encompass it) (encompassment) as the whole encompasses the part, as in the body with respect to its accidents and the related with respect to the soul, or as the necessitating factor encompasses the necessity, as in the rational soul with respect to the adventitious accidents that occur in it, for because it necessitates the body, it necessitates hylic matter.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'madda' (matter) in the context of the investigation of origination (al-huduth). The main text states that if the occuring thing (al-hadith) is a soul, then its locus is a body with which it is related. It then asserts that matter (madda) is interpreted solely as hylic matter (hayula) because both the subject (mawdu) and the related (muta'alliq) encompass it. The commentator first explains the general meaning of 'mahall' (locus) as the place of possibility for the occuring thing or the place of the occuring thing itself. He defines the subject (mawdu) as a locus that sustains a state (hal), and specifies that if the occuring thing is an accident (arad), then its locus is a subject; if it is a form (sura), its locus is hylic matter. The commentator then addresses potential objections: if 'subject' is taken absolutely, it would be refuted by the example of the higher principles (al-mabadi al-'aliya) which are subjects for their own accidents but do not contain matter; if taken specifically as the subject of the occuring thing, it is refuted by hylic matter relative to its accidents, since hylic matter does not contain matter. Therefore, the commentator clarifies that the inclusion of hylic matter in the subject and the related is twofold: either as the inclusion of the whole in the part, as in the body relative to its accidents and the related relative to the soul, or as the inclusion of the necessitating factor in the necessity, as in the rational soul relative to its adventitious accidents, since its necessitation of the body entails the inclusion of hylic matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that madda in kalam refers to any substrate such as body or soul.
Correction: The passage clarifies that in the context of origination, madda is specifically hayula, and the terms subject and related are defined broadly to include body and soul only insofar as they entail hayula.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is madda interpreted solely as hayula?
A) Because the subject and the related both encompass hayula.
B) Because the occuring thing is always a body.
C) Because hayula is the only substance.
D) Because accidents do not exist.

Key Takeaway
Madda is identified with hayula alone because every relevant locus—whether subject or related—either contains hayula as a part or inevitably entails it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Did you recognize that your soul was the subject of that state? How does the fact that your soul's states are tied to the body (through necessity) affect the way you treat your body in moments of spiritual or emotional intensity? What concrete action can you take today to honor that connection?
?
The commentator emphasizes that 'madda' is interpreted as hylic matter alone through a careful logical argument about subjects and relations. How often do you reflect on the precision of the concepts that underlie your faith, such as what it means for God to create from nothing? Does this passage challenge you to think more carefully about the definitions of terms you use in discussing your beliefs? What is one concept you will examine more deeply this week?
?
The passage addresses potential objections by refining the definition of 'subject' so that it includes both body and soul. In your own life, are you ever tempted to simplify spiritual truths to avoid intellectual difficulty? Where might you be avoiding a deeper understanding of your faith because it requires careful reasoning? Will you commit to studying one difficult theological point until you grasp its logical structure, trusting that it will strengthen your iman?

The Example of Divine Power and Possibility
Al-Jurjani analyzes the example of power as a separate substrate for possibility and refutes a misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of 'the power of the capable' serves as an analogy for possibility and why the author refines the wording to avoid equating possibility with power.

Core Idea
Possibility is not a power; it is a potential grounded in the agent's power.

In this passage, al-Jurjani unpacks a subtle but crucial example from the main text. Theologians often used analogies to explain possibility. Here, the author compares possibility to 'the power of the capable' to illustrate the rejected concept of a separate substrate. By analysing the author's exact wording, al-Jurjani shows how the analogy works only under a specific condition and clarifies what 'capability' truly means. This lesson will help you follow his reasoning and avoid a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the parenthetical expressions as they indicate commentary.
2
Identify the example and its purpose
Notice that the example is 'like the power of the capable' (كقدرة القادر). Al-Jurjani explains that it is an example for the concept of possibility to illustrate the separate thing (الأمر المنفصل). Ask yourself: what is being compared?
3
Understand the author's choice of wording
Al-Jurjani points out that the author did not say 'like the capable' but specifically said 'like the power of the capable'. This is to stress that the agent can be a substrate for possibility only if possibility is defined as his power. Reflect on why this condition matters.
The phrase 'according to what some have imagined' targets those who mistakenly identify possibility with power.
4
Grasp the final clarification on 'capability'
The commentator clarifies that 'capability' (اقتدار) here means potential power, not the real attribute or actual capability. This distinction prevents confusion with possibility. Make sure you can differentiate these three senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي كالفاعل القادر فالتمثيل المذكور تمثيل للإمكان ليعلم منه تمثيل الأمر المنفصل. والتقديركان يكون الإمكان قدرة القادر، فيكون محله الفاعل المباين للحادث. وإنما لم يقل : كالقادر إشارة إلى أن صحة كونه محلاً لإمكان الحادث موقوفة على أن يكون الإمكان عبارة عن قدرته . قوله على ما توهمه بعضهم فيه إشارة إلى أن المعرض بخصوص القدرة للتنصيص بالرد عليهم، وإلا فالأولى التعميم بأن يقال : كصحة صدوره من الفاعل . قوله : صحة اقتدار القادر فيه إشارة إلى أن المراد بالقدرة الاقتدار بالقوة لا الصفة الحقيقية ولا الاقتدار بالفعل؛ إذ لا اشتباه لهما بالإمكان.
His statement: (i.e., like the capable agent) So the mentioned example is an example of possibility to make known from it the example of the separate thing. And the estimation would be that possibility is the power of the capable, so its substrate is the agent separate from the temporal. And he did not say 'like the capable' as an indication that the correctness of it being a substrate for the possibility of the temporal depends on possibility being an expression of his power. His statement: 'according to what some have imagined' contains an indication that the mention of power specifically is to explicitly refute them; otherwise, it would be more appropriate to generalize by saying: 'like the correctness of its issuance from the agent'. His statement: 'the correctness of the capable's capability' contains an indication that what is meant by power is capability in potency, not the real attribute nor capability in act; since there is no confusion between them and possibility.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the example given in the main text: 'like the power of the capable'. The commentator clarifies that the example is meant to illustrate the rejected candidate of a 'separate thing' (amr munfaṣil) that is nevertheless related to the possible entity. The analogy proposes that possibility might be likened to the power of a capable agent, so the substrate would be the agent himself, who is separate from the temporal entity. The commentator notes that the author deliberately said 'like the power of the capable' rather than 'like the capable' to emphasize that the suitability of the agent as a substrate depends on possibility being identical with the agent's power (i.e., the agent's ability to create). Then, the phrase 'according to what some have imagined' is discussed: it specifically targets the mistaken identification of possibility with the agent's power, and the commentator suggests it would have been more general to say 'like the correctness of its issuance from the agent'. Finally, the commentator clarifies that 'capability' (iq tidār) here means potential power (al-iqtidār bi-l-quwwa), not the real attribute (al-ṣifa al-ḥaqīqiyya) nor actual capability, since those are not confused with possibility.

Key Terms
قدرة القادر
التمثيل
الأمر المنفصل
الاقتدار
القوة
الصفة الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is identical to the agent's power (e.g., divine power).
Correction: The author specifically refutes this by showing that possibility is not power; the example only illustrates a separate substrate conditionally.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say is the purpose of using the specific example 'like the power of the capable'?
A) To show that possibility is identical to divine power.
B) To indicate that the agent is the substrate for possibility only if possibility is defined as his power.
C) To generalize that any separate thing can be a substrate.
D) To reject the idea that possibility is an attribute.

Key Takeaway
The author's careful wording in the example 'like the power of the capable' shows that possibility is not power; it only serves as an analogy under a specific condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you sometimes treat your own potential or capacity as if it were a separate power independent of God? How does the lesson's distinction between possibility and the power of the capable agent challenge you to realign your understanding of your own abilities? What concrete step can you take today to acknowledge that your potential is not your own but is real only by God's power?
?
The author emphasizes precise wording to avoid misrepresenting the truth about God's power. Is there a way you have been speaking about God or His attributes carelessly? How can you be more deliberate with your words to avoid implying limitations or falsehoods about Him? Commit to checking one phrase you use frequently and correcting it based on this lesson.

Essential vs. Preparatory Possibility
Refutation of an objection by distinguishing two types of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument for an existing substrate requires distinguishing essential possibility from preparatory possibility.

Core Idea
Possibility is not all mental; preparatory possibility is a real quality in the substrate.

The commentator tackles a clever objection: if possibility is a mental consideration, it cannot demand an external substrate. The answer draws a critical distinction between two kinds of possibility, showing that one type is indeed real and subsists in the substrate. This passage clarifies the logical foundation for arguments about the existence of a substrate for possibilities that precede originated things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage which states the objection: possibility is a mental consideration, so it does not need a real substrate.
The objector relies on the agreed point that mental considerations do not require external existence.
2
Identify the answer's distinction
Find where the commentator says 'We say: what is meant by this possibility is preparatory possibility' and note the distinction between essential and preparatory.
This is the key move of the passage.
3
Compare the two possibilities
List the characteristics of essential possibility (mental, necessary concomitant, no variation) and preparatory possibility (real, from the category of quality, in the substrate, variable).
Understanding these differences is crucial for grasping the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإمكان أمر اعتباري كما سبق وأنتم معترفون به. والأمور الاعتبارية لا تستدعي محلاً موجوداً، فكيف تستدلون بثبوت الإمكان قبل وجود الحادث على محل موجود يقوم به إمكانه . ( قلنا : المراد بهذا الإمكان ) الذي يستدل به على وجود محله ( هو الإمكان الاستعدادي، وأنه غير الإمكان الذاتي؛ لأن الإمكان الذاتي أمر اعتباري يعقل للشيء عند انتساب ماهيته إلى الوجود وهو لازم لماهية الممكن قائم بها، يستحيل انفكاكه عنها، كما مر ولا يتصور فيه تفاوت بالقوة والضعف، والقرب والبعد أصلاً، بخلاف الإمكان الاستعدادي فإنه أمر موجود من مقولة الكيف، قائم بمحل الشيء الذي ينسب إليه الإمكان لا به، وغيره لازم له وقابل للتفاوت .
Possibility is a mental consideration as previously, and you acknowledge it. Mental considerations do not require an existing substrate, so how do you argue from the existence of possibility before the existence of the originated thing to an existing substrate in which its possibility subsists? (We say: what is meant by this possibility) by which one argues for the existence of its substrate (is preparatory possibility, and it is not essential possibility; because essential possibility is a mental consideration conceived for a thing when its quiddity is related to existence, and it is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of the possible, subsisting in it, impossible to separate from it, as previously, and no variation in strength or weakness, nearness or distance is conceived in it at all, unlike preparatory possibility, for it is an existing thing from the category of quality, subsisting in the substrate of the thing to which possibility is attributed, not in it, and it is other than it, a necessary concomitant of it, and susceptible to variation.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and an answer regarding whether possibility (al-imkan) requires an existing substrate. The objector asserts that since possibility is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari), it does not need an external substrate. The answer distinguishes essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) from preparatory possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi). Essential possibility is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, purely mental, and not variable. Preparatory possibility, by contrast, is a real existence belonging to the category of quality (mawjud min maqulat al-kayf), subsisting in the substrate (mahall) of the thing to which possibility is attributed, and it admits variation. The passage thus clarifies that the possibility used in the argument for a substrate is preparatory, not essential.

Key Terms

الإمكان الاستعدادي
أمر اعتباري
مقولة الكيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All possibility is merely mental and therefore does not require a substrate.
Correction: Only essential possibility is mental; preparatory possibility is a real quality that subsists in a substrate.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between essential possibility and preparatory possibility according to this passage?
A) Essential is mental, preparatory is real and belongs to the category of quality.
B) Essential is real, preparatory is mental.
C) Both are mental considerations.
D) Both are real qualities.

Key Takeaway
To argue for an existing substrate from possibility, one must refer to preparatory possibility, not the purely mental essential possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is one concrete step you will take today to strengthen it, knowing that it requires a real substrate of effort?
?
Reflect: Do you often treat your potential for change as a mere mental possibility—something you say 'I could' but never act on? Where have you deceived yourself into thinking that your essential possibility (e.g., 'I am a Muslim') is enough, while neglecting the preparatory possibility that demands you build the capacity? What is one area where you need to shift from passive acknowledgment to active preparation?
?
The lesson shows that preparatory possibility varies—it can be near or far, strong or weak. Think of one quality you once had but let weaken (e.g., patience in trials, gratitude in ease). What caused the weakening? And what substrate (discipline, environment, supplication) will you re-establish to bring it back? Commit to a specific action before you sleep tonight.

Priority and Posteriority as Accidental to Time
Defending that priority/posteriority are accidental to time via the distinction of intermediaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between 'intermediary in proof' and 'intermediary in subsistence' in the context of priority and posteriority, and how this distinction refutes certain objections.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are accidents of time, not of things directly.

This passage is part of a commentary defending the view that priority and posteriority are primarily accidents of time, not of other entities except through time. The author responds to an objection that conflates two different types of 'intermediary' — one in logical demonstration and one in real existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once and then its literal translation. Notice the technical terms for priority, posteriority, and the two types of intermediary.
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding every detail yet.
2
Identify the main claim
The central claim is that priority and posteriority are accidental to time itself, not to other things except through time. The author is defending this against an objection.
3
Distinguish the two intermediaries
The objection is answered by distinguishing between 'wasita fi al-ithbat' (intermediary in proof) and 'wasita fi al-thubut' (intermediary in subsistence). The critic only shows the first, but the discussion is about the second.
This is the key move in the argument.
4
Understand the example of Zayd's birth
The example illustrates that the objection fails to address the actual point. The commentator explains that the critic's argument only removes an intermediary in demonstration, not in real existence.
5
Recognize the conclusion
The author concludes that the objections raised by the 'intelligent' are thus refuted, and the cessation of questioning is due to linguistic convention, not an essential attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقد يجاب إلخ) أي لا نسلم قولكم فيكون المتقدم في زمان سابق على وجود الحادث. قوله: (بأن القبلية والبعدية إلخ) فالتفريع المذكور ليس باعتبار أن التقدم الزماني مطلقا يقتضي ذلك، بل لكونه في ما عدا الزمان. قوله: (ولغيره بواسطته) أي عارضتان لغير الزمان بواسطته فهو واسطة في العروض. قوله: أن هذه الاستعدادات إلخ فيه بحث لأن هذا الدليل لو تم لم يدل على وجود الزمان الذي هو المراد من مقالة الحكماء، كما سيشير إليه في آخر المقصد؛ إذ النزاع في سبق كل شيء بأمر موجود، وأما السبق بزمان موهوم فالمتكلمون قائلون به. قوله: ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل ولادة زيد فيه بحث لأن ما ذكر لو سلم لدل على أن القبلية والبعدية عرضان أوليان للزمان بمعنى عدم الواسطة في الإثبات والمطلوب عدم الواسطة في الثبوت. قوله: (اتجه أن يقال لماذا؟) أي ما السبب في عروض التقدم لإحداهما على الأخرى. قوله: (وتلك الخلافة متقدمة على هذه) فيكون ما يقارن إحداهما متقدما بالعرض على ما يقارن الأخرى. قوله: (اتجه السؤال أيضا) أي السؤال عن سبب العروض. قوله: (لم يتجه إلخ) أي لم يتجه السؤال عن سبب عروض التقدم لأحدهما على الآخر، وذلك ظاهر. وبما حررنا لك اندفع الاعتراضات التي اتفق عليها الأذكياء من أن التنوير المذكور إنما يدل على انتفاء الواسطة في الإثبات، وهو لا يقتضي انتفاء الواسطة في الثبوت، ولو سلم فاللازم عدم الواسطة في الثبوت دون العروض والمطلوب هو الثاني، كما صرح به الشارح قدس سره، ولو سلم فانقطاع السؤال إنما هو لاعتبار التقدم في مفهوم العام الأول، حيث قلتم: كان في العام الأول لا لكونه وصفا ذاتيا له، ولا يحتاج إلى الأجوبة التي هي أوهن من نسيج العنكبوت عند النقاد.
His statement: (And it may also be answered, etc.) means: we do not concede your claim that the prior is in a time before the existence of the originated thing. His statement: (that priority and posteriority, etc.) – the aforementioned derivation is not because temporal priority absolutely necessitates that, but because it is in what is other than time. His statement: (and to others through its mediation) – i.e., they are accidental to other than time through its mediation, so it is a medium in attribution (wasita fi al-ʿurud). His statement: (that these dispositions, etc.) contains a discussion because if this proof were valid, it would not indicate the existence of the time that is intended by the thesis of the philosophers, as he will indicate at the end of the objective; for the dispute is about the priority of everything by an existing thing, but as for priority by a fictitious time (zaman mawhum), the theologians (mutakallimun) affirm it. His statement: (Do you not see that when it is said: the birth of Zayd, etc.) contains a discussion because what was mentioned, if granted, would only indicate the absence of intermediary in proof (wasita fi al-ithbat), whereas what is sought is the absence of intermediary in subsistence (wasita fi al-thubut). Even if granted, the necessary conclusion would be the absence of intermediary in subsistence, not in attribution (al-ʿurud), and what is sought is the second, as the commentator (sharih) explicitly stated. Even if granted, the cessation of the question is only due to considering the priority in the concept of the 'first year' since you said: it was in the first year, not because it is an essential attribute of it, and it does not need the answers that are weaker than a spider's web in the view of critics.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the view that priority and posteriority are accidental to time and not to other things except through time. The author responds to several objections: 1) The first face (dispositions) does not prove the existence of real time, only a fictitious time (zaman mawhum) which the mutakallimun accept. 2) The example of Zayd's birth shows that the objection conflates absence of intermediary in proof with absence in subsistence; the discussion is about the latter. 3) The cessation of questioning when reaching the 'first year' is due to the linguistic convention, not an essential property. The author concludes that the objections raised by the 'intelligent' are thus refuted.

Misconception: Some objectors think that proving priority and posteriority without an intermediary in proof (i.e., logical demonstration) suffices to prove that they are not accidental to time.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the debate is about intermediary in subsistence (real existence), not intermediary in proof. The two are distinct, and the objection only addresses the former.

Defending Authorial Wording Against Misinterpretation
Objection and answer about the lām of purpose interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a kalam disputation uses nuanced linguistic analysis (the lām of purpose) to defend the author's original intent against a reinterpretation.

Core Idea
Every interpretation must survive the author's actual wording.

This passage shows a classic kalam move: an objector proposes an alternative reading of a phrase, and the responder rejects it by showing it contradicts the author's own words. The objector suggests that the 'lām' in 'li-shay' wāḥid' indicates purpose, meaning unity and multiplicity do not attach for the sake of one thing but rather unity attaches for the sake of multiplicity. The responder counters that the author's phrase 'not the many' (lā l-kathīr) would then imply multiplicity attaches for the sake of the many, which is meaningless. This tight logical critique reveals how kalam relies on precise phrasing.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's claim that the phrase 'they do not attach to one thing' can be interpreted with the lām of purpose, meaning they attach for the sake of multiplicity. Notice how this reinterpretation would avoid the need for lengthy discussions.
The objector thinks this interpretation is simpler and avoids the upcoming objection.
2
Examine the responder's refutation
The responder points to the author's wording 'lā l-kathīr' (not the many). Think about why this wording is incompatible with the objector's interpretation: if the lām signals purpose, then negating attachment 'for one thing' would leave the purpose as multiplicity; but the author specifically negates the many itself.
3
Consider the final nuance
The responder acknowledges one possible nuance: 'multiplicity attaches to the many for the sake of itself.' Reflect on why this exception is allowed—it preserves the logic without contradicting the author's words.
This shows the depth of kalam reasoning: even when rejecting an interpretation, they leave room for subtle refinements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإنهما لم يعرضا لشيء واحد إلخ) فإن قلت : لهذا الكلام محل غير ما ذكره الشارح، لا يحتاج فيه إلى هذه التطويلات المذكورة، ولا يرد الاعتراض الآتي ابتداء، وهو أن اللام لام الأجل، والسبب لاصلة العروض أي لم يعرضا لأجل شيء، واحد بل عروض الوحدة لأجل الكثرة . قلت : يأباه قول المصنف لا الكثير، فإن المفهوم منه على ذلك المحمل أن الكثرة تعرض لأجل الكثير، وإلا للغا هذا النفي، ولا معنى لأن يقال : عروض الكثرة لأجل الكثير. اللهم إلا أن يقال : معناه يعرض الكثرة للكثير لأجل نفسه أي لذاته.
His saying (For they do not attach to one single thing etc.) For if you say: This statement has an interpretation other than what the commentator mentioned; it does not need these lengthy discussions, and the following objection does not arise initially, namely that the lām is the lām of purpose (lām al-ajal), and the cause is related to the attachment, i.e., they do not attach for the sake of one thing, rather the attachment of unity is for the sake of multiplicity. I say: This is rejected by the author's saying 'not the many' (lā l-kathīr), for the understanding from it on that interpretation is that multiplicity attaches for the sake of the many; otherwise this negation would be pointless, and it would have no meaning to say that multiplicity attaches for the sake of the many. Unless it is said that its meaning is that multiplicity attaches to the many for the sake of itself, i.e., its essence.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning the interpretation of the phrase 'they do not attach to one thing'. The objector suggests that the word 'li' (in 'lam yaʿriḍā li-shayʾ wāḥid') might be interpreted as a lām of purpose, meaning that unity and multiplicity do not attach for the sake of one thing, but rather unity attaches for the sake of multiplicity. The responder rejects this, pointing out that the author's wording 'not the many' (lā l-kathīr) contradicts this interpretation. If it were as the objector says, then the negation would apply to the many itself, implying that multiplicity attaches for the sake of the many, which is meaningless. The responder allows one exception: if one interprets it as 'multiplicity attaches to the many for the sake of itself (its essence)', that could be plausible. This shows the careful linguistic and logical analysis typical of kalam.

Key Terms
لام الأجل
السبب
عروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the phrase 'they do not attach to one thing' can be reinterpreted as 'they do not attach for the sake of one thing' (purpose), and that this resolves the difficulty without contradiction.
Correction: The author's own phrase 'not the many' directly contradicts this, because then it would mean 'multiplicity attaches for the sake of the many', which is either tautological or meaningless.

Checkpoint
What interpretation does the objector propose for the phrase 'they do not attach to one thing'?
A) That it means they do not attach at all.
B) That the 'lām' is for purpose, meaning they attach for the sake of multiplicity.
C) That it means they attach to the many.
D) That it means they attach to a single entity.

Key Takeaway
An alternative interpretation of a kalam phrase must be rejected if it conflicts with the author's own explicit wording, as shown here with 'not the many' negating the lām-of-purpose reading.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Resolving the Opposition of Unity and Multiplicity
The commentator resolves an objection on unity and multiplicity through the distinction of detail and totality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction between unity and multiplicity by distinguishing different perspectives of consideration (detail and totality).

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity can coexist when viewed from different aspects.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a critical objection: if unity and multiplicity are opposites, how can they both be attributed to the same thing? He explains that they apply under different considerations—detail and totality. This distinction is a key tool in kalam for handling apparent opposites.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage to grasp the objection: the objector claims that if unity and multiplicity are opposites, they cannot coexist in the same subject. The commentator then sets up his answer.
Notice the phrase 'فإن قلت' which signals the objection.
2
Grasp the answer via detail and totality
The commentator explains that unity occurs to the essence of the many when the attribute of multiplicity is considered, while multiplicity occurs without that consideration. This is further clarified by the terms 'detail' (تفصيل) and 'totality' (إجمال).
This is the core distinction: the many as many vs. the many as a whole.
3
Appreciate the nuance in the commentary
The subsequent commentary clarifies that it's not merely a perceptual difference but a real difference in what the subject is described by. It also rejects an alternative interpretation that would miss this point.
The commentator emphasizes that the difference in cause (سبب) does not prevent coexistence; the subject must differ essentially or by consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : الوحدة تساوق الوجود
أحد المتقابلين للآخر، إنما المحال عروضه لمعروض الآخر، فالعشرية عارضة للجسم مثلاً، والوحدة عارضة للعشرية، فلم يتحدا في الموضوع حتى يكون ذلك مانعاً من تقابلهما . فإن قلت : فعلى هذا لا يصح أن كل ما فله أن كل ما هو موجود وحدة، فإن الكثير موجود ولم يعرض له ،وحدة كما اعترفتم به :قلت المراد من عروض الوحدة للكثرة أنها عارضة لذات الكثير، مع ملاحظة صفة الكثرة بخلاف الكثرة؛ فإنها عارضة لتلك الذات بلا ملاحظة كثرة . وبعبارة أخرى ذات الكثير من حيث التفصيل معروضة للكثرة ، ومن حيث الإجمال معروضة للوحدة، ولا استحالة في عروض المتقابلين
كذلك اجتماع المتقابلين بالعرض لأنه يستلزم اجتماع المتقابلين بالذات، فلا يرد أن الوحدة والكثرة ليستا متقابلين بالذات حتى يمتنع اجتماعهما.
قوله: (المراد إلخ) فمعنى قوله : عرض الوحدة للكثرة أن للكثرة مدخلاً في عروضها، حتى لو لم يلاحظ اتصافه بالكثرة لم تعرضه الوحدة. وما قيل: إن اللام في قوله : لم يعرضا لشيء واحد لام الأجل، فيكون مآل قوله : لا للكثرة لا لأجل ذاته فلا حاجة إلى التطويل الذي ذكره الشارح قدس سره، ولا يرد الاعتراض الآتي فوهم محض؛ لأن اختلاف سبب المتقابلين لا يؤثر في جواز اجتماعهما، بل لا بد في ذلك من اختلاف المحل ذاتاً أو اعتباراً.
قوله : ( ملاحظة صفة (الكثرة زاد لفظ : الصفة إشارة إلى أنه لا بد من ملاحظة الكثرة باعتبار كونها صفة قائمة به، فالموصوف ذات الكثير مع الكثرة لا ذات الكثير في نفسه، ولا مقيداً بالكثرة موصوفاً بها، وإلا لزم اجتماع المتقابلين.
قوله: (من حيث التفصيل بأن لم يعتبر اتصافه بمرتبة واحدة من مراتب الكثرة ومن حيث الإجمال بأن يعتبر اتصافه بها، فماله هو الجواب الأول، لا فرق بينهما إلا بالتعبير، وليس المراد بالتفصيل والإجمال أن يدرك ذلك الكثير مفصلاً، وأن يدرك مجملاً، على قياس ما يقال في الفرق بين الحد والمحدود، حتى يرد أن الاختلاف بالتفصيل والإجمال راجع إلى الاختلاف في الإدراك دون ذات المعروض حتى ينفع في عدم لزوم اجتماع المتقابلين كيف ولو أريد ذلك كان جواباً آخر لا الجواب السابق بعبارة أخرى.

يلاحظ تفصيله، فيكون المآل إلى حيثية الإجمال والتفصيل وأما على الثاني فالأمر ظاهر.
The Fourth Observatory - First Objective: Unity is concomitant with existence.
One of the two opposites for the other; impossibility is its occurrence to the other's substrate. For example, ten-ness is an accident of the body, and unity is an accident of ten-ness, so they do not unite in the subject such that that would prevent their opposition. If you say: On this basis, it is not valid that everything that has [فله] that everything that exists is a unity, for multiplicity exists and unity has not occurred to it, as you have acknowledged. I say: The intended meaning of the occurrence of unity to multiplicity is that it occurs to the essence of the many, with consideration of the attribute of multiplicity, unlike multiplicity; for it occurs to that essence without consideration of multiplicity. In other words, the essence of the many from the perspective of detail is a substrate for multiplicity, and from the perspective of totality is a substrate for unity, and there is no impossibility in the occurrence of opposites to two substrates. Similarly, the conjunction of opposites accidentally is impossible because it necessitates the conjunction of opposites essentially, so it is not objected that unity and multiplicity are not opposites essentially such that their conjunction is impossible.
His statement: (the intended meaning, etc.) So the meaning of his statement: the occurrence of unity to multiplicity is that multiplicity has an entry in its occurrence, such that if its being described by multiplicity were not considered, unity would not occur to it. And what was said: that the lām in his statement 'they did not occur to one thing' is the lām of purpose, so the purport of his statement is 'not for multiplicity, not for its essence', so there is no need for the lengthy discussion that the commentator, may his secret be sanctified, mentioned, and the following objection is not raised—it is pure illusion; because the difference in the cause of the two opposites does not affect the permissibility of their conjunction, rather in that there must be a difference of subject essentially or by consideration.
His statement: ( consideration of the attribute of multiplicity) he added the word 'attribute' indicating that it is necessary to consider multiplicity as being an attribute subsisting in it, so the described is the essence of the many with multiplicity, not the essence of the many in itself, nor restricted by multiplicity described by it; otherwise the conjunction of opposites would be necessary.
His statement: (from the perspective of detail) meaning he does not consider its being described by a single rank of the ranks of multiplicity, and from the perspective of totality meaning he considers its being described by it. So this is the first answer; there is no difference between them except in expression. And what is intended by detail and totality is not that the many is perceived in detail and perceived in summary, analogous to what is said about the difference between definition and defined, such that it might be objected that the difference by detail and totality reverts to the difference in perception, not to the essence of the substrate, so as to benefit in the non-necessity of the conjunction of opposites. How so? And if that were intended, it would be another answer, not the previous answer in other words.
His statement: ( the intended meaning of the occurrence of unity to multiplicity, etc.) It is not hidden that the context of his speech is that the lām is the lām of attachment, so intending this meaning on this estimation is only valid by carrying the speech on lenience. Know that this answer is nearer than the second answer which he indicated by his statement 'and we can say, etc.', and therefore he presented it, although the second answer is more attached to the wording of the text. So according to the first, the meaning of his statement 'even the many' is that the many insofar as it is many, i.e., with consideration of the attribute of multiplicity. And his statement: 'they did not occur to one thing' means from one aspect. And his statement: 'the occurrence of unity to multiplicity' or 'unity occurs to the many with consideration of multiplicity, not to the many whose detail is considered', so the ultimate import is to the aspect of totality and detail. As for the second, the matter is clear.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion within a commentary on unity and multiplicity. The commentator begins by clarifying that unity and multiplicity are not opposites in a way that prevents them from coexisting in the same subject under different considerations. For instance, ten-ness and unity can both be accidents of the same body. An objector then asks: if that is the case, why is it not true that everything that exists is a unity? After all, multiplicity exists and unity is not attributed to it. The commentator responds by distinguishing the way unity and multiplicity are attributed: unity occurs to the essence of the many only when the attribute of multiplicity is considered, whereas multiplicity occurs to that essence without such consideration. This is further explained by the distinction between detailed consideration (the many as many) and total consideration (the many as a unity). Thus there is no impossibility. The commentator then defends this answer against potential misunderstandings. He explains that the wording "occurrence of unity to multiplicity" implies that multiplicity is a factor in that occurrence. He rejects an alternative interpretation that the lām in "لم يعرضا لشيء واحد" is for purpose, arguing that it would make the answer too brief and miss the necessary distinction of the subject. He also comments on the added word "attribute" to emphasize that multiplicity is considered as a quality of the subject. Finally, he clarifies that the terms "detail" and "totality" refer to considering the many as having or not having a specific rank of multiplicity, not to perceptual detail, and that this answer is more accurate than a later alternative.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity and multiplicity cannot be attributed to the same entity because they are opposites.
Correction: The passage shows they can be attributed under different considerations: the same essence is substrate for multiplicity from the perspective of detail and for unity from the perspective of totality.

Checkpoint
How can unity and multiplicity both be attributed to the same essence?
A) Because they are not truly opposites.
B) By considering the essence from different perspectives: detailed for multiplicity, total for unity.
C) Because multiplicity is not real.
D) Unity and multiplicity cannot be attributed to the same essence.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity can coexist because they are attributed to the same essence under different considerations (detail and totality), harmonizing apparent opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you typically see yourself through the lens of detail (your many roles, mistakes, and achievements) or through the lens of totality (your single purpose and identity before Allah)? Which perspective dominates your self-worth, and what would change if you deliberately held both together?
?
Think of a person you struggle to get along with because of their many flaws (multiplicity). Can you also see them as a single human soul created by Allah (unity)? How would viewing them from both perspectives change your treatment of them?
?
The lesson teaches that unity and multiplicity can coexist without contradiction because they relate to different aspects of the same thing. Is there a conflict in your own life—such as between your worldly responsibilities and your spiritual goals—that you have assumed must be resolved by eliminating one? Could it be that they are not in actual contradiction if you view them from different aspects? Commit to identifying one such conflict and reframing it using this principle.

Proof That Unity Is Not Identical to Existence
The commentator proves that unity is not identical to existence using the example of splitting a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that personal unity (al-wahda al-shakhsiyya) is not identical to personal existence (al-wujud al-shakhsi), and why the co-extensiveness of unity and multiplicity does not imply identity.

Core Idea
Unity and existence are not the same; otherwise splitting a body would destroy its existence—an absurd consequence.

The commentator begins by noting that because unity and multiplicity are always found together, some mistakenly thought unity is identical to existence. In this passage, he presents a decisive refutation using a simple physical example: dividing a body. By following the argument, you will see why theologians insist on distinguishing these two concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before analysis.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify key terms
Spot the terms 'personal unity', 'personal existence', 'division', 'destruction', and 'equivalence' in the text.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation assumes identity and draws an absurd consequence. Notice the conditional structure: if A=B, then dividing a body would annihilate it.
The example of the sea shows this is false.
4
Examine the objector's position
The commentator mentions those who allow the possibility based on continuous corporeal form. See how he dismisses them as contending against reason.
This highlights the rational necessity of the distinction.
5
Reflect on the theological importance
Consider why distinguishing unity from existence matters for attributing unity to Allah without equating it with His existence.

The Fourth Observatory - First Objective: Unity is concomitant with existence.
For one thing from two aspects. And we can say: Unity is an accident of multiplicity essentially, and of the many accidentally, and because of that equivalence between them, some thought that it, i.e., unity, is existence itself. Thus personal unity would be the personal existence established for every determinate existent. And it is refuted: that if personal existence were the same as personal unity, then the division that occurs in a single body would be a destruction of that particular body and a creation of two other bodies, because by division the specific unity is annulled, so the specific existence is annulled. And that, i.e., that division is destruction, is false, for splitting the green sea with a mosquito's needle is not a destruction of it and a creation of two other seas, necessarily. And the one who permits that, on the basis that it is mere improbability that does not contradict permissibility, is one who contends against the requirement of his reason, and is not to be addressed or debated. Rather, the one who permitted it did so on the basis that the corporeal form is a continuous identity in its essence, and when separation occurs to it, that [identity] vanishes.
His statement: (and we can say, etc.) So the meaning of our statement 'every many is one' is general: either described by unity essentially or accidentally. He delayed this answer despite its agreement with the apparent wording of the author, because the statement that unity does not occur to the essence of the many, but is only described by it by virtue of the multiplicity subsisting in it, so the meaning of our statement 'the many is one' - its multiplicity - is contrary to what is apparent and found.
His statement: (and because of that, etc.) The source of the assumption is not universal, so it is not objected that this would necessitate the assumption of identity between every two equal things, as was imagined.
His statement: (so it would be, etc.) He added this derivation to direct the mentioned refutation.
His statement: (so it is annulled, etc.) based on the assumption of identity between them.
His statement: (one who contends against the requirement of his reason) For clear reason judges the difference between division and destruction. For he who says 'give me water from this jug' does not intend to destroy that water and create other water.
His statement: (rather he permitted it, etc.) An explanation of the source of the permissibility, completing the speech, and not intended to rebut that the mentioned permissibility is contending, for it is not rebutted by that.
His statement: (for every determinate existent) He restricted it with 'determinate' to exclude universals according to those who affirm their existence.
His statement: 'destruction of it and creation of two other seas' It was said: it is possible to carry the author's speech on that division in that case would be a destruction entirely and a creation of two seas from the absolute absence initially without any remainder of the first, otherwise the remaining, hypothetically, i.e., the hyle, its accidental unity has been annulled because of the form. So on the estimation that personal unity is the same as personal existence, it should also be prior, so his speech would agree with the doctrine of the philosopher, but it is nothing because of what was previously indicated: that the personal unity of the hyle is preserved according to them by the specific unity of the form, not by its personal unity. So the personal unity of the hyle in the split sea is not annulled, although his statement: 'and the one who permits it, etc.' resists it somewhat.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a proof that personal unity (al-wahda al-shakhsiyya) is not identical to personal existence (al-wujud al-shakhsi). The argument begins with the observation that because unity and multiplicity are co-extensive (every unity is associated with a multiplicity and vice versa), some mistakenly thought unity is existence itself. The refutation: if personal existence were the same as personal unity, then splitting a single body would destroy its specific existence and create two new existences—since division abolishes the specific unity. This is absurd, as splitting the sea with a mosquito's needle does not annihilate it and create two seas. The one who still permits this possibility is merely contending against reason and is not worth debating. The commentator then explains why such a person might allow it: they think the corporeal form is a continuous intrinsic identity, and separation destroys it. The commentary further clarifies each part of the argument. It notes that the answer delaying a point is due to its departure from the apparent meaning. It also addresses a potential objection about the universal assumption, and clarifies that the refutation assumes identity between unity and existence. The commentator then discusses the difference between division and destruction, giving the example of asking for water from a jug. He explains the restriction 'determinate' excludes universal natures. Finally, he addresses an alternative interpretation involving hyle (prime matter), arguing that hyle's personal unity is preserved by the specific unity of the form, not its own personal unity, so the argument stands.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that unity is identical to existence because they are co-extensive.
Correction: Unity is not existence; the proof from division shows that if they were identical, splitting a body would annihilate its existence, which is false.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against identifying personal unity with personal existence?
A) Unity and multiplicity are not co-extensive.
B) If they were identical, splitting a body would destroy its existence and create new ones, which is absurd.
C) The commentator says it is contrary to reason.
D) Personal existence is not established.

Key Takeaway
Unity and existence are distinct; the proof from division shows that they cannot be identical, as division would otherwise destroy existence.

?

How does this lesson's distinction between unity and existence challenge you to separate your sense of self from the transient things that accompany you?
?
The author dismisses those who argue against clear reasoning as 'not worth addressing.' Is there an area of your faith where you stubbornly hold onto a comfortable but logically flawed belief simply because it feels right? Will you submit to the truth even if it requires you to make a difficult distinction between what is always true and what is true by identity?

Proof that Unity is Not Identical to Personhood
Al-Jurjani proves unity differs from personhood using division of a simple body and universal concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that unity (wahda) is distinct from personhood (tashakhkhus), and how it addresses objections based on mental existence and divine attributes.

Core Idea
Unity and personhood can separate: dividing a simple body proves they are not identical.

The same proof that showed unity is inseparable from existence also demonstrates that unity is not the same as personhood. Al-Jurjani presents a simple but powerful example: when a body is split, its unity is lost but its identity persists. This lesson unpacks the proof and the subtle objections raised against it, deepening our understanding of these fundamental concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from the main proof to the objections and replies.
The passage contains both the author's text and the commentator's interjections.
2
Understand the simple body example
Focus on the claim: when a simple body is divided, its unity vanishes but its personal identity (being that particular body) remains. Ask yourself why this proves separateness.
If unity were identical to personhood, division would annihilate the body—an absurd result.
3
Consider universal concepts
The second proof: universals like 'humanity' are described as one (as a concept) but not as particular individuals. This shows unity without personhood.
Even in the mind, universals are considered in terms of their essence, not their particular existence.
4
Examine the objection about mental existence
Some object that mental images are particular, so they have personhood. The reply distinguishes between mode of existence and essence.
The reply: mental images are particular in existence but universal in essence/concept.
5
Reflect on the divine attributes objection
A further objection: could personhood be identical to unity in some cases (like God's essence and existence)? The passage hints that concept and reality differ.
The argument is not fully resolved here; it continues on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الدليل بعينه يدل على أن الوحدة ليست عين التشخص، فإن الجسم البسيط الواحد إذا جزئ زالت وحدته دون هويته الشخصية، وإلا كان التفريق إعداما، ويدل عليه أيضاً أن الأمور الكلية موصوفة بالوحدة دون التشخص . قوله : ( وهذا الدليل بعينه يدل إلخ هذه الدلالة على زعم المصنف، وإن كان غير مرضي عند الشارح كما سيشير إليه قوله : بناء على مجرد استبعاد، وقوله : إنما جوزه من جوزه إلخ . قوله ( موصوفة بالوحدة دون (التشخص أي الأمور الكلية من حيث إنها أمور كلية موصوفة بها دونه فاندفع ما يقال : إن الموجود الذهني صورة شخصية في نفس شخصية، فلا محالة يتصف بالتشخص ووجه الاندفاع أنها من حيث الوجود في الذهن وإن كانت جزئية ومتشخصة، لكن من حيث ذاتها ومفهومها كلية. وبهذا الاعتبار تتصف بالوحدة دون التشخص. وقد يناقش في الدلالة المذكورة بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون التشخص فيما وجد عيناً للوحدة ولا يلزم منه أن يكون كلما وجد أحدهما وجد الآخر. فاعتبر الوجود فإنه عين ذات الباري تعالى مع أنا نتصف بالوجود لا بذات الباري تعالى . نعم مفهوم هذا مغاير لمفهوم ذاك، أو
This same proof indicates that unity is not identical to personhood. For one simple body, when it is divided, its unity ceases while its personal identity remains; otherwise division would be annihilation. It also indicates that universal things are described by unity without personhood. He says: (And this same proof indicates...) [commentary] This indication is according to the claim of the author, although it is not satisfactory to the commentator as he will indicate by his statement: 'based on mere improbability' and his statement: 'only who permits it permits it' etc. He says: (described by unity without personhood) meaning the universal things insofar as they are universal things are described by it without that; thus is repelled what is said: that mental existence is a particular image in a particular soul, so inevitably it is described by personhood. And the repulsion is that they, insofar as existence in the mind, even if particular and particularized, yet insofar as their essence and concept are universal. By this consideration they are described by unity without personhood. And there may be a discussion about the mentioned indication: why may it not be permissible that personhood in what exists is identical to unity and it does not entail that whenever one of them exists the other exists? Consider existence: it is identical to the essence of God Almighty while we are described by existence not by God's essence. Yes, the concept of this is different from the concept of that, or [continuation next page].
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a commentary. It begins by stating that the same proof used earlier (for the inseparability of unity from existence) also shows that unity is not the same as personhood (tashakhkhus). The proof is that when a simple body is divided, its unity is lost but its personal identity (its being that particular body) remains. If unity were the same as personhood, division would annihilate the body entirely—an absurd conclusion. Additionally, universal concepts (like 'humanity') are described as one (being universal) but not personified (they have no particular identity). The commentator then explains the author's statement, noting that some object that mental images are particular and thus have personhood. The response is that universals in the mind are considered in terms of their essence (which is universal), not their particular mode of existence. A further objection is raised: why can't personhood be identical to unity in some cases, even if not always? The example of God's existence is cited, but the point is that concept and reality differ. The passage ends mid-argument on the page break.

وحدة
تشخص
جسم بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many students think that unity and personhood are the same thing, so whatever is one is automatically a particular individual.
Correction: This passage shows that unity can exist without personhood, as in the case of universal concepts. Also, a simple body can lose its unity (when divided) while retaining its personal identity.

Checkpoint
What happens to the unity and personal identity of a simple body when it is divided?
A) Both unity and personal identity remain.
B) Unity ceases but personal identity remains.
C) Personal identity ceases but unity remains.
D) Both are lost, causing annihilation.

Key Takeaway
Unity and personhood are distinct: a divided body loses unity but keeps its identity, and universal concepts have unity without being particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you lose something that once unified your life—like a job, a relationship, or a role—do you feel your personal identity is threatened? This lesson shows that unity can be lost without personhood vanishing. What part of your identity is truly inseparable from who you are? Are you holding onto something as if it defines you, when in fact your core personhood remains even after it's gone? Will you trust that today and let go of what must pass?
?
You claim to be a believer in the One (الواحد). But your life is filled with many attachments—family, wealth, status—that give you a sense of 'one-ness' of purpose. If those were divided and taken away, would your personhood as a servant of Allah remain? This lesson forces you to ask: is your tawheed theoretical or actual? If your unity of focus shatters, does your identity as a Muslim shatter with it? Examine yourself now.
?
The universal concept of 'humanity' has unity but no personhood. But you are not a universal; you are a particular person with a unique soul. Your personhood is given by Allah and cannot be taken away. Yet do you live as if your real identity is your gender, nationality, or personality? These are like 'unity'—they come and go. But your personhood before Allah is permanent. Are you investing in what lasts, or in what can be divided?
