Existence Accompanies Multiplicity, Unity Does Not
Al-Jurjani states the core distinction between existence and unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence can coexist with multiplicity while unity cannot, and how this distinction is foundational for kalam reasoning about division.

Core Idea
Existence is shared; unity is not. Many things exist together but are not one.

Al-Jurjani opens with a sharp distinction between existence and unity. While existence can accompany many things at once, unity cannot. This seems obvious, but it has deep implications: if existence and unity were identical, division would annihilate things—yet we see water remains water after splitting. This lesson unpacks the core argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the compressed style.
The passage starts with 'وأيضاً فالوجود يجامع الكثرة...'.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states two things: existence accompanies multiplicity, and unity does not accompany multiplicity. Ask yourself: what does 'accompany' mean here?
3
Understand 'the many as many'
Al-Jurjani explains that the many, considered as many, are existent but not one. This means multiplicity and unity are mutually exclusive for the same subject at the same time.
The phrase 'ليس من هذه الحيثية بواحد' is key.
4
Reflect on the implication
If existence were tied to unity, then any division would annihilate the original entity. But water remains water after splitting—so existence is not the same as unity.
This connects to the commentary about the deniers of hyle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فالوجود يجامع الكثرة، والوحدة لا تجامعها. ومعنى ذلك ما فصله بقوله : ( فالكثير من حيث هو كثير) أي من حيث تلاحظ كثرته وتفصيله ) موجود ليس من هذه الحيثية ) بواحد . قوله : ( والموجود في الحالتين إلخ) كيلا يكون التفريق إعداماً بالكلية، كما لزم ذلك للنافين للهيولى القائلين بأن الجسم حقيقية هو الاتصال الجوهري فقط ولا يخفى على المنصف أن التفريق كما أنه ليس إعداماً بالكلية ليس إعداماً باعتبار بعض الأجزاء، فإن العقل يحكم بأن الماء بعد التفريق هو الماء السابق، إلا أنه زال منه الوحدة، وعرض له الكثرة . قوله دون هويته الشخصية بناء على أن الوحدة ليست من المشخصات، وإذا قال الحكماد ببقاء هيولى العناصر بالتشخص مع تكثرها باعتبار الأجسام العنصرية . قوله : ( أن الأمور لكلية أي المفهومات الموصوفة بالكلية في أنفسها موصوفة بالوحدة دون التشخص إذ تشخصها بعد عروض المشخصات. قوله : ( وأيضاً فالوجود) عطف على قوله : يبطله بتقدير الفعل، والفاء زائدة. قوله : ( ومعنى ذلك) إنما قال : ذلك لأن ظاهر قوله : فالوجود إلخ يقتضي تحقق الوجود بدون الوحدة وعدم المساوقة بينهما . قوله : (من حيث تلاحظ كثرته أي يلاحظ كونها صفة خارجة عنه قائمة به، فلا ينافي ما مر من قوله : عارضة لذات الكثير مع ملاحظة صفة الكثرة، فإن المراد به كما سبق أنها عارضة له إذا أخذ الكثير مع صفة الكثرة . قوله : ( وليس من هذه الحيثية أي من حيث كونه موصوفاً بالكثرة بواحد، وإلا لزم اجتماع
And also, existence accompanies multiplicity, while unity does not accompany it. The meaning of that is detailed by his statement: (the many insofar as it is many) meaning insofar as its multiplicity and detail are observed) existent, not from this consideration, as one. His statement: (and the existent in both states etc.) lest division be complete annihilation, as was necessary for the deniers of hyle who say that the reality of the body is substantial continuity only. And it is not hidden from the fair-minded that division, as it is not complete annihilation, is not annihilation in respect of some parts. For reason judges that water after division is the previous water, except that unity has vanished from it and multiplicity has occurred to it. His statement: 'without its personal identity' based on that unity is not among the particularizers, and if al-Hukmad says that the hyle of elements remains with personhood despite its multiplicity in respect of elemental bodies. His statement: (that universals) meaning concepts described by universality in themselves are described by unity without personhood since their personhood is after the occurrence of particularizers. His statement: (and also existence) is coordinate to his statement: 'it refutes' with the verb implied, and the fa is redundant. His statement: (and the meaning of that) he only said that because the apparent sense of his statement 'existence etc.' requires the realization of existence without unity and the non-equivalence between them. His statement: (from the consideration that its multiplicity is observed) meaning that it is observed as an attribute external to it subsisting in it, so it does not contradict what passed of his statement: 'it is accidental to the essence of the many with observation of the attribute of multiplicity'. For the intended meaning, as previously, is that it is accidental to it when the many is taken with the attribute of multiplicity. His statement: (and not from this consideration) meaning from the consideration that it is described by multiplicity as one, otherwise there would occur
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and commentary on the relationship between existence, unity, and multiplicity. The main argument is that existence can accompany multiplicity (many things can exist together) whereas unity does not; many things exist but are not one. The commentator explains that 'the many as many' are existent but not one, because if they were one, they wouldn't be many. He then discusses the implication for division: if unity were identical to existence, dividing an object would annihilate it, but in reality the object (like water) remains the same after division, though its unity is lost and multiplicity appears. The commentary also touches on personhood (tashakhkhus), stating that unity is not a particularizer, and that universal concepts are described by unity but not personhood. Further grammatical and logical clarifications are given about the text's structure and the meaning of 'the many as many' being externally accidental to the essence of the many. The passage breaks off discussing the conjunction and the condition of describing the many with multiplicity.

كثرة
وحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many students think existence and unity are inseparable—that if something exists, it must be one.
Correction: The passage shows that existence can be separate from unity; many things exist while not being one.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what is the relation between existence and multiplicity?
A) Existence cannot accompany multiplicity.
B) Existence accompanies multiplicity.
C) Existence is identical to multiplicity.
D) Existence and multiplicity are mutually exclusive.

Key Takeaway
Existence can be shared by many things, but unity cannot; this distinction is crucial for understanding how objects persist through change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is filled with many roles, desires, and thoughts—all existing. But are you one person across all of them, or do you feel fragmented? What would it take for your multiplicity to be unified under Allah's command?
?
The passage says the many exist but are not one. When you look at your own soul, do you see a single, unified self or a collection of conflicting inclinations? How does the oneness of Allah call you to integrate your scattered self into submission?

Division, Persistence, and the Nature of Unity
Al-Jurjani applies the existence-unity distinction to explain division and personhood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between existence and unity explains why division does not destroy identity, and why unity is not a particularizer.

Core Idea
Division removes unity but not existence; the same entity persists though its unity is lost.

Building on the previous principle, Al-Jurjani now tackles a critical objection: if unity were identical to existence, dividing an object would annihilate it. But experience shows otherwise—water remains water when poured into separate cups. This lesson explores the commentary on division, the role of hyle, and the nature of unity as a non-particularizing attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on division
Read from 'قوله : ( والموجود في الحالتين إلخ)' through the water example. See how the author argues that division is not complete annihilation.
The water example is intuitive: after division, the water is the same in kind.
2
Understand the denial of hyle
The deniers of hyle (primordial matter) claim that a body's reality is only substantial continuity. For them, division would destroy the body entirely. Al-Jurjani refutes this by showing that the divided object retains its identity.
Substantial continuity is the idea that a body is nothing but continuous connection of parts.
3
Analyze the nature of unity
The text states that unity is not a particularizer (mushakhkhis). This means unity does not make an individual distinct; personhood comes from other attributes. Universal concepts have unity without being particular individuals.
The commentary discusses 'personal identity' (huwiyya shakhsiyya) and its relation to unity.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
The passage consistently separates existence, unity, and personhood. Existence can persist through division, unity is lost, and personhood is unchanged because it depends on other factors.
This supports the Ash'ari view that objects have a persisting core beyond mere continuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فالوجود يجامع الكثرة، والوحدة لا تجامعها. ومعنى ذلك ما فصله بقوله : ( فالكثير من حيث هو كثير) أي من حيث تلاحظ كثرته وتفصيله ) موجود ليس من هذه الحيثية ) بواحد . قوله : ( والموجود في الحالتين إلخ) كيلا يكون التفريق إعداماً بالكلية، كما لزم ذلك للنافين للهيولى القائلين بأن الجسم حقيقية هو الاتصال الجوهري فقط ولا يخفى على المنصف أن التفريق كما أنه ليس إعداماً بالكلية ليس إعداماً باعتبار بعض الأجزاء، فإن العقل يحكم بأن الماء بعد التفريق هو الماء السابق، إلا أنه زال منه الوحدة، وعرض له الكثرة . قوله دون هويته الشخصية بناء على أن الوحدة ليست من المشخصات، وإذا قال الحكماد ببقاء هيولى العناصر بالتشخص مع تكثرها باعتبار الأجسام العنصرية . قوله : ( أن الأمور لكلية أي المفهومات الموصوفة بالكلية في أنفسها موصوفة بالوحدة دون التشخص إذ تشخصها بعد عروض المشخصات. قوله : ( وأيضاً فالوجود) عطف على قوله : يبطله بتقدير الفعل، والفاء زائدة. قوله : ( ومعنى ذلك) إنما قال : ذلك لأن ظاهر قوله : فالوجود إلخ يقتضي تحقق الوجود بدون الوحدة وعدم المساوقة بينهما . قوله : (من حيث تلاحظ كثرته أي يلاحظ كونها صفة خارجة عنه قائمة به، فلا ينافي ما مر من قوله : عارضة لذات الكثير مع ملاحظة صفة الكثرة، فإن المراد به كما سبق أنها عارضة له إذا أخذ الكثير مع صفة الكثرة . قوله : ( وليس من هذه الحيثية أي من حيث كونه موصوفاً بالكثرة بواحد، وإلا لزم اجتماع

According to the passage, what happens when water is divided?
A) It becomes a completely different substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that water after division is still the same water. Is there any part of your identity that you think would be destroyed if it were 'divided' or changed? What does this teach you about relying on unity (consistency, stability) as a basis for your sense of self?
?
This passage distinguishes unity from personhood. Have you ever confused being 'whole' or 'consistent' with your actual personhood? How might clinging to unity prevent you from accepting change that doesn't affect your true self?
?
The deniers of hyle thought division would be annihilation. What areas of your life do you fear 'division' (change, loss, fragmentation) as if it would annihilate you? How does this lesson challenge that fear?

Unity and Multiplicity Are Additional to Quiddity
Al-Jurjani argues that unity and multiplicity are additional to quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both unity and multiplicity are considered additional to quiddity, based on the reasoning that quiddity in itself is receptive to both and rejects them when added.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are not what things are; they are added descriptions.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani establishes a key distinction in Islamic metaphysics: the attributes of unity and multiplicity are not identical to the essence of a thing (quiddity). Rather, they are additional attributes that the quiddity can receive or reject. This lesson follows his argument step by step, showing that quiddity's receptivity to the opposite of each attribute proves the attribute's distinctness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for unity's addition
Read the Arabic passage and translation for the first argument that unity is additional to quiddity.
Notice the phrase 'on analogy with what passed in the discussion of existence'.
2
Understand the analogy with existence
Recall the earlier argument about existence being additional to quiddity; this argument follows the same pattern.
3
Read the argument for multiplicity's addition
Read the second part of the passage where multiplicity is also argued to be additional.
Note the parallel structure with the unity argument.
4
Compare the two arguments
Notice how both use the same method: quiddity is receptive to one attribute but rejects the other, proving the attribute is not intrinsic.
This method is a common tool in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي مغايرة للماهية زائدة عليها؛ لأنها من حيث هي تقبل الكثرة، وإذا أخذت مع الوحدة تأباها، فلا تكون الوحدة نفسها ولا جزاها على قياس ما مر في بحث الوجود. والكثرة أيضاً غير الماهية، بل زائدة عليها لمثل ذلك؛ فإن الماهية كالإنسانية مثلاً من حيث هي قابلة للوحدة إذا أخذت مع الكثرة مفصلة كانت آبية عنها. والكثرة غير الوجود، وإلا يلزم كون الجمع إعداماً فإنه إذا جمع أجسام كميات في ظروف متعددة، وجعلت في ظرف واحد، فقد زالت كثرتها التي هي وجودها فرضاً، فيلزم إعدام تلك الأجسام وإيجاد جسم واحد، وأنه باطل والمجوز مكابر. وإنما لم يتعرض لتعريف الوحدة والكثرة، لأنهما بديهيتان بمثل ما مر في الوجود، فإن تصور الوحدة جزء من تصور وحدتي المتصورة بالضرورة، وأيضاً فإن كل أحد يعلم أنه واحد بلا كسب منه، وكان في التصريح بمساوقة الوحدة للوجود نوع إشعار ببداهتها على قياس بداهته، وقس حال الكثرة على حال الوحدة
And it (unity) is different from quiddity and additional to it; because quiddity insofar as it is receptive to multiplicity, when taken with unity, it repels it, so unity is neither the same as quiddity nor a part of it, analogously to what passed in the discussion of existence. And multiplicity is also other than quiddity, rather additional to it for the same reason; for quiddity, such as humanity for example, insofar as it is receptive to unity, when taken with detailed multiplicity, is averse to it. And multiplicity is other than existence; otherwise it would necessitate that gathering is annihilation, because if bodies (quantities) in multiple containers are gathered and placed in one container, their multiplicity which is supposed to be their existence has vanished, necessitating the annihilation of those bodies and the creation of a single body—which is false, and the one who permits it is stubborn. And he did not define unity and multiplicity because they are self-evident, like what passed regarding existence, for the conception of unity is part of the conception of my unity necessarily, and also every person knows that he is one without any acquisition, and in the explicit statement of the equivalence of unity to existence there is a kind of indication of its self-evidence analogously to its self-evidence; and analogize the case of multiplicity to the case of unity.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents three interconnected arguments and a concluding statement about self-evidence. First, it establishes that unity is additional to quiddity (mahiyya): quiddity in itself is receptive to multiplicity, but when unity is added, quiddity rejects multiplicity, proving unity is distinct. Second, multiplicity is likewise additional to quiddity for the same reason—quiddity when taken with detailed multiplicity opposes it. Third, multiplicity is not identical to existence; if it were, gathering multiple objects together (thereby reducing their multiplicity) would be annihilation of those objects, which is absurd; the objects remain, but their multiplicity changes. Finally, the author explains why unity and multiplicity are not defined: they are self-evident (badihiyya), like existence. The conception of unity is necessarily part of one's concept of one's own unity; everyone knows they are one without effort. The explicit statement of the equivalence of unity to existence further suggests this self-evidence. The same applies to multiplicity.

Key Terms
وحدة
كثرة
ماهية
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity and multiplicity are part of quiddity or identical to it.
Correction: They are additional attributes distinct from quiddity, as shown by quiddity's resistance when combined with the opposite.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Jurjani argue that unity is not the same as quiddity?
A) Because quiddity is receptive to multiplicity and repels unity.
B) Because unity is self-evident.
C) Because quiddity is eternal.
D) Because multiplicity is existence.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are additional attributes, not the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often define yourself by your roles or qualities (e.g., 'I am a student', 'I am a failure'). The lesson says unity and multiplicity are not what you are, but added descriptions. Can you see yourself as something simpler, a quiddity that is receptive to many descriptions yet not defined by any? What would it mean to live today without attaching your worth to being 'one' or 'many'? Commit to one moment where you recognize an attribute as additional, not essential.
?
The passage shows that quiddity resists unity when taken with multiplicity. Have you ever felt resistance when others try to put you into a single category? How does that resistance hint that your essence is beyond any label? Reflect on a label you've accepted as part of your identity—is it really you, or is it added? Ask Allah to help you see yourself as He sees you: a creation with a quiddity that is pure and receptive.
?
If unity and multiplicity are additional, then your existence itself is also additional (as earlier discussed). Do you live as if your existence is a gift added to your essence, or do you take it for granted? Starting tonight, recite الحمد لله on each breath and mean it as an acknowledgment that existence is extra.

Multiplicity Is Not Existence; Self-Evidence of Unity and Multiplicity
Al-Jurjani proves multiplicity is not existence and asserts the self-evidence of unity and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that multiplicity is not identical to existence and why unity and multiplicity are considered self-evident.

Core Idea
Multiplicity is not existence; unity and multiplicity are known without proof.

Al-Jurjani continues by addressing a potential confusion: some might think multiplicity is the same as existence. He provides a reductio ad absurdum argument. Then he explains that unity and multiplicity are self-evident, needing no definition, just like existence. This lesson unpacks both points.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument against multiplicity being existence
Read the first part of the passage where Al-Jurjani argues that multiplicity is other than existence.
Pay attention to the logical consequence: gathering would be annihilation.
2
Understand the reductio example
The example of taking scattered objects and putting them together illustrates the absurdity of identifying multiplicity with existence.
This is a typical kalam use of reductio ad absurdum (talazum).
3
Read the claim of self-evidence
Read the second part where the author states that unity and multiplicity are not defined because they are self-evident.
Note the evidence: the concept of unity is part of one's own awareness of being one.
4
Reflect on the analogy with existence
Compare the self-evidence of unity to that of existence, as mentioned in the text.
This connects to earlier discussions about existence being self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي مغايرة للماهية زائدة عليها؛ لأنها من حيث هي تقبل الكثرة، وإذا أخذت مع الوحدة تأباها، فلا تكون الوحدة نفسها ولا جزاها على قياس ما مر في بحث الوجود. والكثرة أيضاً غير الماهية، بل زائدة عليها لمثل ذلك؛ فإن الماهية كالإنسانية مثلاً من حيث هي قابلة للوحدة إذا أخذت مع الكثرة مفصلة كانت آبية عنها. والكثرة غير الوجود، وإلا يلزم كون الجمع إعداماً فإنه إذا جمع أجسام كميات في ظروف متعددة، وجعلت في ظرف واحد، فقد زالت كثرتها التي هي وجودها فرضاً، فيلزم إعدام تلك الأجسام وإيجاد جسم واحد، وأنه باطل والمجوز مكابر. وإنما لم يتعرض لتعريف الوحدة والكثرة، لأنهما بديهيتان بمثل ما مر في الوجود، فإن تصور الوحدة جزء من تصور وحدتي المتصورة بالضرورة، وأيضاً فإن كل أحد يعلم أنه واحد بلا كسب منه، وكان في التصريح بمساوقة الوحدة للوجود نوع إشعار ببداهتها على قياس بداهته، وقس حال الكثرة على حال الوحدة
And it (unity) is different from quiddity and additional to it; because quiddity insofar as it is receptive to multiplicity, when taken with unity, it repels it, so unity is neither the same as quiddity nor a part of it, analogously to what passed in the discussion of existence. And multiplicity is also other than quiddity, rather additional to it for the same reason; for quiddity, such as humanity for example, insofar as it is receptive to unity, when taken with detailed multiplicity, is averse to it. And multiplicity is other than existence; otherwise it would necessitate that gathering is annihilation, because if bodies (quantities) in multiple containers are gathered and placed in one container, their multiplicity which is supposed to be their existence has vanished, necessitating the annihilation of those bodies and the creation of a single body—which is false, and the one who permits it is stubborn. And he did not define unity and multiplicity because they are self-evident, like what passed regarding existence, for the conception of unity is part of the conception of my unity necessarily, and also every person knows that he is one without any acquisition, and in the explicit statement of the equivalence of unity to existence there is a kind of indication of its self-evidence analogously to its self-evidence; and analogize the case of multiplicity to the case of unity.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents three interconnected arguments and a concluding statement about self-evidence. First, it establishes that unity is additional to quiddity (mahiyya): quiddity in itself is receptive to multiplicity, but when unity is added, quiddity rejects multiplicity, proving unity is distinct. Second, multiplicity is likewise additional to quiddity for the same reason—quiddity when taken with detailed multiplicity opposes it. Third, multiplicity is not identical to existence; if it were, gathering multiple objects together (thereby reducing their multiplicity) would be annihilation of those objects, which is absurd; the objects remain, but their multiplicity changes. Finally, the author explains why unity and multiplicity are not defined: they are self-evident (badihiyya), like existence. The conception of unity is necessarily part of one's concept of one's own unity; everyone knows they are one without effort. The explicit statement of the equivalence of unity to existence further suggests this self-evidence. The same applies to multiplicity.

كثرة
وجود
إعدام
بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiplicity is the same as existence.
Correction: Multiplicity is distinct; existence remains even when multiplicity changes.

Checkpoint

A) Gathering objects would create new existence.
B) Gathering objects would annihilate them.
C) Multiplicity would be eternal.
D) Unity would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity is not existence, and unity and multiplicity are self-evident concepts.

?
You read that everyone knows they are one without acquisition. But do you act as if you are one—unified in your faith, your actions, your heart? Where do you feel split? And can you live with that split one more day?

Unity and Multiplicity in Cognition
al-Iji states that unity is more known to the intellect and multiplicity to the imagination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji argues for the distinct epistemological roles of intellect and imagination regarding unity and multiplicity.

Core Idea
Unity is the intellect's home, multiplicity is the imagination's starting point.

al-Iji begins by making a striking epistemological claim: unity and multiplicity are not equally known to all faculties. The intellect naturally grasps unity, while the imagination first encounters multiplicity. He then explains the cognitive process that leads from sensory particulars to universal unity. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how our minds relate to the fundamental categories of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: al-waḥda (unity) and al-kathra (multiplicity).
2
Identify the two faculties
Find the words for 'intellect' (al-ʿaql) and 'imagination' (al-khayāl). What does al-Iji say is more known to each? This is the core claim.
The passage says unity is more known to the intellect, multiplicity to the imagination.
3
Trace the abstraction process
Follow the soul's journey: first perceives particulars (juzʾiyyāt), then abstracts a single universal form (ṣūra kulliyya) in the intellect. Notice that unity is described as an accident ('āriḍ) of what is obtained.
This process explains why multiplicity is prior in sensory experience but unity is prior in intellectual understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : الوحدة تساوق الوجود أعرف عند العقل من الكثرة، والكثرة أعرف عند الخيال من الوحدة، فإن النفس تدرك أولاً جزئيات ترتسم صورها في آلاتها، ثم تنتزع من تلك الجزئيات المتكثرة صورة كلية واحدة ترتسم في العقل أي في ذات النفس، فالوحدة عارضة لما هو حاصل
The fourth observatory - first aim: Unity coexists with existence. It is more known to the intellect than multiplicity, and multiplicity is more known to the imagination than unity. For the soul first perceives particulars whose forms are imprinted in its instruments, then it abstracts from those multiplied particulars a single universal form that is imprinted in the intellect, i.e., in the soul's essence. Thus unity is an accident of what is obtained.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanatory statement from the main text (matn) of al-Iji's Al-Mawaqif. It begins by asserting the epistemological priority of unity and multiplicity relative to different faculties: unity is more known (a'raf) to the intellect, while multiplicity is more known to the imagination. The reasoning follows: the soul first perceives particular things through the physical senses (instruments), then abstracts from those multiple particulars a single universal form that is imprinted in the intellect. Therefore, unity is an accident ('arid) that attaches to what is already present in the mind. This establishes the foundational claim that multiplicity is prior in sensory experience, but unity is prior in intellectual understanding.

Refuting Two Proofs of Multiplicity
Presents and refutes two arguments for an infinite regress of unities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments that multiplicity would lead to infinite regress and how they are refuted using the distinctions between essential and accidental unity and between dependence on unity versus existence.

Core Idea
Multiplicity does not require an infinite regress because unity can be accidental and joining depends on existence, not unity.

This passage tackles a key objection against the reality of multiplicity: if multiplicity is real, it seems to require an infinite series of unities. The commentator presents two versions of this objection and then provides their refutation, drawing on concepts from earlier discussions. Understanding these refutations clarifies how multiple things can exist without logical paradox.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first proof and its refutation
Read the argument that the joining of multiplicity depends on a prior unity, leading to either circularity or infinite regress. Then study the answer: unities can share in unity accidentally, so each is distinct by itself, stopping the regress.
This refutation relies on the concept of accidental (ʿaraḍī) unity.
2
Understand the second proof and its refutation
Read the argument that multiplicity would need another multiplicity because it shares commonality and has particularity. Then study the answer: joining depends on existence, not on unity, and dependence on one correlative does not imply dependence on the other.
This refutation parallels the answer regarding individuation.
3
Recognize the underlying principle
Notice that both refutations rely on distinguishing what is essential from what is accidental, and on clarifying what dependence actually requires. This helps avoid fallacious regress arguments.

Source Text

قوله : ( لتوقف انضمامها إلخ) بناء على أن الانضمام حينئذ يكون خارجياً، وهو موقوف على وجود المنضم إليه . والموجود إما واحد أو كثير ، ويمتنع انضمامها إلى الكثير من حيث هو كثير، فيكون انضمامها إلى الوحدة، فالوحدة السابقة إما عين اللاحقة فيلزم الدور، أو غيرها فيلزم التسلسل
قوله: (ويمكن إجراء (الدليلين أما إجراء الثاني فظاهر وأما اجراء الأول فبأن يقال لو وجدت الكثرة لشاركت الكثرات في الكثرة، وامتازت بخصوصية فللكثرة كثرة أخرى، لكونها مركبة مما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز.
قوله : ( وقد تقدم جوابهما في بحث التعين لكن جواب الأول مثل ما تقدم في التعين، وهو أن يقال : اشتراك الوحدات في الوحدة يجوز أن يكون اشتراكاً في عرضي، وحينئذ يكون كل واحدة منها ممتازة بنفسها، فلا يكون للوحدة وحدة أخرى، وأما جواب الثاني فقريب مما تقدم وهو أن يقال : لا نسلم توقف الانضمام على وحدة الماهية إنما اللازم توقفه على وجودها، ولا يلزم من التوقف على أحد المتساوقين التوقف على الآخر، وهو شريك للجواب المتقدم في التعين، أعني منع كون انضمام التعين موقوفاً على تعين الماهية، بل على امتيازها عن سائر الماهيات في كون كل منهما منعاً لتوقف الانضمام وإن تخالفا في السند .
Existing for the dependence of its joining to the quiddity on it being one, because it is impossible for unity to occur to that which is described by multiplicity, and if unity is privative, then multiplicity composed of it is also so, and also the two proofs can be applied to it. And their answer has preceded. And here a proof indicates specifically for unity.
His saying: (for the dependence of its joining etc.) based on the fact that the joining then is external, and it depends on the existence of that to which it is joined. And the existent is either one or many, and it is impossible for it to be joined to the many insofar as it is many, so its joining is to unity. So the prior unity is either identical to the subsequent, leading to circularity, or other than it, leading to infinite regress.
His saying: (and the two proofs can be applied) as for applying the second it is obvious, and as for applying the first, it is by saying: if multiplicity existed, it would share with other multiplicities in multiplicity, and be distinguished by a particularity, so multiplicity would have another multiplicity, because it is composed of what commonality and what distinction.
His saying: (and their answer has preceded in the discussion of individuation) but the answer to the first is like what preceded in individuation, which is to say: the sharing of units in unity can be a sharing in an accident, and then each one of them is distinguished by itself, so unity does not have another unity. As for the answer to the second, it is close to what preceded, which is to say: we do not grant the dependence of joining on the unity of the quiddity; rather, the necessary dependence is on its existence, and it does not follow from dependence on one of two mutually dependent things the dependence on the other. And it is a partner to the preceding answer in individuation, namely, preventing the joining of individuation from being dependent on the individuation of the quiddity, but rather on its distinction from other quiddities, in that each of them prevents the dependence of joining, even if they differ in ground.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two arguments that multiplicity leads to infinite regress. First, the argument from dependence of joining: if multiplicity is real, its joining to a quiddity depends on unity, leading to a regress of unities. The answer distinguishes essential unity from accidental unity: units can share in unity accidentally, so each is distinct by itself, avoiding regress. Second, the argument from composition: multiplicity would share in multiplicity with other multiplicities and be distinguished by a particularity, thus requiring another multiplicity. The answer denies that joining depends on quiddity's unity, rather on its existence, and dependence on one correlative does not entail dependence on the other. This is similar to the answer regarding individuation.

الكثرة (al-kathra, multiplicity)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul, infinite regress)
الدور (al-dawr, circularity)
العرضي (al-ʿaraḍī, accidental)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proofs of multiplicity leading to infinite regress are unanswerable.
Correction: The refutations show that the regress is avoided by recognizing that unity can be accidental and that joining depends on existence, not on unity.

Key Takeaway

Answering a Generalization Objection in Kalam
Objection that the proof for unity applies to multiplicity, and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a kalam objection that attempts to generalize a proof is answered by invoking an earlier established distinction.

Core Idea
An objection that seems universal is neutralized by a prior exception.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection-and-answer structure common in Islamic theology. The objector tries to show that the proof for unity being existential applies just as well to multiplicity, thereby undermining its specificity. The commentator's answer, however, is concise: it refers back to what was already established in the discussion of individuation. This move teaches us how earlier doctrinal distinctions are used to defuse later objections without repeating the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the objector's statement: 'this proof also applies to multiplicity'. Notice how the objector tries to apply the same reasoning to a different concept.
The objection has several branches.
2
Trace the logical branches
Identify the two main scenarios: multiplicity as privative leads to unity being existential; unity as non-existence leads to two sub-possibilities. Understand how each branch is supposed to force a contradiction.
This is a typical case-analysis argument.
3
Examine the answer
Observe that the answer is simply 'what preceded there exactly'. This is a reference to a previous discussion on individuation where a distinction about privative terms was made.
The answer is not a new argument but a reminder.
4
Reflect on the method
Appreciate how kalam arguments often rely on previously established principles to answer objections without starting from scratch. This shows the interconnectedness of theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويخص الوحدة إلخ) إن قلت هذا الدليل يعم الكثرة أيضاً؛ إذ يقال: لو كانت الكثرة عدمية لكان عدم الوحدة، فالوحدة إما وجودية والكثرة ليست إلا مجموع الوحدات كونها وجودية، هو (أنه لو كانت الوحدة عدماً لكان عدم الكثرة) التي تقابلها؛ لامتناع أن تكون عدماً مطلقاً، أو عدماً لشيء آخر لا تقابله، وإذا كانت عدماً للكثرة ( فالكثرة إما وجودية والوحدة جزؤها، فتكون ( الوحدة أيضاً (موجودة ) على تقدير كونها معدومة وهذا خلف مع أنه المطلوب. وإما ،عدمية، فتكون الوحدة عدماً للعدم فتكون ثبوتية وهذا قريب مما نقله عن الإمام الرازي في باب التعين. ) والجواب عنه : (ما سبق) هناك بعينه.
His saying: (and specifically for unity etc.) If you say: this proof also applies to multiplicity, because it is said: if multiplicity were privative, it would be the privation of unity, so unity is either existential and multiplicity is nothing but the sum of unities being existential, or it is that if unity were non-existence it would be the non-existence of multiplicity which is its opposite, because it is impossible for it to be absolute non-existence, or non-existence of something else not opposite to it, and if it is non-existence of multiplicity, then multiplicity is either existential and unity is part of it, so unity would also be existential on the assumption that it is non-existence, and this is a contradiction although it is what is sought; or multiplicity is privative, then unity would be non-existence of non-existence and thus positive. And this is close to what he transmitted from Imam al-Razi in the chapter on individuation. And the answer to it is: (what preceded) there exactly.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the proof that unity is existential. The objector argues that the same proof can be applied to multiplicity, showing that it too must be existential. The proof for unity is thus not specific. The answer simply refers to what preceded in the discussion of individuation, which establishes that a privative term does not have to be the privation of a specific thing, so the reasoning fails. The passage shows how kalam arguments often rely on earlier established distinctions.

Correction: The answer refers to an earlier distinction that shows privative terms do not require a specific opposite, so the objector's assumption is invalid. The objection is thus deflected.

Unity and Multiplicity Are Not Intrinsic Opposites
The commentator explains that unity and multiplicity are opposites but not essentially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the opposition between unity and multiplicity is not essential but occurs due to their failure to inhere in the same subject.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity oppose, but not because of what they are.

This lesson addresses a subtle point in kalam: although unity and multiplicity are opposites, their opposition is not an essential property. The commentator explains that they are opposites only in the sense that they cannot both inhere in the same thing from one aspect. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the commentator's precise language.
The passage includes clarifications of key terms like 'intrinsic' and 'inherence'.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that unity and multiplicity are opposites, but their opposition is not intrinsic (dhati). Why? Because they do not occur to one subject individually.
Opposition exists, but not due to essences.
3
Understand 'intrinsic' in context
The commentator clarifies that 'intrinsic' here does not mean required by the essence, but rather what attaches to the essence. This is a technical usage.
The proof uses the phrase 'because they do not occur' to indicate accidental inherence.
4
Note the clarification on actual inherence
The negation of inherence is not about actual co-inherence, but about the impossibility of both inhering in the same subject simultaneously from the same aspect.
Opposites may not both actually inhere; one may inhere and the other not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : الوحدة والكثرة ليست ذاتية

بين الوحدة والكثرة مقابلة قطعاً إذ لا يجوز اجتماعهما في شيء واحد من جهة ،واحدة، لكن (مقابلة الوحدة والكثرة ليست ذاتية أي ليس بين ذاتيهما تقابل؛ ( لأنهما لا تعرضان لموضوع واحد بالشخص أي ليستا منسوبتين بالعروض إلى

قوله : (أي ليس إلخ) يعني ليس المراد بالذاتية مقتضى الذات بل ما يعرض الذات بدليل
قوله : لأنهما لا تعرضان .
قوله: (أي ليستا منسوبتين إلخ) أي ليس المراد نفي العروض بالفعل؛ لأنه لا يلزم أن يعرض المتقابلات بالفعل لموضوع واحد بدلاً، فإنه قد يلزم أحدهما للمحل، وقد يخلو المحل عنهما.
الوجودية، فالكثرة وجودية وإما عدمية فتكون الكثرة عدماً للعدم فتكون ثبوتية. قلت: هذا الدليل مثل الدليل المجرى في الوحدة لا عينه كيف ولا يصح أن يقال على تقدير وجودية الوحدة والكثرة جزء الوحدة على نحو ما قيل في الوحدة .
قوله : (أي ليستا (منسوبتين إشارة إلى أن ليس المراد بالعروض المنفي العروض بالفعل،
حتى يرد أن ذلك ليس بلازم للتقابل ؛ لجواز لزوم أحد المتقابلين للمحل .
The Fourth Observatory - The Third Purpose: Unity and multiplicity are not intrinsic.
Between unity and multiplicity there is opposition absolutely, for it is not permissible for both to coexist in one thing from one aspect, but the opposition of unity and multiplicity is not intrinsic, meaning there is no opposition between their essences, because they do not occur to one subject individually, meaning they are not related by inherence to
the investigation, so both knowledge and particularity are past; because this proof is mentioned in the books of the people. And his saying: 'a proof indicating etc.' is to signal that the agent of 'yakhuss' is the sum of what was mentioned, not that it is with its noun and predicate as is common.
His saying: (if unity were non-existence) the basis of the inference is the absence of distinction between the privative and non-existence. His saying: (absolute non-existence) meaning non-existence not attached to anything, otherwise it would be the contradictory of existence, not its equivalent. His saying: (or non-existence of something else) whether taken as specific or vague, and you may include this division in absolute non-existence by meaning non-existence not restricted to something specific, whether it is not restricted at all or attached to something.
His saying: (what preceded) which is that the privative is not necessarily non-existence of something, so the mentioned disjunction is invalid.
His saying: (meaning not etc.) means that by 'intrinsic' is not meant what is required by the essence, but rather what occurs to the essence, as evidenced by his saying: because they do not occur.
His saying: (meaning they are not related etc.) means that what is negated is not actual inherence; because it is not necessary that opposites actually inhere in one subject alternately, for it may be that one of them inheres in the subject, and the subject may be devoid of both.
The existential, so multiplicity is existential, or if privative then multiplicity is non-existence of non-existence and thus positive. I say: this proof is like the proof applied to unity, not the same; how can it be said on the assumption that unity is existential and multiplicity is a part of unity? on the manner of what was said in unity.
His saying: (meaning they are not related) is an indication that what is meant by negated inherence is not actual inherence, so that it might be objected that this is not necessary for opposition; because it is permissible for one of the opposites to inhere in the subject.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of opposition between unity and multiplicity. It first asserts that they are opposites because they cannot coexist in one thing from the same aspect. However, the opposition is not intrinsic (dhati), meaning it does not arise from their essences, but rather because they do not actually inhere in the same subject. The commentator clarifies that 'intrinsic' here does not mean essential but rather whatever attaches to the essence. He also explains that the negation of inherence is not of actual inherence, because opposites may not both actually occur to a subject; one may inhere and the other not. The passage also includes a note that a previous proof regarding unity and multiplicity being existential or privative is similar to a proof in the chapter on individuation.

Key Terms
الذاتية (al-dhātiyya, intrinsic)
المقابلة (al-muqābala, opposition)
العروض (al-ʿurūḍ, inherence)
العدم (al-ʿadam, non-existence)
الوجود (al-wujūd, existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity and multiplicity are intrinsic opposites because they cannot coexist.
Correction: The passage explains that their opposition is not based on their essences but on the fact that they cannot inhere in the same subject. Thus the opposition is accidental, not essential.

Checkpoint
Why is the opposition between unity and multiplicity not intrinsic according to this passage?
A) Because they are not opposites
B) Because they do not inhere in the same subject
C) Because one is privative and the other existential
D) Because the author says so

Key Takeaway
The opposition between unity and multiplicity is real but accidental, not essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When do you find yourself insisting that two opposing ideas cannot both be true because you think their opposition is essential, when in fact it might be accidental? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider your own stubbornness in holding onto certain beliefs?
?
Reflect on a situation where you have treated a difference of opinion as if it were an intrinsic contradiction, thereby closing off understanding. How can you now approach such differences with the awareness that opposition is not always rooted in the nature of things?
?
The lesson distinguishes between essential and accidental opposition. Is there a belief or habit you hold that you have assumed is intrinsic to your identity, but might actually be accidental? Will you dare to examine it?

The Argument for Unity's Existence and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes an argument for unity being existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disjunctive argument that unity must be existent and the principle that refutes it.

Core Idea
The argument forces unity to exist, but the refutation shows it assumes an unnecessary link.

This passage presents a logical argument meant to prove that unity (the property of being one) is an existent attribute, not a mere absence. Al-Jurjani lays out the argument and then dismisses it with a principle from earlier discussion. We will see how the argument works and why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation one time. Notice the tight logical structure of the argument.
The argument uses a disjunction: if unity is non-existence, then it must be non-existence of its opposite.
2
Analyze the disjunctive argument
Trace the two branches: if multiplicity is existent, unity becomes part of it and thus existent; if multiplicity is non-existent, unity becomes non-existence of non-existence, hence positive. Both lead back to existence.
The argument aims for a contradiction if unity were truly non-existent.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer says 'what preceded' – a principle that a non-existent need not be the non-existence of a specific thing. This invalidates the disjunction.
The refutation is brief but decisive: it cuts the assumption that non-existence must be relative to an opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : الوحدة والكثرة ليست ذاتية. كونها وجودية، هو أنه لو كانت الوحدة عدماً لكان عدم الكثرة التي تقابلها؛ لامتناع أن تكون عدماً مطلقاً، أو عدماً لشيء آخر لا تقابله، وإذا كانت عدماً للكثرة فالكثرة إما وجودية والوحدة جزؤها، فتكون الوحدة أيضاً موجودة على تقدير كونها معدومة وهذا خلف مع أنه المطلوب. وإما عدمية، فتكون الوحدة عدماً للعدم فتكون ثبوتية وهذا قريب مما نقله عن الإمام الرازي في باب التعين. والجواب عنه: ما سبق هناك بعينه. قوله: (ما سبق) وهو أن العدمي لا يجب أن يكون عدماً لشيء، فلا يصح الترديد المذكور.
The fourth observatory - the third aim: Unity and multiplicity are not essential. That they are existent is [proved] by the argument that if unity were non-existence, then it would be the non-existence of multiplicity which is its opposite; because it is impossible for it to be absolute non-existence, or non-existence of something else not opposing it. And if it is non-existence of multiplicity, then multiplicity is either existent and unity is part of it, so unity would also be existent under the assumption that it is non-existent, which is contradictory—and that is what is required. Or [multiplicity] is non-existent, then unity is non-existence of non-existence, so it is positive. This is close to what he transmitted from Imam al-Razi in the chapter on determination. The answer to it is: what preceded there exactly. His words: 'what preceded'—that is, that a non-existent does not have to be non-existence of something, so the mentioned disjunction is not valid.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that unity is existent (not non-existent) and then gives the standard answer. The argument is a disjunctive syllogism: assume unity is non-existent. If it is non-existence, it must be the non-existence of its opposite, multiplicity. Then multiplicity is either existent or non-existent. If existent, unity is part of it, so unity is also existent—contradiction. If non-existent, then unity is the non-existence of non-existence, which is positive, so again existent. The answer, 'what preceded', refers to an earlier principle: a non-existent need not be the non-existence of any particular thing; thus the disjunction is unfounded. The commentary clarifies this principle, showing why the argument fails.

Key Terms
عدمية
وجودية
عدم
وجود
تقابل
تعين
ترديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disjunctive argument conclusively proves that unity is existent.
Correction: The argument assumes that non-existence must be tied to a specific opposite, but this is not necessary. Therefore the disjunction is not valid, and the proof fails.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument for the existence of unity based on its opposition to multiplicity?
A) If unity were non-existent, multiplicity would be impossible.
B) If unity were non-existent, then multiplicity would either be existent or non-existent, leading to contradiction.
C) Unity is part of multiplicity, so it must exist.
D) Al-Razi said that unity is existent.

Key Takeaway
The argument that unity must be existent is refuted because it wrongly assumes that non-existence is always relative to a specific opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you often assume that if you are not one thing, you must be its exact opposite? Think of a specific example. How might this lesson's refutation of that assumption free you to see yourself more accurately before Allah? What would it mean to accept that your 'non-existence' of a virtue does not necessarily imply the existence of its opposite vice, but rather a neutral absence? Commit to catching yourself in this binary thinking once today and reframing it.
?
The argument forces unity to exist logically, but the refutation shows that non-existence does not require a specific opposite. When you consider the oneness of Allah (tawhid), do you treat it as a mere logical conclusion or as a living reality that demands your heart? In what ways have you reduced tawhid to an intellectual abstraction rather than letting it transform your actions and fears? Today, before each prayer, pause and remind yourself that your understanding of unity must go beyond logic to direct your worship.
?
The refutation hinges on the principle that a non-existent ('adami) does not have to be tied to a particular thing. Are there areas of your spiritual life where you feel an absence (e.g., lack of khushu', rizq, or closeness to Allah) and immediately assume that this absence is linked to a specific cause (e.g., a particular sin, lack of effort)? Using the lesson's insight, how can you break that automatic link and instead turn to Allah without assuming the opposite? Write down one such area and resolve to not jump to conclusions about its cause for the next week.

Why Unity and Multiplicity Are Not Essentially Opposite
Al-Jurjani defines and proves the non-essential opposition of unity and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opposition between unity and multiplicity is not essential, despite them being opposites, and the conditions for essential opposition in kalam.

Core Idea
Not all opposites are essentially opposite; true essential opposition requires a single subject.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary addresses a subtle point: unity and multiplicity clearly cannot coexist in one thing, so they are opposites. But are they essentially opposite? Al-Jurjani argues that they are not, because they fail the key condition of applying to one individual subject. Understanding this distinction clarifies how Islamic theologians classify attributes and relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the author's terminology and flow.
Notice the emphasis on 'subject' (موضوع) and 'inherence' (عروض).
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Jurjani states that unity and multiplicity are opposed but not essentially opposite. Locate this claim in the text.
The key phrase is 'ليست ذاتية' – not essential.
3
Understand the condition for essential opposition
Essential opposition requires two things to be related to one individual subject. Why is this condition crucial?
Opposites must be able to alternate in the same subject; see the definition of تقابل.
4
Examine the two proofs
The author gives two reasons: (1) the subject of unity is part of the subject of multiplicity, and (2) unity is prior to multiplicity. Grasp how each reason shows the lack of a single subject.
The part-whole relation prevents them from being correlatives (متضايفان).
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters: it prevents false analogies and clarifies how attributes like unity and multiplicity are related in Islamic theology.
Essential opposition is stricter than mere logical opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث: الوحدة والكثرة ليست ذاتية. بين الوحدة والكثرة مقابلة قطعاً إذ لا يجوز اجتماعهما في شيء واحد من جهة واحدة، لكن مقابلة الوحدة والكثرة ليست ذاتية أي ليس بين ذاتيهما تقابل؛ لأنهما لا تعرضان لموضوع واحد بالشخص أي ليستا منسوبتين بالعروض إلى موضوع واحد شخصي. واتحاد الموضوع معتبر في المتقابلين مطلقاً لأن التقابل هو امتناع اجتماع شيئين في موضوع واحد من جهة واحدة. ومعنى ذلك أن العقل إذا لاحظهما وقاسهما إلى موضوع واحد شخصي جوز بمجرد ملاحظتهما ثبوت كل واحد منهما فيه على سبيل البدل دون الاجتماع من جهة واحدة لكن ربما امتنع ثبوت أحدهما له بسبب تعين الآخر فيه لأمر من خارج، وليس الحال في الوحدة والكثرة كذلك لأن موضوع الوحدة جزء لموضوع الكثرة، كما أن الوحدة جزء لها. ولأن الوحدة متقدمة وجوباً على الكثرة؛ لأنها مبدأ لها وجزء منها، فلا تكون الوحدة متضايفة للكثرة؛ لأن المتضايفين متكافئان لا تقدم لأحدها على الآخر وجوداً ولا تعقلاً. وأيضاً يمكن تعقل الوحدة بدون الكثرة، فلا تضايف بينهما. قوله: (أي ليس إلخ) يعني ليس المراد بالذاتية مقتضى الذات بل ما يعرض الذات بدليل قوله: لأنهما لا تعرضان. قوله: (أي ليستا منسوبتين إلخ) أي ليس المراد نفي العروض بالفعل؛ لأنه لا يلزم أن يعرض المتقابلات بالفعل لموضوع واحد بدلاً، فإنه قد يلزم أحدهما للمحل، وقد يخلو المحل عنهما.
The third aim: Unity and multiplicity are not essential. There is certainly opposition between unity and multiplicity since they cannot both coexist in one thing from one aspect, but the opposition of unity and multiplicity is not essential, i.e., there is no opposition between their essences; because they do not apply to one individual subject, i.e., they are not related by inherence to one individual subject. And the unity of subject is considered in opposites absolutely, because opposition is the impossibility of two things coexisting in one subject from one aspect. The meaning of that is that the intellect, when it observes them and compares them to one individual subject, permits, merely by observing them, the existence of each of them in it alternately without coexistence from one aspect, but perhaps the existence of one of them is prevented from belonging to it due to the specification of the other in it for an external reason. The case is not so with unity and multiplicity because the subject of unity is part of the subject of multiplicity, just as unity is part of it. And because unity is necessarily prior to multiplicity, because it is the principle of it and part of it, so unity is not correlative with multiplicity, because correlatives are equivalent, neither having priority over the other in existence or conception. Also, unity can be conceived without multiplicity, so there is no correlation between them. His words: 'i.e., not' – he means by 'essential' not what is necessitated by the essence, but what occurs to the essence, indicated by his words: 'because they do not apply.' His words: 'i.e., they are not related' – he means that the negation of inherence is not actual inherence, because it is not necessary that opposites actually inhere in one subject alternately, for one of them may attach to the locus, and the locus may be empty of both.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines and proves why unity and multiplicity are not essentially opposite. It first states that although they are opposed (they cannot coexist in one thing from one aspect), their opposition is not essential—meaning it is not a property of their essences. The reason is that they do not apply to one individual subject: the subject of unity is part of the subject of multiplicity. The author then explains the definition of opposition: it requires a single subject such that the intellect can conceive each existing in it alternately. But unity and multiplicity fail this because their subjects are related as part and whole. Additionally, unity is prior to multiplicity both in existence and conception, so they are not correlatives (which require equivalence). Finally, the commentary clarifies that 'essential' here means 'occurring to the essence,' not 'necessitated by the essence,' and that the negation of actual inherence does not mean inherence is impossible—it simply means the subject may be empty of both.

Key Terms
ذاتية
تقابل
عروض
موضوع
متضايفان
تقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two concepts are opposites, their opposition is essential.
Correction: Essential opposition requires that the two opposites apply to one single subject and be correlatives. Unity and multiplicity fail this because their subjects are related as part and whole, and unity is prior, so their opposition is not essential.

Checkpoint
Why are unity and multiplicity not essentially opposite according to the author?
A) Because they can coexist in the same subject.
B) Because they do not apply to one individual subject.
C) Because unity is posterior to multiplicity.
D) Because they are not opposites.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are not essentially opposite because they do not share one individual subject and unity is prior to multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own life, where are you trying to reconcile contradictory qualities without first asking if they share a single subject? How might this logical distinction about unity and multiplicity reveal areas where you are inconsistent or confused in your beliefs?
?
If unity is prior to multiplicity both conceptually and in existence, what does that mean for your priorities? Do you actively prioritize oneness in your worship and understanding of Allah, or do you let multiplicity of concerns fragment your devotion?
?
Al-Jurjani says that unity can be conceived without multiplicity. Can you honestly say that you focus on the oneness of Allah without getting lost in the multiplicity of His creation? What is one concrete step you can take today to train your mind to conceive of unity without relying on multiplicity?

Definition of Taqābul and Why Unity and Multiplicity Are Not Opposites
Defines taqābul and argues unity and multiplicity lack a common subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of taqābul (opposition) and why unity and multiplicity do not satisfy its condition of sharing a single subject.

Core Idea
Opposites must share a single subject; unity and multiplicity do not.

This passage from al-Sanūsī’s work establishes the technical definition of opposition (taqābul) in logic and Kalām. It then applies that definition to test whether unity and multiplicity are truly opposites. The conclusion challenges a common assumption and reveals a deeper principle about the relationship between these two fundamental concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first defines taqābul and then applies it to unity and multiplicity.
Pay attention to the phrase 'موضوع واحد شخصي' — a single personal subject.
2
Identify the definition of taqābul
Locate the sentence that defines taqābul as the impossibility of two things existing together in one subject from one aspect. Reflect on its components.
The definition includes three conditions: two things, one subject, and one aspect.
3
Understand the intellect’s role
The author explains that when the intellect observes two candidates for opposition, it judges it possible for each to inhere in the subject alternately. Grasp this alternation concept.
Alternation (بدل) is key: the intellect permits each individually, but not both together.
4
Apply to unity and multiplicity
Read the final sentence: the subject of unity is part of the subject of multiplicity. Therefore, they do not share a single personal subject. Conclude why they are not opposites.
This is the main argument: different subjects prevent the required comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : الوحدة والكثرة ليست ذاتية موضوع واحد شخصي، واتحاد الموضوع معتبر في المتقابلين مطلقاً لأن التقابل هو امتناع اجتماع شيئين في موضوع واحد من جهة واحدة ومعنى ذلك أن العقل إذا لاحظهما وقاسهما إلى موضوع واحد شخصي جوز بمجرد ملاحظتهما ثبوت كل واحد منهما فيه على سبيل البدل دون الاجتماع من جهة واحدة لكن ربما امتنع ثبوت أحدهما له بسبب تعين الآخر فيه لأمر من خارج، وليس الحال في الوحدة والكثرة كذلك لأن موضوع الوحدة جزء لموضوع الكثرة، كما أن الوحدة جزء لها.
Observation Four - Aim Three: Unity and multiplicity are not innate opposites. [They require] a single personal subject, and the unity of the subject is considered in opposites absolutely, because opposition is the impossibility of two things coming together in one subject from one aspect. The meaning of that is that the intellect, when it observes them and compares them to a single personal subject, by merely observing them, judges it possible for each one of them to subsist in it by way of alternation, without coming together from one aspect. But perhaps the subsistence of one of them is impossible for it due to the other’s being determined in it for an external reason. However, the situation is not like that for unity and multiplicity, because the subject of unity is part of the subject of multiplicity, just as unity is a part of it.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for two things to be opposites (taqābul) and then argues that unity (waḥda) and multiplicity (kathra) do not meet that condition. It states that opposites require a single personal subject (mawḍūʿ wāḥid shakhṣī) and that the intellect, upon considering them, must judge it possible for each to inhere in that subject alternately, though not simultaneously from one aspect. The impossibility of simultaneous inherence is what makes them opposites. However, for unity and multiplicity, their subjects are not the same: the subject of unity is a part of the subject of multiplicity, just as unity itself is a part of multiplicity. Therefore, the intellect cannot compare them to a single subject, so they are not truly opposites.

Key Terms
تقابل
موضوع واحد شخصي
الوحدة
الكثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that unity and multiplicity are opposites because they are contradictory concepts.
Correction: The passage clarifies that true opposition requires a single subject for both; unity and multiplicity have different subjects (the subject of unity is part of the subject of multiplicity), so they fail the condition.

Checkpoint
Why are unity and multiplicity not considered opposites according to the definition given?
A) Because they cannot be conceived together.
B) Because their subjects are not identical; the subject of unity is part of the subject of multiplicity.
C) Because the intellect does not permit their alternation.
D) Because they are both necessarily prior.

Key Takeaway
Opposition requires a single personal subject; unity and multiplicity do not share such a subject, so they are not true opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that true opposites share a single subject; unity and multiplicity do not because one is part of the other. In your spiritual life, do you treat your moments of focusing on the oneness of Allah (tawḥīd) and your involvement in the multiplicity of worldly affairs as conflicting opposites? What if, like in the passage, one is part of the other? How would that reshape your daily worship and priorities? Commit to reflecting on one situation this week where you can see unity and multiplicity as complementary rather than opposing.
?
The intellect judges it possible for opposites to alternate in the same subject. Think of a personal struggle—perhaps between hope and fear of Allah, or between reliance on self and reliance on Allah. Have you assumed these are opposites that cannot coexist? Based on this lesson's principle, are they truly opposites? What would it look like to allow them to alternate in your heart, each in its proper place? Will you consciously invite both hope and fear into your prayer this evening?

Unity and Multiplicity: Not Correlatives
Proves unity and multiplicity are not correlatives or other opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity and multiplicity cannot be correlatives or any other type of direct logical opposite, based on the necessary priority of unity.

Core Idea
Unity's priority disproves all direct opposition with multiplicity.

Sanusi continues his analysis of unity and multiplicity by examining whether they are logical opposites. He systematically proves that they cannot be correlatives, contraries, privations, or negations. This lesson walks through each argument, showing how the fundamental priority of unity underlies all these negations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim against correlation
Read the first part of the passage: unity is necessarily prior to multiplicity because it is its source and part. Correlatives must be equal in existence and conception, so priority destroys correlation.
Correlatives like father and son are co-dependent.
2
See the second argument against correlation
Unity can be conceived without multiplicity, but not vice versa. This independence also negates correlation.
3
Extend to other oppositions
The passage then argues unity is not a contrary, privation, or negation of multiplicity. In each case, the reason is that unity constitutes multiplicity, while true opposites do not constitute each other.
Contraries: hot and cold; neither constitutes the other.
4
Synthesize the key insight
All these denials hinge on the fact that unity is constitutive and prior. This establishes that unity and multiplicity are not genuine opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولأن الوحدة ) متقدمة وجوباً ( على الكثرة؛ لأنها مبدأ لها وجزء منها، (فلا تكون) الوحدة ( متضايفة للكثرة؛ لأن المتضايفين متكافئان لا تقدم لأحدهما على الآخر وجوداً ولا تعقلاً. وأيضاً يمكن تعقل الوحدة بدون الكثرة، فلا تضايف بينهما . ( ولا ضداً لها؛ إذ ليس أحد الضدين متقدماً على الآخر وجوباً، (و) الوحدة (مقومة) للكثرة ، فلا تكون الوحدة ( عدماً لها، فلا يكون التقابل بينهما تقابل العدم ( والملكة، ولا السلب والإيجاب؛ لأن أحدهما لا يقوم الآخر ، ( ولا ضداً) أيضاً لأن أحد الضدين لا يقوم ضده، وإنما جعل التقدم اللازم من التقويم دليلاً على نفي التضايف والتضاد .
(And because unity) is necessarily prior (to multiplicity; because it is the beginning of it and part of it, (so) unity (is not) correlative to multiplicity; because correlatives are equivalent, neither has priority over the other in existence or conception. Also, unity can be conceived without multiplicity, so there is no correlation between them. (Nor is it) a contrary to it; since neither of two contraries is necessarily prior to the other, (and) unity (is constitutive) of multiplicity, so unity is not (a privation) of it, so the opposition between them is not opposition of privation and possession, nor of negation and affirmation; because one of them does not constitute the other, (nor a contrary) also, because one of two contraries does not constitute its contrary. And the priority necessitated by constitution was made a proof for negating correlation and contrariety.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents two main arguments that unity and multiplicity are not correlatives (mutadā'ifān), and then extends the reasoning to deny other types of opposition. First, unity is necessarily prior to multiplicity because it is the source and a part of multiplicity; correlatives must be equal in existence and conception, so priority negates correlation. Second, unity can be conceived without multiplicity, which also negates correlation. Then it argues that unity is not a contrary (ḍidd) of multiplicity because contraries are not necessarily prior to each other, and unity is constitutive (muqawwima) of multiplicity, which means it is not a privation (ʿadam) nor a negation (salb) of multiplicity, since the privation and negation are not constitutive of their opposites. Thus, the opposition between unity and multiplicity is not real opposition in any of these categories.

Key Terms
متضايفان
تقدم وجوبي
تقويم
ضد
عدم وملكة
سلب وإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that mere conceptual priority suffices to negate correlation, but the passage emphasizes both existence and conception.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that correlatives must be equal in both existence (wujūdan) and conception (taʿqulan). Priority in either is sufficient to negate correlation.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given that unity and multiplicity are not correlatives?
A) They cannot be conceived together.
B) Unity is necessarily prior to multiplicity.
C) They share the same subject.
D) They are both contraries.

Key Takeaway
Unity's necessary priority over multiplicity proves they are not correlatives or any other real opposites.

What would it take, starting today, to re-center your intentions on the One who alone is truly prior?
?
If unity can be conceived without multiplicity, but multiplicity cannot be conceived without unity, how does that reality speak to your dependence on Allah? Do you ever act as if the many blessings and trials in your life are independent of His single will? When you feel overwhelmed by the 'many,' do you turn to the One? Write down one concrete habit you can adopt to remind yourself of this priority.
?
The passage denies that unity and multiplicity are true opposites—they are not correlatives, not contraries. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes pit the oneness of your faith against the diversity of your experiences? This lesson forces you to see that the multiplicity of creation is not in opposition to the Creator's unity, but is constituted by it. Personal Subject and Mental Permission in Taqābul
Explains the meaning of 'personal subject' and 'mental permission' in opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meanings of 'personal subject' and 'mental permission' as used in the definition of opposition, and how the impossibility in question concerns the intellect's judgment rather than actual reality.

Core Idea
Opposition is about the intellect's inability to judge coexistence from one aspect, not actual impossibility.

This passage delves into the technical vocabulary of opposition in kalam. The author clarifies that 'personal' means a single subject without multiplicity, and then explains that the impossibility in opposition is not in reality but in the mind's judgment. Understanding these nuances prevents common misunderstandings and lays the groundwork for the entire theory of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms like 'شخصي' and 'تجويز عقلي'.
2
Identify the definition of 'personal subject'
Look at the first part: 'شخصي أي ما لا يكون فيه تعدد أصلاً'. Understand that it means a subject with no multiplicity even by consideration, but correlatives can coexist in the same person under different aspects.
The example of Zayd being both father and son illustrates this.
3
Distinguish impossibility in reality vs. intellect
The passage distinguishes between 'امتناع الاجتماع في نفس الأمر' and 'امتناع الاجتماع في العقل'. The impossibility meant in opposition is not that the two things cannot exist together in reality, but that the intellect cannot judge them as coexisting.
For instance, death and knowledge can coexist in reality but are not opposites.
4
Understand the reduction to alternation
Since the intellect cannot judge coexistence, it necessarily judges alternation (تعاقب) in the same subject. This shows that alternation is a necessary implication.
This refutes an objection that only relation to a subject is required.
5
Note the condition of conformity to reality
The passage ends by noting that the intellect's judgment must conform to actual reality (تجويزا مطابقا لنفس الأمر).
This ensures the definition is grounded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (شخصي) أي ما لا يكون فيه تعدد أصلاً، ولو بالاعتبار، فإن المتضايفين قد يجتمعان في موضع واحد بالشخص إذا كان فيه تعدد بالاعتبار كالأبوة والبنوة المجتمعتين في زید باعتبارين المتخالفين قد يمتنع قوله : ( ومعنى ذلك إلخ) أي ليس المراد امتناع الاجتماع في نفس الأمر؛ لأن المفهومين اجتماعهما في نفس الأمر مع عدم تقابلهما كالموت مع العلم والقدرة والوجوب مع التركيب والتحيز بل امتناع الاجتماع في العقل بأن لم يجوز العقل اجتماعهما، ثم امتناع تجويز الاجتماع الذي هو عبارة عن حصول الشيئين معاً إما بامتناع تجويز الحصول، أو بامتناع المعية والأول ليس بمراد؛ إذ المتقابلان لا يمتنع حصولهما في المحل فضلاً عن التجويز، فتعين الثاني . وامتناع تجويز معيتهما في المحل يستلزم تجويز تعاقبهما، فيؤول معنى التعريف إلى ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره فاندفع ما قيل : إن المعتبر في مفهوم المتقابلين نسبة كل منهما إلى محل واحد، وأما أنه يجب أن يجوز العقل ثبوت كل منهما فيه بدلاً فلا . قوله : (جوز) أي العقل تجويزاً مطابقا لنفس الأمر.
His saying: (personal) i.e., that which has no multiplicity at all, even by consideration, for correlatives may come together in a single personal location if there is multiplicity of consideration, like paternity and sonship coming together in Zayd under two considerations of opposite aspects, may be impossible. His saying: (And the meaning of that etc.) meaning the intended [impossibility] is not impossibility of coexistence in actual reality; because the two concepts coexist in actual reality without their being opposites, like death with knowledge and power, and necessity with composition and spatial occupation. Rather, it is impossibility of coexistence in the intellect, in that the intellect does not judge their coexistence possible. Then the impossibility of judging coexistence, which is the occurrence of the two things together, is either due to impossibility of judging occurrence, or impossibility of judging togetherness; the first is not intended, since opposites are not impossible to occur in the subject, let alone judgment, so the second is determined. And the impossibility of judging their togetherness in the subject necessitates judging their alternation, so the meaning of the definition reduces to what the commentator (quddisa sirruhu) mentioned, and thus is repelled what was said: that what is considered in the concept of opposites is the relation of each to a single subject, but that the intellect must judge the possibility of each in it alternately is not [required]. His saying: (judges it possible) i.e., the intellect, in a judgment conforming to actual reality.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory clarification of key terms in the definition of taqābul. It first explains that 'personal' (shakhsī) means a subject that has no multiplicity even by consideration, though correlatives can coincide in the same person under different aspects (like Zayd being both father and son). Then it clarifies the phrase 'the meaning of that etc.', distinguishing between impossibility in actual reality and impossibility in the intellect's judgment. The intended impossibility is not that the two opposites cannot exist together in reality, because some non-opposites like death and knowledge can coexist. Rather, it is that the intellect cannot judge them as coexisting in the same subject from one aspect. The impossibility of judging coexistence leads to judging alternation. This refutes an objection that only the relation to a single subject is required, not the intellect's judgment of possibility. Finally, it notes that the intellect's judgment must conform to actual reality.

Key Terms

تجويز عقلي
نفس الأمر
اعتبار
