Reflect on your own life: are there areas where you treat important opposites (like truth and falsehood, justice and oppression) as merely accidental or situational, rather than essential? Write down one specific belief you hold that you need to re-examine to see if it is an essential part of your faith or merely accidental.

Resolving the Objection and Distinguishing Types of Opposition
Responds to objection, presents transmitted view, and distinguishes three types of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the objection about conceptual difference and distinguishes different kinds of opposition between unity and multiplicity.

Core Idea
Even if concepts differ, the opposition remains; and different contexts yield different types of opposition.

The commentator addresses the objection that the definitions involve different concepts from pure negation. He argues that even if unity is reduced to non-division, multiplicity is not division. Then he presents a transmitted view that distinguishes between conceptual and extensional opposition, and within extensional, accidental and contrariety.

Guided Path
1
Read the response to the objection
Understand that even if unity is non-division, multiplicity is a collection of non-divisions, not division.
2
Understand the transmitted view
The transmitted view suggests that the intended meaning was about extensional opposition, not conceptual.
3
Grasp the three types of opposition
Learn the three types: essential (conceptual), accidental (part-whole), and contrariety (mutual annulment).

Observation 4 - Goal 3: Unity and multiplicity are not self-subsistent. Such that it divides into things that share in its reality, like two individuals or individuals of one species. And do not be unaware that the multiplicity composed of things of different realities, like man, horse, and donkey, enters the definition of unity and leaves the definition of multiplicity. So it is better to say: Unity is that a thing is such that it is not divisible, and multiplicity is that it is divisible. And it is not hidden that their opposition (i.e., the opposition of the mentioned in the two definitions of unity and multiplicity) is by negation and affirmation, and that (the opposition of negation and affirmation) is essential opposition. So between unity and multiplicity, as defined by these two definitions, there is essential opposition, not accidental, as they mentioned. Unless you make unity and multiplicity two things that are followed by that mentioned in their definition, for then it is possible that their opposition is not essential. But it is not proven that they are such things, and nothing in their speech indicates that. And there is consideration in it; because the opposition of negation and affirmation is only between division and its negation, and there is no doubt that a thing being such that it is not divisible is a concept different from the concept of non-division, and likewise its being such that it is divisible is a concept different from the concept of division. If you say: there is leniency in the expression, and the intended meaning is that unity is non-division. I say: this, if valid for unity, does not apply to multiplicity; because its reality is composed of unities, so if unity is non-division, then the reality of multiplicity is a collection of negations of divisions, and that is a concept different from the concept of division, even if the concept of division is necessary for it. Then he said: (And it is not far that they intended the many and the one from it, not the concept of the one and the many) meaning that it is not far that what they mean by their statement: There is no essential opposition between unity and multiplicity is that there is no opposition between multiplicity and the unity that is its part except accidentally, in terms of measure and measured as established, not that there is essential opposition between the concepts of unity and multiplicity. And it has been narrated from him that he said: If opposition is considered between their two concepts, it is essential opposition by negation and affirmation as mentioned in the book. And if considered between what they apply to, then if considered between multiplicity and the unity that is its part, it is accidental opposition, as is well-known. And if considered between multiplicity and the unity that occurs upon the subject of multiplicity and annuls it and negates it, like multiple waters when poured into a single jar, or between unity and the multiplicity that occurs upon the subject of unity and negates it, like a single water poured into multiple vessels, then it is opposition by contrariety; because the habit of a contrary when it comes to the place of the other is to annul it and negate it, and the habit of unity and multiplicity... His statement: 'I say: this etc.' has a discussion; because the intention of the author is that between the two mentioned concepts in their definitions there is opposition by affirmation and negation, and that does not harm that both concepts or one of them is different from their realities, and therefore he said: 'unless they are made' etc. His statement: 'that there is no opposition between multiplicity and the unity that is its part etc.' So what is meant by the many and the one is multiplicity and unity in terms of being described by multiplicity and unity, which is what they apply to absolutely. And the pronoun 'from it' refers to the many, and he only did not say: they intended what is described by multiplicity and unity, to avoid the misunderstanding that they intend the specific individuals of them. His statement: 'between the concepts of unity and multiplicity', so what is meant by his statement: 'not the concept of the one and the many' is that they are non-divisible and divisible, because the vague essence is outside the concept of the derivative, as the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) clarified in his books, and they are the concepts of unity and multiplicity. His statement: 'And it has been narrated from him etc.' added in this narration the intention of multiplicity and unity that occur upon them. His statement: 'because the habit etc.' its summary is that unity and multiplicity that occur upon a single subject, each annuls the other, and any two things whose habit is that are two contraries, and this is not reasoning in the second figure as the apparent wording suggests, so that it would be objected that it does not yield from two affirmatives.

Commentary
This passage defines unity (al-wahda) and multiplicity (al-kathra) and examines the nature of their opposition (taqābul). The author begins by stating the subject: unity and multiplicity are not self-subsistent (dhatiyya). He then provides a preliminary definition: a thing is such that it divides into things sharing its reality, like individuals of a species. However, he points out a problem: a multiplicity composed of different realities (e.g., man, horse, donkey) would fall under the definition of unity because they share in the reality of 'animal'? Actually, the author says such multiplicity enters the definition of unity and exits the definition of multiplicity, so a better definition is needed. Thus, he proposes: unity is that a thing is such that it is not divisible (lā yanqasim), and multiplicity is that it is divisible (yanqasim). He then asserts that the opposition between these two definitions is by negation (salb) and affirmation (ījāb), which is essential (bi al-dhāt) opposition, not accidental. However, he introduces a caveat: if one treats unity and multiplicity as two entities that are followed by these definitions, then the opposition might not be essential. But he notes that this has not been proven. The author then raises an objection: the opposition by negation is between division (inqisām) and its negation (salbu al-inqisām), yet the definitions use 'such that it is not divisible' and 'such that it is divisible', which are different concepts from non-division and division themselves. A hypothetical objection suggests that the intended meaning is simply non-division for unity. The author responds that even if that were valid for unity, it fails for multiplicity because multiplicity is composed of unities, so if unity is non-division, multiplicity becomes a collection of negations of divisions, which is different from division. The discussion then turns to a transmitted view: perhaps the ancients meant by 'no essential opposition between unity and multiplicity' that there is no opposition between multiplicity and the unity that is its part, except accidentally. Further distinctions are considered: if opposition is between the concepts, it is essential; if between their referents, it varies. Specifically, if between multiplicity and the unity that is its part, it is accidental; if between them as properties that annul each other when occurring on the same subject, it is opposition by contrariety (taḍādd). The commentator clarifies these points, noting that the author's intention was that the two definitions yield an opposition by negation, even if the concepts differ. The passage ends with a note that the argument is not a second-figure syllogism but a straightforward description.

Author's Intent on Conceptual Opposition
Commentator explains author's intent on conceptual opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conceptual opposition between unity and multiplicity is valid even if the concepts differ from their real definitions.

Core Idea
A concept's opposition need not match its reality.

This lesson focuses on a key clarification in the debate over unity and multiplicity. The commentator explains that the author's objection-answer hinges on the conceptual opposition between 'unity' and 'multiplicity' by affirmation and negation, irrespective of whether these concepts align with their real definitions. Understanding this point resolves a potential misunderstanding in the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the commentator's assessment that there is 'discussion' (baḥth) in the author's statement, and then the clarification of the author's intent.
Pay attention to the phrase 'تقابل بالإيجاب والسلب' (opposition by affirmation and negation).
2
Identify the author's intent
The commentator states that the author's purpose is to show that there is an opposition by affirmation and negation between the two concepts. Recognize that this opposition is logical: one affirms what the other negates.
3
Understand the irrelevance of real definitions
The commentator emphasizes that it does not harm the opposition if the concepts themselves differ from their realities. Grasp why this distinction is important: even if the definition of 'unity' as non-division is not exactly the concept used in argument, the conceptual opposition still holds.
The author signals this by saying 'unless they are made'.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this principle applies to other theological or logical discussions: conceptual frameworks can be valid even when they do not perfectly correspond to extra-mental realities.
This is a recurring theme in kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قلت : هذا إلخ) فيه بحث؛ لأن مقصود المصنف أن بين المفهومين المذكورين تعريفيهما تقابلاً بالإيجاب والسلب ولا يضر ذلك كون كلا المفهومين أو أحدهما مغايراً لحقيقتيهما، ولذا قال : إلا أن يجعلا إلخ.
His statement: 'I say: This etc.' There is discussion in it; because the author's intent is that between the two mentioned concepts, their definitions have an opposition by affirmation and negation, and it does not harm that both concepts or one of them is different from their realities. Hence he said: 'Unless they are made etc.'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a brief commentary on the author's response ('I say: This...') in the earlier objection-answer. The commentator notes that there is a discussion (baḥth) because the author's real purpose is to establish that between the two concepts (unity and multiplicity) there is an essential opposition by affirmation and negation (i.e., one affirms what the other negates). It does not matter that these concepts are different from the real definitions (ḥaqīqa) of unity and multiplicity (non-division and division). The author indicates this by saying 'unless they are made' (except that they are considered as concepts). The commentator thus clarifies that the conceptual opposition is valid regardless of the nature of the definitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection-answer fails because the definition of unity (non-division) is not the same as the concept of unity in the argument.
Correction: The commentator explains that conceptual opposition is independent of the real definitions; the argument remains valid as long as the concepts are opposed by affirmation and negation.

Checkpoint

A) To show that their definitions are identical.
B) To establish that between them there is an essential opposition by affirmation and negation.
C) To prove that they are both accidents.
D) To deny any opposition between them.

Key Takeaway
Conceptual opposition by affirmation and negation is valid regardless of whether the concepts match their real definitions.

Two Cases When Unity and Multiplicity Do Not Cohabit
Author explains two possibilities when multiple things come together, showing no union in subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why multiplicity and unity cannot coexist in the same subject, based on two logical scenarios presented by the author.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity never share the same subject, regardless of how things combine.

This short but tightly-reasoned passage tackles a subtle question: if multiple things come together to form a single thing, do unity and multiplicity somehow merge in one subject? The author systematically eliminates both possibilities, showing that either multiplicity persists in the parts that remain, or a new subject entirely replaces the old one. This logical groundwork protects later theological discussions from conceptual confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without overanalyzing. Let the author's main claim sink in.
Note the phrase 'فلا اتحاد في الموضوع' which is the key conclusion.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases for when multiple things unite. Identify each case and notice what happens to the subjects of multiplicity and unity.
First case: the original things remain. Second case: they vanish and a new thing appears.
3
Ask why this argument matters
Reflect on why a theologian would need to establish that unity and multiplicity cannot share a subject. This prevents contradictions in discussions about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موضوع الكثرة إنما يتوهم إذا اجتمعت أشياء متعددة، بحيث يحصل منها شيء واحد، فحينئذ نقول: إن كانت تلك الأشياء باقية بأعيانها، وقد تركب منها شيء واحد، فالكثرة باقية في موضوعها الذي هو تلك الأشياء التي صارت أجزاء للمركب. والوحدة عارضة للمجموع من حيث هو مجموع، فلا اتحاد في الموضوع، ولا إبطال للكثرة. وإن زالت تلك الأشياء التي كانت معروضة للكثرة وحصل شيء آخر هو معروض للوحدة، فلا اتحاد في الموضوع أيضاً، لأن موضوع الكثرة هو ذلك الزائل، وموضوع الوحدة هو هذا الحادث. وقس على ذلك طريان الكثرة على موضوع الوحدة.
The subject of multiplicity is only imagined when multiple things come together such that a single thing results from them. Then we say: if those things remain in their essences and a single thing has been composed from them, then multiplicity remains in its subject which is those things that became parts of the composite. Unity is accidental to the aggregate as an aggregate, so there is no union in the subject, nor nullification of multiplicity. And if those things that were the locus of multiplicity have vanished and another thing occurs which is the locus of unity, then also there is no union in the subject, because the subject of multiplicity is that which vanished, and the subject of unity is this newly occurring thing. And analogize to that the occurrence of multiplicity upon the subject of unity.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument. It begins by defining when the notion of multiplicity arises: when multiple things combine to form a single thing. The author then presents two cases. First, if the original things remain in their essence after composition, then multiplicity persists in those things as parts of the whole. Unity is only an accident of the aggregate, not inherent in the parts. Thus, the multiplicity is not destroyed, and there is no true union of unity and multiplicity in one subject. Second, if the original things vanish and a new single thing arises, then the subject of multiplicity is gone and a new subject for unity exists. Again, there is no union in one subject. The same reasoning applies when multiplicity occurs upon a subject of unity. The purpose is to show that unity and multiplicity cannot coexist in the same subject, refuting any notion of essential opposition.

Key Terms
كثرة (multiplicity)
وحدة (unity)
موضوع (subject)
عارضة (accidental)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That when multiple things become one, unity and multiplicity somehow merge into a single substance.
Correction: The passage shows that even after composition, unity and multiplicity remain separate: either multiplicity persists in the parts, or a new subject entirely replaces the old one. They never occupy the same subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to multiplicity when multiple things combine and remain in their essences?
A) Multiplicity is nullified.
B) Multiplicity persists in the parts as subject.
C) A new subject arises.
D) Unity becomes essential.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity cannot share a subject; in every combination scenario, they remain distinct in their loci.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel pulled between many desires and commitments, do you ever try to force them into a single identity? Reflect on how the lesson's principle that unity and multiplicity cannot share a subject might help you discern whether your multiple roles are genuinely integrated or just forced together. What would it take for your multiplicity to truly submit to a single purpose?
?
The author says that when things remain in their essences, unity is only accidental to the aggregate. In your own life, do you ever claim a unity (like 'I am a person of integrity') while keeping parts of yourself that are at odds? What would it mean for those parts to vanish so a genuine unity can emerge?
?
Consider a situation where you have tried to achieve unity within yourself (e.g., one focus, one priority) but still experience inner multiplicity. According to this passage, what must happen for those multiple things to truly become one? Are you willing to let some aspects of yourself vanish to make room for a new unified subject?

Multiplicity Composed of Units: No Essential Opposition
Author states the realized view: multiplicity is composed of units, so no essential opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why multiplicity and unity are not essentially opposed according to the realized view of the scholars.

Core Idea
Multiplicity is just units gathered, so it cannot oppose unity.

In this passage, the author presents the definitive scholarly position (tahqeeq) on the relationship between multiplicity and unity. The key insight is that multiplicity is not a separate reality but simply a composition of units. This understanding dissolves any notion of essential opposition between the two concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: definition, example, consequence, conclusion.
The author uses the example of the number two to illustrate the point.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms 'تحقيق' (realized view), 'ملتئمة' (composed), 'وحدات' (units), 'لازم' (necessary consequence), and 'تقابل بالذات' (essential opposition). Understand how each contributes to the argument.
These five terms are the building blocks of this passage.
3
Trace the argument
Follow the argument step by step: multiplicity is composed of units (definition), exemplified by two being two units, divisibility is a necessary consequence outside the essence, so there is no essential opposition.
Note the careful distinction between essence and necessary consequence.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common mistake of thinking multiplicity and unity are opposites. The passage shows that since multiplicity is built from units, they cannot be opposites.
This refutes any view that posits a fundamental conflict between the two.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and then try to explain the argument in your own words.
The key takeaway is that multiplicity and unity coexist without opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم التحقيق المفهوم من كلامهم هو أن الكثرة ملتئمة من الوحدات فإن حقيقة الاثنين مثلا وحدتان فليس هناك شيء يعتبر فيها سوى الوحدتين. وأما الانقسام فلازم لتلك الحقيقة خارج عنها، وإذا كان حقيقة الكثرة مركبة من حقيقة الوحدة لم يكن بين حقيقتيهما تقابل بالذات أصلاً.
Then the realized view understood from their statements is that multiplicity is composed of units. For example, the reality of two is two units; there is nothing considered in it except the two units. As for divisibility, it is a necessary consequence of that reality, external to it. And since the reality of multiplicity is composed of the reality of unity, there is no essential opposition between the two realities at all.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the positive doctrine (tahqeeq) of the scholars. It states that multiplicity is not a new reality over and above units; it is simply a collection of units. For example, the number two is nothing but two units. Division, which might seem inherent in multiplicity, is actually a necessary consequence (lazim) that is external to the essence. The key conclusion: since multiplicity is composed of unity, there cannot be any essential opposition (taqabul bil-dhat) between the two. This refutes the idea that they are opposites like black and white.

Key Terms
تحقيق (realized view)
ملتئمة (composed)
وحدات (units)
لازم (necessary consequence)
تقابل بالذات (essential opposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiplicity and unity are opposites, like existence and non-existence.
Correction: The passage shows that multiplicity is composed of units, so it cannot be essentially opposed to unity. They are compatible realities.

Checkpoint

A) A new attribute distinct from units.
B) A composite of units.
C) Divisible by nature.
D) Opposite to unity.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity is simply a composition of units, and since it is built from unity, there is no essential opposition between them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Or can you separate the act from your essence and trust that Allah can compose your scattered efforts into unity with Him? Confess to Allah one specific area where you feel divided and ask Him to unite your heart.

Objection on the Two Scenarios: Unity and Multiplicity as Accidental
Objection against the commentator's interpretation of two scenarios, with reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector challenges the commentator's interpretation of 'remaining in their essences' and how the reply defends the possibility of unity and multiplicity being successive accidents.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity can be successive accidents; they do not define individual identity.

In this passage, an objection is raised against the commentator's explanation of two scenarios involving the persistence of things. The objector presents two possible interpretations of 'remaining in their essences' and refutes both, while the reply argues that unity and multiplicity are not essential individuators but can be successive accidents. This debate highlights the philosophical underpinnings of identity and change in kalam and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its two interpretations
The objector considers two meanings of 'remaining in their essences': remaining in multiplicity, and remaining in individuality. Read the Arabic passage and its translation to identify these two cases.
The phrase 'بأعيانها' is key: it can mean 'in their essences' or 'in their individual identities'.
2
Analyze the refutation of the first interpretation
If the commentator meant remaining in multiplicity, the objector argues that removal of multiplicity does not entail annihilation. The example of water in jugs illustrates this: collecting waters into one jug does not destroy them.
Notice the objector uses a thought experiment to show that multiplicity is not essential to existence.
3
Analyze the refutation of the second interpretation
If the commentator meant remaining in individuality, the objector denies the implication that individuality vanishes. Instead, the thing remains an individual but is now qualified by unity. The objector asserts that unity and multiplicity are not individuators.
The objector introduces Plato's doctrine of conjunction and disjunction as successive accidents.
4
Understand the reply's alternative
The reply argues that the commentator's reliance on hylomorphism (matter and form) is not accepted by all. The same matter can be subject to both multiplicity and unity at different times via different forms. This defends the possibility of a common subject.
The reply concludes that matter can be truly qualified by unity or multiplicity without being essentially one or many.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (إن كانت الأشياء باقية بأعيانها إلخ) قيل عليه: إن أراد به أن تلك الأشياء باقية بتعددها على ما ينبئ عنه لفظ بأعيانها، فنختار أنها غير باقية بتعددها، ولم يزل أيضاً فإن زوال الكثرة عن شيء لا يقتضي زوال وجوده، وإلا لكان جمع المياه التي في كيزان متعددة في كوز واحد، إعداماً لها بالكلية، وإيجاداً لماء آخر من كتم العدم والضرورة قاضية ببطلانه. وإن أراد أنها باقية بشخصها فنمنع الملازمة ونقول: تلك الأشياء التي كانت واحدة بالشخص باقية بشخصها إلا أنها زالت عنها تلك الكثرة، وعرضت لها وحدة حقيقية. والحاصل: أنا لا نسلم أن الوحدة والكثرة من المشخصات، حتى يزول بزوال أحدهما وطريان الآخر وجود موضوعهما، لم يجوز أن يكونا من العوارض المتعاقبة، كما هو مذهب أفلاطون في الاتصال والانفصال. وما ذكره الشارح مبني على الهيولى والصورة حتى يلزم انعدام الصورة الجسمية التي هي معروضة للكثرة في الكيزان، إذا جعل تلك المياه في كوز واحد، وحصول صورة واحدة متصلة في حد ذاته لا مفصل فيها أصلاً، فلا تقوم حجة على نفاتهما ومنهم المصنف، كما. وأيضاً ما سيجيء ذكره إنما يدل على أن الصورة الجسمية الواحدة بالشخص لا يمكن أن تكون موضوعاً للوحدة والكثرة، ولا تقوم برهاناً على أن أمراً واحداً بالشخص لا يمكن أن يكون موضوعاً لهما. لم لا يجوز أن يكون موضوعهما هيولى الماء الباقية بعينها في الحالين وقد اتصف في إحداهما بالكثرة اتصافاً حقيقياً، ولو بواسطة الصورة، وفي الأخرى بالوحدة ولو بواسطتها أيضاً، وذلك كاف في اتحادهما محلاً. وما يقال من أن الهيولى ليست في حد ذاتها واحدة، ولا كثيرة، فمعناه أن الاتصاف بأحدهما ليس مقتضى ذاتها لا أنها ليست موصوفة بأحدهما حقيقة، فإن ذلك ممنوع.
His statement: 'If those things remain in their essences etc.' It was objected to him: If he means by that that those things remain in their multiplicity, as the phrase 'in their essences' indicates, then we choose that they do not remain in their multiplicity, and they have not vanished either, because the removal of multiplicity from a thing does not entail the removal of its existence, otherwise the collection of waters in multiple jugs into one jug would be their complete annihilation and the creation of another water from the concealment of non-existence, and necessity judges its falsity. And if he means that they remain in their individuality, then we deny the implication and say: those things that were one in individuality remain in their individuality, but that multiplicity has been removed from them and a true unity has occurred to them. The conclusion: we do not accept that unity and multiplicity are among the individuators, such that the existence of their subject vanishes with the removal of one and the occurrence of the other; they may be among the successive accidents, as is Plato's doctrine regarding conjunction and disjunction. What the commentator mentioned is based on hylomorphism such that it would entail the annihilation of the corporeal form which is the locus of multiplicity in the jugs when those waters are placed in one jug, and the occurrence of a single continuous form in itself with no division at all, so it does not constitute a proof against its deniers, including the author. Also, what will be mentioned later only indicates that a single corporeal form in individuality cannot be a subject for unity and multiplicity, but it does not prove that a single thing in individuality cannot be a subject for them. Why is it not possible that their subject is the matter of the water remaining itself in both states, having been qualified by multiplicity in one state, truly, even if through the form, and in the other by unity, also through it? That suffices for their union in locus. And what is said that matter is in itself neither one nor many means that the qualification by one of them is not a requirement of its essence, not that it is not truly qualified by one of them, for that is denied.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector attacks the commentator's interpretation of the two scenarios. He presents two possible meanings of 'remaining in their essences' and refutes both. First, if 'remaining in their essences' means remaining as multiple, then we deny that they remain multiple; the removal of multiplicity does not annihilate the thing. The example of water in jugs shows that collecting water does not annihilate the water's existence. Second, if it means remaining as individuals, then we deny that multiplicity vanishes; the individuals remain but are now qualified by unity. The answer then argues that unity and multiplicity are not individuators; they can be successive accidents (Plato's doctrine). The commentator's reliance on hylomorphism (matter and form) is not accepted by all, as it would entail that the same matter can be the subject of both multiplicity and unity at different times via different forms. The passage concludes by affirming that matter can be the common subject.

Key Terms
بأعيانها (in their essences)
بتعددها (in their multiplicity)
بشخصها (in their individuality)
مشخصات (individuators)
عوارض متعاقبة (successive accidents)
هيولى (matter/hyle)
صورة (form)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that unity and multiplicity are essential attributes that define the identity of a thing, so that a change from many to one would change the thing's identity.
Correction: The passage argues that unity and multiplicity are not 'individuators' (مشخصات) but can be successive accidents. The thing can remain the same individual while its unity or multiplicity changes, as with water combined from multiple jugs.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what is the second interpretation of 'remaining in their essences'?
A) Remaining in multiplicity
B) Remaining in individuality
C) Remaining in form
D) Remaining in accident

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are accidental attributes, not essential individuators; the same subject can be qualified by both at different times.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own identity, are there attributes you treat as 'individuators' — things you believe define you so deeply that if they changed, you would no longer be 'you'? What would it mean for your faith to learn that even unity and multiplicity are accidental, not essential to your existence before Allah?
?
The water in the jugs lost its multiplicity but did not cease to exist. How does this passage's teaching — that the subject remains even when accidents change — speak to that experience?
?

Why Unity and Multiplicity Are Not Opposites
Sanusi refutes the claim that unity and multiplicity are opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the kalam tradition rejects the claim that unity (wahda) and multiplicity (kathra) are genuine opposites (diddayn), and what conditions are required for true opposition.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are not opposites—true opposition requires more than mutual exclusion in individuals.

In kalam, the relationship between unity and multiplicity is often misunderstood. Some claim they are opposites like black and white. But as al-Sanusi explains, opposition has specific logical conditions—lack of a common subject and discussion of essences. This passage clarifies the correct stance and refutes the error of those who overextend the concept of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the author's refutation structure.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two refutation reasons
Look for the two reasons the author gives against tadadd: lack of union in subject and discussion of essences, not individuals.
3
Understand the pretender's view
The author mentions a pretender who says they are opposites. Note how the author dismisses this by pointing out they don't meet the conditions of opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا هو مقصد القوم في المقام، لا أن بين مفهومي تعريفيهما تقابلاً بالذات أو بالعرض، والقول بأن التقابل بين الكثرة والوحدة الطارئة إحداهما على الأخرى المبطلة إياها تقابل التضاد باطل؛ لما عرفت من عدم الاتحاد في الموضوع ولأن الكلام في حقيقتيهما لا في أفرادهما. والوحدة المذكورة أعني الوحدة الطارئة على موضوع الكثرة جزء من كثرة مركبة من وحدات كل واحدة منها طارئة على موضوع كثرة مخصوصة، ومبطلة إياها، فلا تكون ذات هذه الوحدة مقابلة لماهية الكثرة. ومن المتصلفين من قال: الوحدة والكثرة ضدان؛ إذ نحن لا نوجب بين الضدين غاية الخلاف، مع أن الوحدة والكثرة مما يتباعدان جداً، ولا نوجب أيضاً امتناع تقوم أحد الضدين بالآخر، مع أن الوحدة
This is the intention of the people in this context: not that there is between the concepts of their definitions an opposition in essence or accident. The statement that the opposition between multiplicity and the unity that occurs upon it, the one nullifying the other, is an opposition of contradiction is false; because of what you have known of the lack of union in subject, and because the discussion is about their essences, not their individuals. And the mentioned unity, I mean the unity that occurs upon the subject of multiplicity, is part of a multiplicity composed of units, each of which occurs upon a subject of a specific multiplicity and nullifies it; so this unity itself is not opposite to the essence of multiplicity. And among the pretenders is he who said: Unity and multiplicity are two opposites; for we do not require between two opposites extreme difference, even though unity and multiplicity are very far apart; nor do we require the impossibility of one opposite being constitutive of the other, even though unity...
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that unity and multiplicity are two opposites. First, it states the correct intention: there is no essential or accidental opposition between the concepts. Then it refutes the claim that they are opposites of contradiction (tadadd). The reasons: no union of subject, and the discussion is about essences. Moreover, the unity that occurs on a subject of multiplicity is itself part of a higher-order multiplicity, so it cannot be opposite to the essence of multiplicity. The author then mentions the opinion of some pretentious people who say they are opposites, but they do not apply the proper conditions of opposites (such as extreme difference or mutual exclusivity of constitution). The passage is cut off at 'even though unity' but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
تقابل (opposition)
تضاد (contradiction/contrariety)
مقصد (intention)
متصلّف (pretender)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because unity and multiplicity cannot coexist in the same subject, they must be opposites (diddayn).
Correction: Opposition requires more conditions: a common subject or discussion of essences. Unity and multiplicity do not meet these conditions.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the claim of opposition (tadadd) is false?
A) Because multiplicity and unity are the same.
B) Because there is no union in subject and the discussion is about essences.
C) Because they are both accidental.
D) Because the concept of opposition does not apply.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are not opposites because they lack a common subject and the discussion is about their essences, not their instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed two concepts must be opposites simply because they seem mutually exclusive? How does this lesson challenge you to think more carefully about the nature of Allah's unity and the apparent multiplicity in creation? What false opposition might you need to abandon today to deepen your Tawhid?
?
The pretender claimed knowledge but did not meet the conditions of true opposition. Are there areas in your own faith where you hold opinions without examining their logical foundations? What is one belief you hold about Allah's attributes that you have not scrutinized closely? Will you commit to investigating it this week?

The Ranks of Numbers Are Different in Essence
Definition and proof that ranks of numbers are distinct species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ranks of numbers are considered distinct species in kalam, differing in essence rather than by convention.

Core Idea
Numbers are not just quantities; each rank has a unique essence.

After discussing the nature of multiplicity, the author turns to the specific ranks of numbers. He argues that these ranks are not merely different in degree but are fundamentally different kinds of beings. This passage introduces the rational basis for distinguishing prime numbers from composite ones and others, using the kalam method of analyzing concomitants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Then read the full translation. Let the author's own words establish the claim before you analyze it.
Notice the use of technical terms like 'mahiyya' and 'lawazim'.
2
Identify the main thesis
The passage opens by declaring that the ranks of numbers are distinct species differing in essence. Mark this statement and see how it is supported.
3
Understand the proof: difference in concomitants
The author argues that the ranks are distinguished by specific forms, and this is shown by their different necessary properties (concomitants). Ask yourself why difference in properties implies difference in essence.
In kalam, if two things have different necessary properties, they cannot be the same in essence.
4
Examine the examples
The passage lists four concomitants: being prime (samm), logical (mantiq), composite (tarkib), and primal (awwaliyya). For each, note the definition given in the commentary.
The term 'logical' here refers to perfect numbers or products of a number multiplied by itself.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters for theology and philosophy. If numbers are essentially different, then mathematical truths are grounded in real essences, not just conventions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع: مراتب الأعداد ]
مراتب الأعداد أنواع متخالفة بالماهية فإنها وإن كانت متشاركة في كونها كثرة لكنها متمايزة بخصوصيات هي صورها النوعية وذلك لاختلافها باللوازم، كالصمم والمنطقية والتركيب والأولية واختلاف اللوازم يدل على اختلاف
قوله : ( كالصمم والمنطقية إلخ) والأولية هي كون العدد بحيث لا يعده إلا الواحد، كالثلاثة والخمسة والسبعة وغيرها والتركيب كونه بحيث يعده غير الواحد أيضاً، كالأربعة والثمانية والتسعة. والمنطق قد يراد به المحذور أعني ما يكون حاصلاً من ضرب عدد في نفسه
The Fourth Purpose: The Ranks of Numbers. The ranks of numbers are species differing in essence; for although they share in being multiplicity, they are distinguished by particularities which are their specific forms, and that is due to their difference in concomitants, such as being prime, logical, composite, and primal. And difference in concomitants indicates difference [in essence]. His saying: 'such as being prime, logical, etc.' And primal (al-awwaliyya) is that the number is such that only one counts it, like three, five, seven, etc. And composite (al-tarkib) is that it is such that other than one also counts it, like four, eight, and nine. And 'logical' (al-mantiq) may refer to the product, meaning what results from multiplying a number by itself.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the ranks of numbers. The author states that the ranks of numbers (e.g., prime, composite, etc.) are different species (anwa') that differ in essence (māhiyya). Although they share the common property of being multiplicity (kathra), they are distinguished by particularities (khuṣūṣiyyāt) that serve as their specific forms (ṣuwar naw'iyya). The proof of essential difference is that they have different concomitants (lawāzim), i.e., properties that necessarily follow from each rank. The author then gives examples: being prime (ṣamm) means the number is only divisible by one (like 3, 5, 7); being composite (tarkīb) means it is divisible by numbers other than one (like 4, 8, 9); and 'logical' (mantiq) here refers to a perfect number or the product of a number multiplied by itself, though the text is cut short. The commentator clarifies these terms briefly.

مراتب الأعداد

الكثرة
الصور النوعية
اللوازم
الصمم
المنطقية
التركيب
الأولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ranks of numbers are merely conventional labels for the same underlying quantity. For example, 'prime' and 'composite' are just different descriptions of the same number being measured differently.
Correction: The passage asserts that ranks are different in essence (mahiyya), not just by convention. The proof is that they have different necessary properties (concomitants). If two ranks had the same essence, they would have the same necessary properties; but prime and composite numbers have different properties (e.g., divisibility), so they must differ essentially.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for differentiating the ranks of numbers?
A) Their being measured by different units.
B) Their specific forms indicated by different concomitants.
C) Their magnitude or size.
D) Their being either even or odd.

Key Takeaway
The ranks of numbers are distinct species in kalam because they have different necessary properties, showing they differ in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own actions, do you assume that they are all essentially the same because they come from you, or do you examine their 'concomitants'—their necessary properties—to see if some belong to a different species? Which actions in your life might actually be 'composite' (impure) rather than 'prime' (pure)?
?
The passage teaches that sharing a common property (multiplicity) does not mean things are the same in essence. Consider your relationships: you and another person may both be 'human', but what are the specific forms that distinguish your essence from theirs? How can you honor those differences rather than assume uniformity?
?
If difference in necessary properties proves essential difference, what does that mean for your own character? Are your habits and traits merely accidental, or do they reveal a deeper spiritual species? Pray that Allah shows you the true nature of your soul as it really is.

Ranks of Numbers as Essentially Distinct Types
Al-Iji defines the ranks of numbers as essentially different types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji defines the ranks of numbers as essentially distinct types based on their specific necessary properties.

Core Idea
Numbers are not just quantities; each rank is a unique essence defined by its necessary properties.

Al-Iji begins by asserting that the ranks of numbers are distinct types differing in essence. While all numbers share the common concept of multiplicity, they are differentiated by specific characteristics that constitute their specific forms. These characteristics are revealed through their necessary properties (لوازم), such as being prime or composite.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation to get the gist.
Notice the structure: definition, explanation, evidence, example.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms 'مراتب الأعداد', 'كثرة', 'صورة نوعية', 'لوازم', and the listed properties. Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the logical inference
Al-Iji argues that difference in necessary properties (لوازم) implies difference in the subjects (ملزومات). Grasp this logical step.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the example of ten
Consider how ten shares multiplicity but is distinguished by its specific kind of multiplicity. Why does that matter?
It illustrates the principle in a concrete case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مراتب الأعداد أنواع متخالفة بالماهية فإنها وإن كانت متشاركة في كونها كثرة لكنها متمايزة بخصوصيات هي صورها النوعية وذلك لاختلافها باللوازم كالصمم والمنطقية والتركيب والأولية واختلاف اللوازم يدل على اختلاف الملزومات فالعشرة مثلاً تشارك ما عداها في أنها كثرة وتمتاز عنها بخصوصية كونها كثرة مخصوصة وهي مبدأ لوازمها
The ranks of numbers are types differing in essence. For although they share in being multiplicity, they are distinguished by specific characteristics that are their specific forms (صور نوعية), and that is due to their differing in necessary properties (لوازم), such as the prime (صمم), the square (منطقية), the composite (تركيب), and the primary (أولية). And the difference in necessary properties indicates the difference in the subjects (ملزومات). So ten, for example, shares with others in being multiplicity, and is distinguished from them by the specific characteristic of being a specific multiplicity, and it is the source of its necessary properties.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines the ranks of numbers (مراتب الأعداد) as essentially different types (أنواع متخالفة بالماهية). It explains that while all numbers share the common property of being a multiplicity (كثرة), each rank is distinguished by specific characteristics that constitute its specific form (صورة نوعية). These specific characteristics are identified by their necessary properties (لوازم), which are attributes necessarily following from the essence. The passage lists examples: الصمم (prime, i.e., divisible only by one and itself), المنطقية (square, i.e., the product of a number multiplied by itself), التركيب (composite, divisible by numbers other than one and itself), and الأولية (primary, a number that only the number one counts?). The author argues that since these properties differ, the underlying subjects (ملزومات) must also differ essentially. The example of ten illustrates this: ten shares multiplicity with other numbers but is distinguished by its specific kind of multiplicity, which grounds its unique necessary properties.

Numbers Are Composed of Their Units Without a Formal Part
Al-Jurjani argues that numbers are composed solely of their units without a formal part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position that numbers are directly constituted by their units without any additional formal part, and how this relates to definitions and properties.

Core Idea
A number is nothing but the sum of its units—no hidden structure needed.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a subtle metaphysical question about the composition of numbers. Some thinkers argued that numbers require both material parts (units) and a formal part that unites them. Al-Jurjani rejects this, asserting that the number is simply the sum of its units, and that all properties arise from that sum alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its flow. Notice the key terms like تقوم, وحدات, جزء صوري.
2
Understand the main claim
Identify the initial claim that each number is constituted by its units, with no other parts. Then note the opposing view and its refutation.
3
Grasp the correct view
Al-Jurjani says the numerical composite is exactly the sum of its units. Understand why this is sufficient for properties and why no accidental configuration is needed.
4
Reflect on the definition example
Consider the example of 'ten is five and five' as a description (رسم) not an essential definition. This illustrates the point about internal numbers being necessary concomitants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقوم كل (عدد) من أنواع الأعداد بوحداته التي مبلغ جملتها ذلك النوع من العدد وكل واحدة من تلك الوحدات جزء لماهيته وليس لها جزء سوى الوحدات فما يقال من أن وحدات كل عدد أجزاء مادية له فلا بد هناك من جزء صوري كلام ظاهري بل الصواب أن المركب له العددي هو عين مجموع وحداته وهذا المجموع المخصوص منشأ الخواص واللوازم العددية وأنه لا حاجة في ذلك إلى اعتبار هيئة عارضة للوحدات بعد اجتماعها بل الأعداد التي فيه لازمة له فلو عرفت كل عدد بما فيه كما يقال العشرة خمسة وخمسة كان رسماً له
And each (number) from the types of numbers is constituted by its units, the sum of which amounts to that type of number, and each one of those units is a part of its essence, and it has no part other than the units. So what is said that the units of each number are its material parts and so there must be a formal part is superficial talk. Rather, the correct view is that the numerical composite is exactly the sum of its units, and this specific sum is the source of the properties and numerical necessary properties, and there is no need in that for considering an accidental configuration for the units after their coming together. Rather, the numbers that are in it are necessary for it, so if you defined each number by what is in it, as is said: ten is five and five, that would be a description (رسم).
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage discusses the constitution (تقوم) of numbers. It begins by stating that each number is constituted by its units (وحدات) such that the total of those units equals that specific number, and each unit is an integral part of the number's essence, with no other parts besides the units. It then refutes a claim: some say that the units are only material parts (أجزاء مادية) and therefore a formal part (جزء صوري) is needed to unite them into a number. The passage dismisses this as superficial talk (كلام ظاهري). Instead, the correct view (الصواب) is that the numerical composite (المركب العددي) is exactly (عين) the sum of its units. This specific sum itself is the source (منشأ) of all the properties (خواص) and necessary properties (لوازم عددية) of that number. There is no need to posit an additional accidental configuration (هيئة عارضة) imposed on the units after they are combined. Finally, it clarifies that any other numbers contained within a given number (e.g., five and five in ten) are not constituent parts but are necessary concomitants (لازمة) of that number. Defining a number by what it contains (e.g., 'ten is five and five') yields only a definition by description (رسم), not an essential definition (حد).

هيئة عارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Numbers require both material parts (units) and a formal part that unites them into a single number.
Correction: The correct view is that the number is exactly the sum of its units; no formal part is needed because the specific sum itself is sufficient for the number's identity and properties.

Checkpoint

A) It consists of material units and a formal part.

C) It consists of the numbers contained within it.
D) It requires an accidental configuration added to the units.

Key Takeaway
Numbers are sufficiently defined by their units; no formal part is needed, and properties arise from the specific sum alone.

The Nature of Ten: Composed of Units Only
Aristotle defines the reality of ten and presents a proof from conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's argument that the number ten is essentially composed of ten units directly, not through intermediate numbers.

Core Idea
Ten is ten units at once, not a combination of other numbers.

Aristotle begins by stating the nature of ten as a collection of units. He then argues that if ten were composed of other numbers, we would not be able to conceive it without thinking of those numbers. Since we can conceive ten in its essence while ignoring those numbers, they are not part of its essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style—every phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage explicitly states that ten is a total of units and that it is not composed of other numbers like three and seven. Identify this as Aristotle's thesis.
3
Understand the argument from conception
The proof relies on the possibility of conceiving ten in its essence (bi-kunnihi) while being heedless of other numbers. This shows that those numbers are not essential to ten.
Conceiving in essence means grasping the thing through its essential attributes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that none of those intermediate numbers are part of the reality of ten; ten is directly composed of its units.
This sets the stage for the more formal proof in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع: مراتب الأعداد
(مجموع وحدات مبلغها ذلك المذكور الذي هو العشرة أي حقيقة العشرة هي عشر وحدات مرة واحدة . ( وقال أرسطو : إنها ) أي العشرة (ليست ثلاثة وسبعة ولا أربعة وستة)، وغير ذلك من الأعداد التي يتوهم تركبها منها؛ لإمكان تصور العشرة ) بكنهها (مع الغفلة عن هذه الأعداد ) ، فإنك إذا تصورت حقيقة كل واحدة من وحداتها من غير شعور بخصوصيات الأعداد المندرجة تحتها، فقد تصورت حقيقة العشرة بلا شبهة فلا يكون شيء من تلك الأعداد داخلا في حقيقتها . (بل عشرة مرة واحدة، وربما يستدل على ذلك بأن تركب العشرة من الاثنين والثمانية ليس أولى من تركبها من الثلاثة والسبعة أو الأربعة والستة، أو الخمسة والخمسة، فإن تركبت من بعضها لزم الترجيح بلا ،مرجح وإن تركبت من الكل لزم استغناء الشيء عما هو ذاتي له؛ لأن كل واحد منها كاف في تقويمها فيستغني عما عداه، فإن قلت: جاز أن يكون كل واحد منها مقوماً لها باعتبار القدر المشترك بين جميعها، إذ لا مدخل في تقويمها لخصوصياتها . قلت القدر المشترك بينها : الذي يفي بحقيقة العشرة هو الوحدات فما ذكرته اعتراف بالمطلوب. نعم ربما ينقض الدليل بأن تركبها من الوحدات أيضاً ليس أولى من تركبها من تلك الأعداد، فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح؛ لأن اشتمال تلك الأعداد على الوحدات لا يفيد ترجيحاً. ويجاب بأنه لما كفت الوحدات في تحصيل العشرة لم يكن لخصوصيات الأعداد المندرجة فيها مدخل في تحصلها، وهذا بالحقيقة رجوع إلى الاستدلال الأول .

به
قوله : ( فإنك إذا تصورت إلخ يعني تصور الشيء بالكنه إنما يكون بتصور ذاتياته بالكنه، فإذا تصور حقيقة كل واحدة من الوحدات المخصوصة بمرتبة من المراتب كانت تلك المرتبة متصورة بالكنه مع الغفلة عن جميع المراتب التي فيها .
قوله : ( لأن اشتمال إلخ) دفع لما قيل من أن تركبها من الوحدات أولى، لأنه لازم على كل حال لاشتمال تلك الأعداد عليها بأنه لا يفيد الترجيح، وإلا لزم أن يكون تركب السرير من العناصر أولى من تركبه من الخشب المخصوصة لاشتمالها عليها .
•
قوله : ( وهذا بالحقيقة إلخ إذ لا فرق بينهما إلا بأن الأول استدلال بكفايتها في التعقل بالكنه، وهذا بكفايتها في حصول نفسها. وقد يجاب عن النقض بأنه لما ظهر بطلان التقوم بالأعداد بقسميه تعين التركب من الوحدات إذ لا ثالث وليس بشيء، لأن بطلان التقوم بالأعداد إنما يظهر إذا لم يكن دليله منقوضاً .
قوله : (من غير شعور إلخ) ربما يوجه كلام أرسطو بأن الستة مثلاً وحدات ست، بشرط عدم انضمام الأخرى فعند الانضمام زالت الستة لزوال ،شرطها وبه يظهر سر عدم التركيب من الأعداد، وسر إمكان التعقل بدون تلك الأعداد مع أن تلك الأعداد عين الوحدات .
The Fourth Observatory - Fourth Objective: The Ranks of Numbers. (It is a total of units whose sum is that mentioned number which is ten, i.e., the reality of ten is ten units at once.) (And Aristotle said: It (i.e., ten) is not three and seven, nor four and six), and other numbers from which one might imagine it composed; because it is possible to conceive ten in its essence while being heedless of these numbers. For if you conceive the reality of each one of its units without awareness of the particularities of the numbers subsumed under it, then you have indeed conceived the reality of ten without doubt, so none of those numbers are part of its reality. (Rather, ten is ten at once. And perhaps one may prove that by the fact that the composition of ten from two and eight is not more preferable than its composition from three and seven, or four and six, or five and five. For if it were composed from some of them, then a preference without a preponderant would be necessary, and if it were composed from all, then a thing would be independent of what is essential to it; because each one of them is sufficient in constituting it, so it would be independent of others. If you say: It is possible that each one of them constitutes it with respect to the common factor among all of them, since their particularities have no role in constituting it. I say: The common factor among them that suffices for the reality of ten is the units, so what you mentioned is an admission of the sought. Yes, the proof could be refuted by saying that its composition from units also is not more preferable than its composition from those numbers, leading to preference without a preponderant; because the inclusion of those numbers of units does not provide preference. And it is answered that since the units suffice in obtaining ten, the particularities of the numbers subsumed under it have no role in its actualization, and this is in reality a return to the first proof.

By it.
His saying: 'For if you conceive, etc.' means that conceiving a thing in its essence is only by conceiving its essential attributes in their essence, so if you conceive the reality of each one of the specific units in a particular rank, then that rank is conceived in its essence while being heedless of all the ranks in it.
His saying: 'Because the inclusion, etc.' is a refutation of what was said: that its composition from units is more preferable, because it is necessary in every case due to those numbers including them, [but] that it does not provide preference; otherwise it would follow that the composition of a bed from elements is more preferable than its composition from specific wood, because they include them.
His saying: 'And this is in reality, etc.' since there is no difference between them except that the first is a proof by their sufficiency in intellectual conception in essence, and this by their sufficiency in obtaining the thing itself. And it may be answered to the refutation that since the invalidity of constitution by numbers in both its divisions became clear, composition from units is determined since there is no third, and it is nothing, because the invalidity of constitution by numbers only appears if its proof is not refuted.
His saying: 'Without awareness, etc.' Perhaps Aristotle's statement may be interpreted that six, for example, is six units, with the condition of not joining the other, so upon joining, six ceases due to the cessation of its condition, and by this the secret of non-composition from numbers becomes apparent, as well as the secret of the possibility of conceiving without those numbers despite those numbers being identical to the units.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument about the nature of the number ten, attributed to Aristotle. It begins by stating that ten is a collection of units, its reality being ten units at once. The author then presents Aristotle's view: ten is not composed of other numbers like three and seven, etc. The proof relies on the possibility of conceiving ten in its essence without thinking of those numbers. If you conceive each unit individually without considering the particularities of the numbers that could be formed from them, you still conceive ten. Therefore, those numbers are not part of its essence. Then a further proof is given: if ten were composed from any specific pair (e.g., 2+8, 3+7, etc.), there would be no reason to prefer one pair over another, leading to a preference without a preponderant (tarjih bila murajjih), which is impossible. If it were composed from all pairs simultaneously, then each pair would be sufficient to constitute ten, making it independent of the others, which is absurd because a thing cannot be independent of what is essential to it. An objection is raised: perhaps each pair constitutes it with respect to the common factor (i.e., the units) and not their particularities. The reply is that the common factor is precisely the units, so this admits the sought conclusion. Then a counter-refutation is considered: the composition from units also suffers from the same problem of no preference. The answer is that since the units alone are sufficient, the particularities of the numbers have no role, so the original proof stands. The commentary elaborates each point, discussing the meaning of 'conceiving in essence' and giving analogies like the composition of a bed from elements versus specific wood.

Key Terms
العشرة
الوحدات
التصور بالكنه
الغفلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that ten is simply the sum of, say, 4+6, and thus essentially composed of those numbers.
Correction: The argument shows that such pairs are accidental; the essence of ten is the units themselves, independent of any particular decomposition.

Checkpoint
According to Aristotle's argument, why is ten not composed of three and seven?
A) Because three and seven do not add up to ten
B) Because we can conceive ten without thinking of three and seven
C) Because Aristotle said so
D) Because three and seven are prime numbers

Key Takeaway
Ten is essentially ten units, not a composite of other numbers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, which parts do you mistake for the whole? Are you defining yourself by the 'pairs' of your past – achievements, failures, relationships – while forgetting the 'units' that make you you before Allah?
?
The passage teaches that ten is fully conceived by its units alone, not by the sums that produce it. In the same way, are you trying to understand yourself by analyzing your components (your history, your traits) rather than standing simply as you are in the present moment?
?
If your essence is not composed of the 'sub-numbers' of your biography, what would it mean to see yourself as a whole, entirely dependent on Allah, without adding or subtracting anything? Can you conceive of yourself that way, even for a moment?

The Proof from Preference and Its Objections
The author presents a proof from impossibility of preference and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if ten were composed from specific number pairs, it would lead to an impossible preference without a preponderant, and see how objections are resolved.

Core Idea
No reason to prefer 2+8 over 3+7; therefore ten cannot be composed from either.

After establishing that ten is composed of units, the author strengthens the argument by considering how composition from intermediate numbers would lead to logical absurdities. If ten were composed from some specific pair, there would be no reason to choose that pair over others. If composed from all pairs simultaneously, each would be sufficient, making ten independent of its supposed constituents. This lesson unpacks these arguments and the subsequent objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about preference without preponderant
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice the structure: first a proof, then an objection, then a reply, then a counter-refutation, and a final answer.
This is a dialectical argument—like a debate on paper.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The proof considers two possibilities: either ten is composed from some particular pair, or from all pairs. Both lead to absurdities.
3
Analyze the objection and reply
The objection suggests that each pair constitutes ten through a common factor. The reply identifies that common factor as the units, which concedes the original point.
4
Examine the counter-refutation and final reply
The counter-refutation argues that even composition from units suffers the same preference problem. The final reply returns to the sufficiency of units, a form of the first proof.
This shows the importance of the earlier argument from conception.

(مجموع وحدات مبلغها ذلك المذكور الذي هو العشرة أي حقيقة العشرة هي عشر وحدات مرة واحدة . ( وقال أرسطو : إنها ) أي العشرة (ليست ثلاثة وسبعة ولا أربعة وستة)، وغير ذلك من الأعداد التي يتوهم تركبها منها؛ لإمكان تصور العشرة ) بكنهها (مع الغفلة عن هذه الأعداد ) ، فإنك إذا تصورت حقيقة كل واحدة من وحداتها من غير شعور بخصوصيات الأعداد المندرجة تحتها، فقد تصورت حقيقة العشرة بلا شبهة فلا يكون شيء من تلك الأعداد داخلا في حقيقتها . (بل عشرة مرة واحدة، وربما يستدل على ذلك بأن تركب العشرة من الاثنين والثمانية ليس أولى من تركبها من الثلاثة والسبعة أو الأربعة والستة، أو الخمسة والخمسة، فإن تركبت من بعضها لزم الترجيح بلا ،مرجح وإن تركبت من الكل لزم استغناء الشيء عما هو ذاتي له؛ لأن كل واحد منها كاف في تقويمها فيستغني عما عداه، فإن قلت: جاز أن يكون كل واحد منها مقوماً لها باعتبار القدر المشترك بين جميعها، إذ لا مدخل في تقويمها لخصوصياتها . قلت القدر المشترك بينها : الذي يفي بحقيقة العشرة هو الوحدات فما ذكرته اعتراف بالمطلوب. نعم ربما ينقض الدليل بأن تركبها من الوحدات أيضاً ليس أولى من تركبها من تلك الأعداد، فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح؛ لأن اشتمال تلك الأعداد على الوحدات لا يفيد ترجيحاً. ويجاب بأنه لما كفت الوحدات في تحصيل العشرة لم يكن لخصوصيات الأعداد المندرجة فيها مدخل في تحصلها، وهذا بالحقيقة رجوع إلى الاستدلال الأول .

به
قوله : ( فإنك إذا تصورت إلخ يعني تصور الشيء بالكنه إنما يكون بتصور ذاتياته بالكنه، فإذا تصور حقيقة كل واحدة من الوحدات المخصوصة بمرتبة من المراتب كانت تلك المرتبة متصورة بالكنه مع الغفلة عن جميع المراتب التي فيها .

قوله : ( وهذا بالحقيقة إلخ إذ لا فرق بينهما إلا بأن الأول استدلال بكفايتها في التعقل بالكنه، وهذا بكفايتها في حصول نفسها. وقد يجاب عن النقض بأنه لما ظهر بطلان التقوم بالأعداد بقسميه تعين التركب من الوحدات إذ لا ثالث وليس بشيء، لأن بطلان التقوم بالأعداد إنما يظهر إذا لم يكن دليله منقوضاً .
قوله : (من غير شعور إلخ) ربما يوجه كلام أرسطو بأن الستة مثلاً وحدات ست، بشرط عدم انضمام الأخرى فعند الانضمام زالت الستة لزوال ،شرطها وبه يظهر سر عدم التركيب من الأعداد، وسر إمكان التعقل بدون تلك الأعداد مع أن تلك الأعداد عين الوحدات .
The Fourth Observatory - Fourth Objective: The Ranks of Numbers. (It is a total of units whose sum is that mentioned number which is ten, i.e., the reality of ten is ten units at once.) (And Aristotle said: It (i.e., ten) is not three and seven, nor four and six), and other numbers from which one might imagine it composed; because it is possible to conceive ten in its essence while being heedless of these numbers. For if you conceive the reality of each one of its units without awareness of the particularities of the numbers subsumed under it, then you have indeed conceived the reality of ten without doubt, so none of those numbers are part of its reality. (Rather, ten is ten at once. And perhaps one may prove that by the fact that the composition of ten from two and eight is not more preferable than its composition from three and seven, or four and six, or five and five. For if it were composed from some of them, then a preference without a preponderant would be necessary, and if it were composed from all, then a thing would be independent of what is essential to it; because each one of them is sufficient in constituting it, so it would be independent of others. If you say: It is possible that each one of them constitutes it with respect to the common factor among all of them, since their particularities have no role in constituting it. I say: The common factor among them that suffices for the reality of ten is the units, so what you mentioned is an admission of the sought. Yes, the proof could be refuted by saying that its composition from units also is not more preferable than its composition from those numbers, leading to preference without a preponderant; because the inclusion of those numbers of units does not provide preference. And it is answered that since the units suffice in obtaining ten, the particularities of the numbers subsumed under it have no role in its actualization, and this is in reality a return to the first proof.

By it.
His saying: 'For if you conceive, etc.' means that conceiving a thing in its essence is only by conceiving its essential attributes in their essence, so if you conceive the reality of each one of the specific units in a particular rank, then that rank is conceived in its essence while being heedless of all the ranks in it.
His saying: 'Because the inclusion, etc.' is a refutation of what was said: that its composition from units is more preferable, because it is necessary in every case due to those numbers including them, [but] that it does not provide preference; otherwise it would follow that the composition of a bed from elements is more preferable than its composition from specific wood, because they include them.
His saying: 'And this is in reality, etc.' since there is no difference between them except that the first is a proof by their sufficiency in intellectual conception in essence, and this by their sufficiency in obtaining the thing itself. And it may be answered to the refutation that since the invalidity of constitution by numbers in both its divisions became clear, composition from units is determined since there is no third, and it is nothing, because the invalidity of constitution by numbers only appears if its proof is not refuted.
His saying: 'Without awareness, etc.' Perhaps Aristotle's statement may be interpreted that six, for example, is six units, with the condition of not joining the other, so upon joining, six ceases due to the cessation of its condition, and by this the secret of non-composition from numbers becomes apparent, as well as the secret of the possibility of conceiving without those numbers despite those numbers being identical to the units.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument about the nature of the number ten, attributed to Aristotle. It begins by stating that ten is a collection of units, its reality being ten units at once. The author then presents Aristotle's view: ten is not composed of other numbers like three and seven, etc. The proof relies on the possibility of conceiving ten in its essence without thinking of those numbers. If you conceive each unit individually without considering the particularities of the numbers that could be formed from them, you still conceive ten. Therefore, those numbers are not part of its essence. Then a further proof is given: if ten were composed from any specific pair (e.g., 2+8, 3+7, etc.), there would be no reason to prefer one pair over another, leading to a preference without a preponderant (tarjih bila murajjih), which is impossible. If it were composed from all pairs simultaneously, then each pair would be sufficient to constitute ten, making it independent of the others, which is absurd because a thing cannot be independent of what is essential to it. An objection is raised: perhaps each pair constitutes it with respect to the common factor (i.e., the units) and not their particularities. The reply is that the common factor is precisely the units, so this admits the sought conclusion. Then a counter-refutation is considered: the composition from units also suffers from the same problem of no preference. The answer is that since the units alone are sufficient, the particularities of the numbers have no role, so the original proof stands. The commentary elaborates each point, discussing the meaning of 'conceiving in essence' and giving analogies like the composition of a bed from elements versus specific wood.

Key Terms
الترجيح بلا مرجح
القدر المشترك
التقويم
الاستدلال الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because 2+8 equals ten, ten is truly composed of two and eight as essential parts.
Correction: The argument shows that no such pair is essential; they are accidental decompositions.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem if ten were composed from all possible pairs (e.g., 2+8, 3+7, etc.)?
A) Each pair would be insufficient to constitute ten
B) Ten would be independent of what is essential to it
C) There would be too many pairs
D) It would be impossible to add them

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of preference without a preponderant shows that ten cannot be composed from intermediate numbers, and the only consistent view is composition from units.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision between two equally plausible options, do you ever choose arbitrarily without a preponderant? What does that reveal about your understanding of rational choice?
?
This proof exposes the impossibility of arbitrary preference in numbers. In your own life, where have you allowed 'preference without a preponderant' to dictate your actions—perhaps in matters of faith or moral choices?
?
If the only consistent way to constitute ten is through units, what does that imply for the way you build your own beliefs? Are you relying on 'intermediate numbers'—secondary sources or opinions—rather than the foundational units of clear evidence?

The Divisions of the One: Personal and Non-Personal
The author defines the one and divides it into personal and non-personal, then explains the true one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main divisions of 'the one' (personal and non-personal) and the defining characteristics of the true one.

Core Idea
The one is either personal (indivisible into particulars) or non-personal (divisible); the true one is indivisible in every sense.

This lesson introduces a foundational classification in Islamic theology: the division of the concept 'one' into personal and non-personal. The author defines each type and then identifies the 'true one' as a subtype of the personal one that admits no division whatsoever. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the attribute of oneness to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow, then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning. Pay attention to the parentheses which indicate the author's definitions.
The passage begins with the heading and then lists the divisions.
2
Identify the two divisions of the one
Look for the two categories: one in person (al-wāḥid bi-shakhṣ) and one not in person (al-wāḥid lā bi-shakhṣ). Notice the criterion: whether the concept prevents or allows predication to many.
3
Distinguish the true one
Among the one in person, the author singles out the true one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī) which does not accept division at all. Consider the three types of indivisibility: quantitative, mental, and essence-individuation.
The passage ends with the condition 'if it has no concept other than that it is not...'
