The Fourth Observatory - Fifth Objective: On the Divisions of the One ]
The Fifth Objective: On the Divisions of the One. (On the divisions of the one, and it) i.e., the one (either does not divide into particulars such that its conception prevents it from being predicated of many) and it is the one in person (al-wāḥid bi-shakhṣ), or it divides into particulars such that its conception does not prevent sharing (and it is other than it) i.e., other than the one in person, and it is called one not in person. (And it) i.e., the one not in person (is many and has one aspect, so it is one in one respect) and many in another respect. (As for the one in person, if it does not accept division) into parts at all (then it is the true one), and it is the true one (if it has no concept other than that it is not...

His saying: 'On the divisions of the one' and by it the divisions of unity are known.
His saying: 'And it is many, and has one aspect of unity' because its attribution with multiplicity is hidden due to it being with respect to the particulars, and its attribution with unity is manifest due to it being with respect to itself, so the attribution with multiplicity was made the criterion for judgment out of care for it, and its attribution with unity was made a qualification for it, so what was said is repelled: that what appears of this judgment is superfluous. And the correct is to suffice with his saying: 'one in one respect, many in another respect'. And the meaning of his saying 'it is many' is that it necessarily follows that it is many, unlike the one in person, for which that does not necessarily follow.
His saying: 'one in one respect etc.' i.e., one in terms of concept, many in terms of individuals.
His saying: 'at all' i.e., not in terms of quantitative parts, nor in terms of other than them, whether they are predicable or not, as he will clarify later. As for the non-acceptance of the three kinds, namely unity, point, and the separate entities of individuation, of external division, it is apparent. And as for their non-division into mental parts, because unity and point are not included in any of the nine categories, so they have no genus or differentia. Similarly, the genus-ness of substance has not been established, so the separate entities have no genus. And as for their non-division into essence and individuation, it is based on the fact that individuation is not a part of the person. And the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) in his gloss on Sharḥ al-Tajrīd restricted the parts here to quantitative, and said: 'We only restricted the parts to quantitative, in order to include the personal unity, the personal point, and the personal separate entity among what does not divide, on the assumption that individuation is a part of persons. And the last two also enter on the assumption of their composition from predicable parts.' End. And you should not interpret his statement here as meaning that the term 'at all' means that it has no quantitative parts, neither in reality nor in sensation; because, with the mind not leaning towards it, it contradicts what will come later.
His saying: 'if it has no concept' i.e., a specific essence.
His saying: 'Either it does not divide into particulars' - what is meant by non-division into particulars is that it is not predicated of them. So the aggregate of Zaid and 'Amr is one in person, and some have also stated that, but apparently it is outside the divisions of the one in person that he will mention. Unless it is included under the one by aggregation, and there is something in that.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the concept of 'the one' (al-wāḥid). It begins by stating that the one is either indivisible into particulars (i.e., it cannot be predicated of many individuals) — this is the one in person (al-wāḥid bi-shakhṣ), such as a specific individual; or it is divisible into particulars (i.e., it can be predicated of many) — this is the one not in person, like a species or genus. The latter is many with respect to its individuals but one with respect to its concept. Then it specifies that among the one in person, if it does not accept division at all (neither into quantitative parts, nor into mental parts, nor into essence and individuation), then it is the true one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī). The true one has no concept other than that it is not... (the text cuts off, but the commentary explains that 'no concept' means no specific essence). The commentary elaborates: the one not in person is said to be many necessarily, because it applies to many, whereas the one in person does not necessarily imply many. The commentator clarifies that 'at all' (aṣlan) means no division into quantitative parts, nor into other parts whether predicable or not. He gives examples: unity, point, and separate entities (like intellects) do not divide externally. They also do not divide mentally because they have no genus or differentia. They also do not divide into essence and individuation because individuation is not a part of the person. The commentator also notes a disagreement about whether the aggregate of two individuals can be considered one in person, but the apparent view excludes it.

الواحد بالشخص
الواحد لا بالشخص
الواحد الحقيقي
الأجزاء

Key Takeaway
The one is primarily divided into personal (indivisible into particulars) and non-personal (divisible), and the true one is the personal one that admits no division whatsoever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you seek unity in your worship and intentions, or are you divided between multiple loyalties? How does understanding the true one challenge you to direct all your devotion to the One who is truly indivisible?
?
Consider the concept of the one not in person: something that is one in one respect but many in another. Are there areas in your life where you claim unity of purpose but are actually divided in action? What would it look like to become a true personal one in your commitment to Allah?
?
The true one has no parts and no plurality. When you say 'Allah is One,' do you truly believe in His absolute simplicity, or do you intellectually associate partners with Him in your heart? Reflect on how this logical lesson deepens your tawheed.

Primary Division of the One: Individual and Universal
al-Ījī divides al-wāḥid into individual and universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary division of al-wāḥid into individual (wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ) and universal (wāḥid lā bi al-shakhṣ), and how each is defined by whether its conception prevents predication of many.

Core Idea
The one is either individual or universal based on its conceivability of multiple instances.

Before delving into the various senses of 'one', al-Ījī first establishes a fundamental distinction. This passage divides the concept of unity into two broad types: that which cannot be shared (individual) and that which can be shared (universal). Understanding this division is essential for later discussions on identity, difference, and the attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the classification.
The passage uses technical terms; don't worry about the details yet.
2
Identify the two primary kinds
Notice the two categories: wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ and wāḥid lā bi al-shakhṣ. Understand what 'dividing into particulars' means in this context.
Individual one: its meaning prevents it from being applied to many. Universal one: its meaning allows it.
3
Define each type
Read the commentary carefully. The individual one is exemplified by a specific person like Zayd; the universal one is exemplified by a genus like 'human'.
4
Reflect on the 'one from an aspect' phrase
Consider why the universal one is called 'one' – because it has a single aspect (the concept) even though it applies to many individuals.
This resolves the apparent contradiction that a universal is both one and many.

في أقسام الواحد وهو) أي الواحد (إما لا ينقسم إلى جزئيات بأن يكون تصوره مانعاً من حمله على كثيرين ) وهو الواحد بالشخص ، أو ينقسم إلى جزئيات بأن لا يمنع تصوره من الشركة ( وهو غيره ) أي غير الواحد بالشخص ويسمى واحداً لا بالشخص (وإنه) أي الواحد لا بالشخص ) كثير وله جهة واحدة فهو واحد من وجه) وكثير من وجه آخر . ( وإن قبل ( الواحد بالشخص ( القسمة فإما ) أن ينقسم إلى أجزاء ) مقدارية ( متشابهة) في الحقيقة (وهو الواحد بالاتصال)،
On the divisions of the one (al-wāḥid). (He said:) That is, the one (either does not divide into particulars, in that its conception prevents it from being predicated of many) – and that is the individual one (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ); or it divides into particulars, in that its conception does not prevent sharing (and that is other than it) – that is, other than the individual one, and it is called one not by individual (wāḥid lā bi al-shakhṣ). (And that) – that is, the one not by individual – (is many and has one aspect, so it is one from an aspect) and many from another aspect. (As for the individual one: if it does not accept division) into parts at all, (then it is the real one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī). And it) – that is, the real one – (if it has no concept other than that it does not divide) – i.e., other than the concept of non-division – (then it is the individual unity (al-waḥda al-shakhṣiyya). And if it has) a concept other than that, then it is either possessing position (dhū waḍʿ), i.e., capable of sensory indication (and that is the (numerical) point (al-nuqṭa) individualized), or it does not possess position, and that is the individuated separate entity (al-mufāriq al-mushakhkhaṣ). (And if the individual one accepts) division, then it is either that it divides into (quantitative) parts (similar) in reality (and that is the one by contiguity (al-wāḥid bi al-ittiṣāl)).
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents a systematic classification of the 'one' (al-wāḥid). It begins by dividing al-wāḥid into two primary kinds: (1) that which does not divide into particulars (juzʾiyyāt), meaning its conception prevents it from being predicated of many individuals – this is the individual one (wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ), e.g., Zayd. (2) That which does divide into particulars, meaning its conception does not prevent sharing among many – this is other than the individual one, called 'one not by individual' (wāḥid lā bi al-shakhṣ), i.e., universal concepts like 'human'. This universal one is many in terms of individuals but has one aspect (the concept), so it is one from one aspect and many from another. The passage then turns to the subdivisions of the individual one. If it does not accept division into parts at all, it is the real one (wāḥid ḥaqīqī). The real one is then subdivided: if it has no concept other than non-division, it is individual unity (waḥda shakhṣiyya, e.g., the unity of an individual). If it has a concept beyond non-division, then either it has position (i.e., is located in space, like a numerical point) – this is the individualized point (nuqṭa mushakhkhaṣa) – or it has no position, like an individuated separate entity (mufāriq mushakhkhaṣ), which includes immaterial individuals such as the Necessary Being (al-wājib) or intellects. If the individual one does accept division, it divides into quantitative parts that are similar in essence – this is the one by contiguity (wāḥid bi al-ittiṣāl), such as a quantity of water. Thus the passage provides a comprehensive taxonomy of the various senses of 'one' in kalam and philosophy, linking logical and ontological considerations.

الواحد لا بالشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'one not by individual' is a subtype of individual one.
Correction: It is the opposite: the individual one is unique and not shared; the non-individual one is universal and shared.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the 'individual one' (wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ)?
A) The concept of humanity
B) The individual Zayd
C) The number two
D) The color red

Key Takeaway
The primary division of 'the one' is between the individual (unique, not shareable) and the universal (shareable, one in concept but many in instances).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as a 'believer', do you mean it as an individual one (unique to your personal conviction) or as a universal one (a label shared by many)? Which meaning demands more accountability from you today?
?
The passage shows that something can be one from one aspect and many from another. Is there an area of your character where you claim consistency (one) but your actions show contradiction (many)? What will you do to align them?
?
The individual one cannot be predicated of many. Your relationship with Allah is uniquely yours—no one can take your place in worship. How does that truth change the way you approach your next prayer?

Subdivisions of the Individual One
al-Ījī subdivides the individual one into real one and one by contiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ījī further divides the individual one into the real one (wāḥid ḥaqīqī) and the one by contiguity (wāḥid bi al-ittiṣāl), and the subtypes of the real one.

Core Idea
The individual one is either indivisible (real one) or divisible (one by contiguity).

Having established the primary division, al-Ījī now turns to the individual one and classifies it according to whether it accepts division or not. This leads to a more detailed taxonomy that includes the concept of the 'real one' (wāḥid ḥaqīqī) and its own subdivisions: pure unity, the point, and the separate entity. This classification helps distinguish between various kinds of indivisible entities encountered in philosophy and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the Arabic text from 'أما الواحد بالشخص' to the end. Read the translation and commentary.
2
Understand the split: indivisible vs. divisible
The individual one is divided into that which does not accept division (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī) and that which does accept division (al-wāḥid bi al-ittiṣāl).
Division here means division into parts.
3
Analyze the real one's subtypes
The real one is further subdivided based on whether it has a concept beyond non-division. If not, it is pure individual unity. If so, it is either a point (positioned) or a separate entity (non-positioned).
The separate entity includes immaterial individuals like God or intellects.
4
Examine the one by contiguity
This is the individual one that accepts division into similar quantitative parts, like a block of marble or a quantity of water.

Commentary
This passage presents a systematic classification of the 'one' (al-wāḥid). It begins by dividing al-wāḥid into two primary kinds: (1) that which does not divide into particulars (juzʾiyyāt), meaning its conception prevents it from being predicated of many individuals – this is the individual one (wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ), e.g., Zayd. This universal one is many in terms of individuals but has one aspect (the concept), so it is one from one aspect and many from another. The passage then turns to the subdivisions of the individual one. If it does not accept division into parts at all, it is the real one (wāḥid ḥaqīqī). The real one is then subdivided: if it has no concept other than non-division, it is individual unity (waḥda shakhṣiyya, e.g., the unity of an individual). If it has a concept beyond non-division, then either it has position (i.e., is located in space, like a numerical point) – this is the individualized point (nuqṭa mushakhkhaṣa) – or it has no position, like an individuated separate entity (mufāriq mushakhkhaṣ), which includes immaterial individuals such as the Necessary Being (al-wājib) or intellects. Thus the passage provides a comprehensive taxonomy of the various senses of 'one' in kalam and philosophy, linking logical and ontological considerations.

The One by Connection: Type and Subject
Defines the one by connection and classifies it by type and subject, then gives two definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how something can be considered 'one by connection' in two ways: in type (naw') and in subject (mawdu'), and the two definitions of connection.

Core Idea
Connection defines unity through intrinsic linkage, not mere aggregation.

This lesson explores the concept of 'one by connection' (al-wahid bi al-ittisal) in Islamic theology. We will see how after division, parts can be considered one in type (sharing a common nature) or one in subject (sharing a common locus). The passage also gives two precise definitions of what it means for something to be connected: meeting at a common boundary or having interdependent motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the definitions.
2
Understand the two classifications
Identify the difference between 'one in type' and 'one in subject' using the water example.
Water after division remains water in type, but its parts can occupy the same matter.
3
Grasp the two definitions of connection
Note how connection is defined as either meeting at a common boundary (like lines forming an angle) or as two bodies whose motions necessitate each other.
These are technical definitions used in kalam.
4
Consider the atomist perspective
The passage mentions that for those who affirm the atom (juz'), the one by connection is only one in type, not in subject.
This highlights a key difference between kalām schools.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : في أقسام الواحد
المؤثر بعد القسمة) الانفكاكية (واحد) بالنوع)، فإن الماء الواحد إذا جزئ، كان هناك ماءان متحدان في الحقيقة النوعية، وواحد بالموضوع أي بالمحل، (عند من يقول بالمادة ) ، فإن تلك الأجزاء الحاصلة بالقسمة من شأنها أن يتصل بعضها ببعض. وتحل في مادة واحدة بخلاف أشخاص الناس، إذ ليس من شأنها الاتصال والاتحاد. وأما عند من يقول بالجزء فالواحد بالاتصال بعد القسمة عنده واحد بالنوع دون الموضوع. والتحقيق : أن الواحد بالاتصال الحقيقي إنما يتصور على القول بنفي الجزء، فإن الأجزاء الموجودة بالفعل إذا اجتمعت واتصل بعضها ببعض حتى يحصل منها مركب كان ذلك المركب واحداً بالاجتماع حقيقة، سواء كانت تلك الأجزاء متشابهة أو متخالفة ( وأنه ( أي الواحد بالاتصال ( يقال لمقدارين يتلاقيان عند حد ) مشترك بينهما كالخطين المحيطين بزاوية . (و) يقال أيضاً ( لجسمين يلزم من حركة كل) منهما ( حركة الآخر، وهي على أنواع وأولاها بالاتصال ما كان الالتحام فيه طبيعياً

منها بعد القسمة فرد له. قوله : ( وواحد بالموضوع لأنه لا بد للاتصال الواحد الذي هو قبل القسمة، والاتصالين الحاصلين بعد القسمة من محل يقبلها؛ لئلا يكون التفريق إعداماً بالكلية، وأما قوله : فإن تلك الأجزاء إلخ فلا معنى له إذ ليس عند نفاة الجزء شأن الأجزاء اتصال بعضها ببعض، بل زوال اتصالين وحدوث اتصال ولا حلول تلك الأجزاء في مادة بل حلول الاتصال . اللهم إلا أن يؤول ويقال : المراد من اتصال بعض الأجزاء ببعض حدوث اتصال واحد وضمير كل راجع إلى الاتصال لا إلى الأجزاء، وكذا قوله بخلاف أشخاص الناس لا معنى له؛ لأن المقصود بيان مخالفة الواحد بالاجتماع للواحد بالاتصال في وحدة المادة وأشخاص الناس واحد بالاجتماع إلا أن لا يعتبر في الواحد بالاجتماع الاتصال الحسي.

The Fourth Observation - The Fifth Objective: On the divisions of the one. The effective after the divisive division is one in type (naw'), for a single water, when divided, results in two waters united in specific reality (haqiqa nawiyya), and one in subject (mawdu'), i.e., in locus (mahall), according to those who affirm matter (madda). For those parts resulting from division are such that they can connect to one another and settle in one matter, unlike individual humans, for they are not capable of connection and union. But according to those who affirm the atom (juz'), the one by connection (al-wahid bi al-ittisal) after division is for him one in type but not in subject. The verification: That the one by real connection is only conceivable on the view of denying the atom, for actually existing parts, when they gather and connect to one another until a composite results, that composite is one by aggregation (ijtima') in reality, whether those parts are similar or dissimilar. And that — i.e., the one by connection — is said of two magnitudes that meet at a common boundary, like two lines surrounding an angle; and it is also said of two bodies such that the motion of one necessitates the motion of the other. And it has types, and the foremost in connection is that in which the bonding is natural. His statement: 'One in type' because its parts, since they are similar, i.e., agreeing in reality, each one of them after division is an individual of it. His statement: 'And one in subject' because the one connection that is before division, and the two connections resulting after division, must have a locus that receives them, lest the division be a complete annihilation. As for his statement: 'For those parts, etc.', it has no meaning, for according to the deniers of the atom, the parts do not have the property of connecting to one another; rather, there is a cessation of two connections and a coming into existence of one connection, and no indwelling (hulul) of those parts in matter, but rather an indwelling of the connection. Unless it is interpreted and said: What is meant by 'connection of some parts with others' is the coming into existence of one connection, and the pronoun 'each' refers to the connection, not to the parts. Similarly, his statement: 'unlike individual humans' has no meaning, because the intent is to show the difference between the one by aggregation and the one by connection regarding the unity of matter, and individual humans are one by aggregation, unless sensory connection is not considered in the one by aggregation. His statement: 'in which the bonding is natural' — i.e., created (khalqiyya) in its different degrees; then that in which the bonding is artificial (sina'iyya), like the parts of a chain, in its different degrees. His statement: 'such that they can connect, etc.' — in this formulation there is a kind of deficiency, because his statement 'for those parts, etc.' is an explanation that the parts of the one by connection after division are one in locus, and this does not appear from the statement that those parts can connect and settle in one matter; rather, what is understood from it is that they are ready to settle in it, just as they are ready for connection. And if one reads 'tahullu' (they settle) in the nominative as a conjunct to the whole phrase 'such that they can connect' and not just to the object of 'an' alone, it does not remove the incongruity except for a hint of redundancy in mentioning readiness for connection, except that his statement 'in contrast to it' — since they are not capable of connection and union — repudiates it with a kind of repudiation. And it is better to say: for...
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the 'one by connection' (al-wahid bi al-ittisal), a key concept in discussions of unity. It begins by stating that after division, the effective (al-mu'aththir) is one in type, using water as an example: when a single water is divided, the two resulting waters are united in specific reality (naw'). It then distinguishes between 'one in subject' (bi al-mawdu') according to those who affirm matter, meaning the parts after division can connect and settle in one underlying matter, unlike humans whose bodies cannot thus unite. For atomists (qul bi al-juz'), the one by connection after division is only one in type, not in subject. The verification (al-tahqiq) explains that real connection is only conceivable if one denies the atom, because only then can parts actually exist and combine into a composite that is one by aggregation (ijtima'), regardless of similarity or dissimilarity. The passage then gives two definitions of 'one by connection': (1) two magnitudes meeting at a common boundary (like lines forming an angle), and (2) two bodies whose motions are interdependent. The types are listed in order of priority: natural bonding (khalqi), which is primary, then artificial bonding (sina'i) like the links of a chain. The commentary clarifies the statements about parts and locus, addressing objections from atomists, and explains that the phrase 'capable of connection' actually refers to the connection itself, not the parts indwelling in matter. The final remarks criticize a formulation regarding readiness for connection and settling in matter, suggesting a better phrasing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'one by connection' implies parts inherently capable of indwelling in matter.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is the connection itself that indwells, not the parts, according to the negation of atoms.

Checkpoint
According to the verification in this passage, when is the one by real connection conceivable?

Key Takeaway
The one by connection is defined either by sharing a common type or a common subject, and connection itself is defined by meeting at a boundary or interdependent motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

If not, what is holding you back from that inseparable connection?

Types of Bonding in the One by Connection
Classifies the types of bonding in the one by connection: natural and artificial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of bonding (iltiham) in the one by connection: natural and artificial, and their respective degrees.

Core Idea
The highest form of connection is natural bonding; artificial bonding is secondary.

This lesson continues the discussion on the one by connection by examining the types of bonding that constitute connection. The passage distinguishes between natural (khalqi) bonding, which is inherent and created, and artificial (sina'i) bonding, which is manufactured like the links of a chain. It also mentions that these come in different degrees.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the types of bonding.
2
Understand natural bonding
Natural bonding (al-iltiham al-tabi'i) is the primary type, created by nature (khalq). It includes organic unity.
Example: the parts of a living body.
3
Understand artificial bonding
Artificial bonding (al-iltiham al-sina'i) is secondary, like the links of a chain or the parts of a machine.
Artificial unity depends on external arrangement.
4
Consider the order
The passage states that the first in connection is natural bonding, implying it is the most perfect form.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : في أقسام الواحد

The Fourth Observation - The Fifth Objective: On the divisions of the one. The effective after the divisive division is one in type (naw'), for a single water, when divided, results in two waters united in specific reality (haqiqa nawiyya), and one in subject (mawdu'), i.e., in locus (mahall), according to those who affirm matter (madda). For those parts resulting from division are such that they can connect to one another and settle in one matter, unlike individual humans, for they are not capable of connection and union. But according to those who affirm the atom (juz'), the one by connection (al-wahid bi al-ittisal) after division is for him one in type but not in subject. The verification: That the one by real connection is only conceivable on the view of denying the atom, for actually existing parts, when they gather and connect to one another until a composite results, that composite is one by aggregation (ijtima') in reality, whether those parts are similar or dissimilar. And that — i.e., the one by connection — is said of two magnitudes that meet at a common boundary, like two lines surrounding an angle; and it is also said of two bodies such that the motion of one necessitates the motion of the other. And it has types, and the foremost in connection is that in which the bonding is natural. His statement: 'One in type' because its parts, since they are similar, i.e., agreeing in reality, each one of them after division is an individual of it. His statement: 'And one in subject' because the one connection that is before division, and the two connections resulting after division, must have a locus that receives them, lest the division be a complete annihilation. As for his statement: 'For those parts, etc.', it has no meaning, for according to the deniers of the atom, the parts do not have the property of connecting to one another; rather, there is a cessation of two connections and a coming into existence of one connection, and no indwelling (hulul) of those parts in matter, but rather an indwelling of the connection. Unless it is interpreted and said: What is meant by 'connection of some parts with others' is the coming into existence of one connection, and the pronoun 'each' refers to the connection, not to the parts. Similarly, his statement: 'unlike individual humans' has no meaning, because the intent is to show the difference between the one by aggregation and the one by connection regarding the unity of matter, and individual humans are one by aggregation, unless sensory connection is not considered in the one by aggregation. His statement: 'in which the bonding is natural' — i.e., created (khalqiyya) in its different degrees; then that in which the bonding is artificial (sina'iyya), like the parts of a chain, in its different degrees. His statement: 'such that they can connect, etc.' — in this formulation there is a kind of deficiency, because his statement 'for those parts, etc.' is an explanation that the parts of the one by connection after division are one in locus, and this does not appear from the statement that those parts can connect and settle in one matter; rather, what is understood from it is that they are ready to settle in it, just as they are ready for connection. And if one reads 'tahullu' (they settle) in the nominative as a conjunct to the whole phrase 'such that they can connect' and not just to the object of 'an' alone, it does not remove the incongruity except for a hint of redundancy in mentioning readiness for connection, except that his statement 'in contrast to it' — since they are not capable of connection and union — repudiates it with a kind of repudiation. And it is better to say: for...
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the 'one by connection' (al-wahid bi al-ittisal), a key concept in discussions of unity. It begins by stating that after division, the effective (al-mu'aththir) is one in type, using water as an example: when a single water is divided, the two resulting waters are united in specific reality (naw'). It then distinguishes between 'one in subject' (bi al-mawdu') according to those who affirm matter, meaning the parts after division can connect and settle in one underlying matter, unlike humans whose bodies cannot thus unite. For atomists (qul bi al-juz'), the one by connection after division is only one in type, not in subject. The verification (al-tahqiq) explains that real connection is only conceivable if one denies the atom, because only then can parts actually exist and combine into a composite that is one by aggregation (ijtima'), regardless of similarity or dissimilarity. The passage then gives two definitions of 'one by connection': (1) two magnitudes meeting at a common boundary (like lines forming an angle), and (2) two bodies whose motions are interdependent. The types are listed in order of priority: natural bonding (khalqi), which is primary, then artificial bonding (sina'i) like the links of a chain. The commentary clarifies the statements about parts and locus, addressing objections from atomists, and explains that the phrase 'capable of connection' actually refers to the connection itself, not the parts indwelling in matter. The final remarks criticize a formulation regarding readiness for connection and settling in matter, suggesting a better phrasing.

The One by Continuity: Definition and Types
Defines al-waahid bil-ittisaal and classifies it into natural and artificial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what 'the one by continuity' means and how it is classified into natural and artificial unity.

Core Idea
Unity by continuity connects parts through common boundaries or interdependent movement.

This lesson introduces a key concept in kalam ontology: the one by continuity. Sanusi defines it in two ways and then distinguishes between natural and artificial fusion. Understanding this helps you see how philosophers classify composite entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that the author presents two definitions and then a classification.
The passage uses technical terms like 'ittisaal' (continuity) and 'iltehaam' (fusion).
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines 'the one by continuity' in two ways: (1) two magnitudes meeting at a common boundary, and (2) two bodies whose movements are interdependent. Write these down in your own words.
The first example is like two lines forming an angle; the second is like linked objects.
3
Distinguish natural from artificial fusion
The passage states that natural fusion is created (khalqiyyan) and comes in degrees, while artificial fusion is constructed (like chain links). Compare them: natural unity involves interpenetration of parts, artificial unity does not. Also note the similarity to social unity.
Artificial unity resembles social unity but is stronger because of movement correlation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه (أي الواحد بالاتصال) يقال لمقدارين يتلاقيان عند حد مشترك بينهما كالخطين المحيطين بزاوية. (و) يقال أيضاً (لجسمين يلزم من حركة كل منهما حركة الآخر، وهي على أنواع وأولاها بالاتصال ما كان الالتحام فيه طبيعياً. قوله: ( ما كان الالتحام فيه طبيعياً ) أي خلقياً على اختلاف مراتبه، ثم ما كان الالتحام فيه صناعياً كأجزاء السلسلة على اختلاف مراتبه. وهذا القسم شبيه جداً بالوحدة الاجتماعية. قوله: (شبيه جداً بالوحدة الاجتماعية)؛ لعدم تداخل أطراف أجزائه، بخلاف القسم الأول، وأقوى من الوحدة الاجتماعية للتلازم في الحركة.
And that (i.e., the one by continuity) is said of two magnitudes that meet at a common boundary, like the two lines surrounding an angle. And it is also said of two bodies such that the movement of each entails the movement of the other. It has types, and the foremost among them in continuity is that whose fusion is natural. His saying: (that whose fusion is natural) i.e., created, in its various degrees. Then that whose fusion is artificial, like the parts of a chain, in its various degrees. And this [last] division is very similar to social unity. His saying: (very similar to social unity) because its parts do not interpenetrate, unlike the first division, and it is stronger than social unity due to the correlation in movement.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies 'the one by continuity' (al-waahid bil-ittisaal). It begins by giving two applications of the term: first, two magnitudes meeting at a common boundary (like the sides of an angle); second, two bodies whose movements are interdependent. It then states that this unity has varying degrees, the highest being when the fusion (al-iltehaam) is natural (khalqiyyan), as opposed to artificial fusion such as the links of a chain. The commentator clarifies that natural fusion is created (i.e., inherent in the body's nature), while artificial is man-made. The passage then observes that the artificial division is very similar to social unity (al-wahdah al-ijtimaa‘iyyah). The commentary explains that unlike natural unity (where parts may interpenetrate), artificial unity involves parts that do not interpenetrate; it is stronger than social unity because the parts are linked such that the movement of one necessitates the movement of the other.

الالتحام الصناعي
الوحدة الاجتماعية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That natural and artificial unity are the same or that artificial unity lacks correlation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that natural unity involves interpenetration of parts, while artificial unity does not; but artificial unity is stronger than social unity because movement of one part forces movement of the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The One Not by Individual: Species, Genus, Differentia, and Accident
Classifies al-waahid laa bil-shakhs into four types: bil-naw‘, bil-jins, bil-fasl, and bil-‘arad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers classify the aspect of unity in universals into species, genus, differentia, and accident based on whether the unity is essential or accidental.

Core Idea
Unity in universals is either essential or accidental, dividing into four clear categories.

In this passage, the author classifies the 'one not by individual' — a universal concept that is one in itself but many in its instances. He explains that the aspect of unity (jihah al-wahdah) can be either essential to the multiplicity or accidental. This classification is fundamental to understanding how universals relate to their particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's classification structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main categories
The passage divides the aspect of unity into essential (dhaati) and accidental ('aradi). Find where this division is introduced.
Essential unity is internal to the multiplicity; accidental is external.
3
Examine the essential subcategories
Under essential unity, note the three kinds: species, genus, differentia. Each has a specific definition and example.
Species = entire quiddity; genus = complete commonality; differentia = incomplete commonality.
4
Understand the accidental category
Accidental unity is illustrated by 'one by subject', where the unifying aspect is the substrate (e.g., the same man laughing and writing).
The commentary mentions other accidentals but only by subject is discussed.
5
Reflect on the terminology
The passage clarifies that 'one by species' can refer to the aspect of unity (waahid naw'i) or to the multiplicity (waahid bil-naw'). Understand the distinction.
This prevents confusion when reading other texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الواحد لا بالشخص) فقد عرفت أنه واحد من جهة وكثير من جهة أخرى فجهة الوحدة فيه إما ذاتية للكثرة ) أي غير خارجة عنها، وحينئذ فإما تمام ماهيتها وهو الواحد بالنوع)، كالإنسان بالنسبة إلى أفراده فيقال : الإنسان واحد نوعي وأفراده واحدة بالنوع، (أو جزؤها، فإن كان ) ذلك الجزء ( تمام المشترك بين تلك الكثرة وغيرها ، فهو الواحد بالجنس) إما قريباً كالحيوان بالنسبة إلى أفراده وإما بعيداً على اختلاف مراتبه، كالجسم النامي، والجسم والجوهر بالقياس إلى أفرادها ( وإلا ) وإن لم يكن ذلك الجزء تمام المشترك، فالواحد (بالفصل كالناطق مقيساً إلى أفراده ( وإما عارض) أي تكون جهة الوحدة أمراً عارضاً للكثرة ، أي محمولاً عليها خارجاً عن ماهيتها، (وهو الواحد بالعرض)، وذلك (إما ) واحد بالموضوع) إن كانت جهة الوحدة موضوعة بالطبع لتلك الكثرة، كما يقال : الضاحك والكاتب واحد في الإنسانية) فإن الإنسان. قوله: (أي غير خارجة عنها) ليشمل تمام الماهية. قوله: (كالإنسان) مثال لتمام الماهية. قوله: (فيقال : الإنسان واحد نوعي إلخ إشارة إلى أن الضمير في قوله : وهو الواحد بالنوع راجع إلى الكثير، لا إلى تمام ماهيتها من حيث صدقه على الكثرة، وقس على ذلك فيما سيأتي . فالاصطلاح على أن يقال : لجهة الوحدة واحد نوعي أي واحد من الأنواع، وللكثير الذي هو جهة وحدته واحد بالنوع أي وحدته باعتباره، كما فصله في شرح حكمة العين. قوله: (أي محمول عليها سواء كان بالطبع أو لا ليشمل القسمين. ثم إن أريد بعروضها للماهية أنها كافية في عروضها بعد الوجود كانت هذه الأقسام للوازم، وإليه تشير عبارة المصنف، حيث فرق بين عوارض الماهية وبين عوارض الوجود بأنه لو فرض الخلو عنها لم تكن
As for the one not by individual, you already know that it is one from one aspect and many from another. So the aspect of unity in it is either essential to the multiplicity, i.e., not external to it. In that case, either it is the entire quiddity, and that is the one by species, like man in relation to his individuals; so it is said: 'Man is one by species, and his individuals are one by species.' Or it is a part of it. If that part is the complete commonality between that multiplicity and others, then it is the one by genus, either proximate like animal in relation to its individuals, or remote in its various degrees, like the growing body, body, and substance in relation to their individuals. Otherwise, i.e., if that part is not the complete commonality, then it is the one by differentia, like 'rational' measured against its individuals. Or it is accidental, i.e., the aspect of unity is an accident of the multiplicity, predicated on it and external to its quiddity, and that is the one by accident. That is either one by subject if the aspect of unity is subject (substrate) by nature for that multiplicity, as it is said: 'The laughing and the writing are one in humanity,' for man. His saying: (i.e., not external to it) to include the entire quiddity. His saying: (like man) an example of the entire quiddity. His saying: (so it is said: 'Man is one by species' etc.) indicates that the pronoun in his saying 'and that is the one by species' refers to the multiplicity, not to the entire quiddity in terms of its being true of multiplicity. Similarly for what follows. The convention is to say: for the aspect of unity, 'one by species,' i.e., one of the species; and for the multiplicity which is its aspect of unity, 'one by species,' i.e., its unity in consideration of it, as detailed in Sharh Hikmat al-‘Ayn. His saying: (i.e., predicated on it, whether by nature or not) to include both divisions. Then if by its being accidental to the quiddity one means that it is sufficient for its occurrence after existence, then these divisions are for concomitants. The author's expression alludes to this where he distinguishes between accidents of quiddity and accidents of existence by saying that if their being free from them were supposed, they would not be...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage classifies 'the one not by individual' (al-waahid laa bil-shakhs), which is a universal concept that is one in itself but many in its instantiations. The aspect of unity (jihah al-wahdah) is either essential (dhaatiyyah) to the multiplicity or accidental ('aaridah). If essential, it is either the entire quiddity (tamaam al-maahiyyah) — this is the one by species (bil-naw‘), e.g., 'man' is one species and its individuals are one by species — or a part of the quiddity. If that part is the complete commonality (al-mushtarak al-taamm) between that multiplicity and others, it is one by genus (bil-jins), which can be proximate or remote (e.g., animal, growing body, body, substance). If the part is not the complete commonality, it is one by differentia (bil-fasl), e.g., 'rational'. If the aspect of unity is accidental, it is one by accident (bil-‘arad), which includes one by subject (bil-mawdoo‘), where the unifying aspect is the substrate, e.g., 'the laughing and the writing are one in humanity' because they share the same subject (the same human). The commentary further explains the terminology: the aspect of unity itself is called 'one by species' (waahid naw‘i), while the multiplicity sharing that unity is 'one by species' (waahid bil-naw‘). It also notes that these divisions pertain to concomitants (lawaazim) if we consider accidental unity after existence.

الواحد بالجنس
الواحد بالفصل
الواحد بالعرض
الواحد بالموضوع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'one by species' refers to the species itself rather than to the multiplicity of individuals.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'one by species' (bil-naw‘) is said of the multiplicity (the individuals) not the species itself; the aspect of unity is called 'one by species' (naw‘i) as a type.

Checkpoint

B) One by species
C) One by differentia

Key Takeaway
The classification of unity in universals is based on whether the aspect of unity is essential or accidental, with essential further divided into species, genus, and differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the different ways you identify yourself—as a Muslim, as a parent, as a professional, as a friend. According to the categories in this lesson, which of these are essential (by species, genus, or differentia) and which are accidental? How does that change your attachment to them?
?
The passage classifies 'the laughing and the writing are one in humanity' as accidental unity. Think of a relationship in your life that you consider 'one' but that might be based on accidental factors. Are you willing to re-evaluate its importance in light of what is truly essential in your worship of Allah?
?
If your essence is defined by your rational soul (as implied by the one by differentia 'rational'), what aspects of your daily life reflect that essence, and which distract from it? Write down one concrete change you will make today to align your actions with your essential nature.

The Essential Types of the One that is Not Personal
Commentator defines wahid la bi'l-shakhs and its essential subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential subdivisions of the 'one that is not personal' and the logical criteria that distinguish them.

Core Idea
The 'one not personal' unifies through species, genus, or differentia — each a different logical part of the quiddity.

This lesson unpacks a key logical distinction from the commentary: how a universal concept can be one in aspect yet many in applicability. The commentator defines three essential subtypes of al-wahid la bi'l-shakhs based on whether the aspect of unity is the entire quiddity, a common part, or a distinguishing part. Understanding these will sharpen your grasp of how terms apply across instances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall structure of the classification.
Note that the commentator begins by recalling the definition of al-wahid la bi'l-shakhs.
2
Identify the three divisions
Look for the three subtypes: al-wahid bi'l-naw', al-wahid bi'l-jins, and al-wahid bi'l-fasl. Notice how each is defined by whether the aspect of unity is the entire quiddity, a common part, or a distinguishing part.
The examples (human, animal, rational) are crucial for understanding the distinctions.
3
Analyze the genus subdivision
Observe that genus is further divided into proximate and remote, with examples like 'animal', 'growing body', 'body', 'substance'.
This shows that 'one by genus' applies at multiple levels of generality.
4
Clarify the terminology
The passage ends with a clarification: 'one in species' (wahid naw'i) refers to the aspect of unity itself, while 'one by species' (wahid bi'l-naw') refers to the many that are united. Understand this distinction.
This resolves a common confusion between the two expressions.

As for the one that is not personal (al-wahid la bi'l-shakhs), you have known that it is one in one respect and many in another respect. The aspect of unity in it is either essential to the many (i.e., not external to it). Then it is either the entirety of its quiddity—and it is the one by species (al-wahid bi'l-naw'), like 'human' with respect to its individuals—so it is said: 'Human is one in species and its individuals are one by species'; or it is a part of it. If that part is the entirety of what is common between that many and others, then it is the one by genus (al-wahid bi'l-jins), either proximate (qarib) like 'animal' with respect to its individuals, or remote (ba'id) according to its different levels, like 'growing body', 'body', and 'substance' in relation to their individuals. Otherwise (if that part is not the entirety of what is common), then it is the one by differentia (al-wahid bi'l-fasl), like 'rational' measured against its individuals. His saying: "Very similar to social unity" because of the non-intermingling of the extremities of its parts, unlike the first division, and it is stronger than social unity due to the concomitance in motion. His saying: "As for the one that is not personal, it has appeared from its previous definition that the one that is not personal is the universal concept (al-mafhum al-kulli), and it is one inasmuch as it is itself, and many in terms of applicability (al-sidq). So the aspect of unity is the very concept when considered in itself, i.e., with disregard for applicability." His saying: "i.e., not external to it" to include the entirety of the quiddity. His saying: "like 'human'" is an example for the entirety of the quiddity. His saying: "so it is said: 'Human is one in species' etc." is an indication that the pronoun in his saying "and it is the one by species" refers to the many, not to the entirety of its quiddity in terms of its truth about the many. Measure by that in what follows. So the convention is to say: For the aspect of unity, 'one in species' (wahid naw'i), i.e., one of the species, and for the many that is the aspect of its unity, 'one by species' (wahid bi'l-naw'), i.e., its unity by virtue of it, as detailed in Sharh Hikmat al-'Ayn.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the essential subtypes of al-wahid la bi'l-shakhs (the one that is not personal). The author begins by recalling that al-wahid la bi'l-shakhs is one in aspect but many in another aspect. The aspect of unity can be either essential to the many (i.e., intrinsic) or accidental. Here he discusses the essential branch.

He divides essential unity into three types. First: the aspect of unity is the entire quiddity (tamam al-mahiyya) of the many, called al-wahid bi'l-naw' (one by species). Example: 'human' considered with respect to its individuals; we say 'human is one in species' and its individuals are one by species. Second: if the aspect of unity is only a part of the quiddity, then consider that part: if it is the full commonality between those many and others, it is al-wahid bi'l-jins (one by genus), which can be proximate (e.g., 'animal' with respect to humans and horses) or remote at various degrees (e.g., 'growing body', 'body', 'substance'). Third: if that part is not the full commonality (i.e., it distinguishes this many from others), it is al-wahid bi'l-fasl (one by differentia), e.g., 'rational' with respect to humans.

The commentary then clarifies several points: the phrase 'very similar to social unity' is explained as due to the lack of interpenetration of parts, unlike the first division (personal unity), and it is stronger than social unity because of the concomitance in motion. The commentator explains that al-wahid la bi'l-shakhs is a universal concept, one in itself, many in applicability; the aspect of unity is the concept considered without regard to applicability. He also clarifies that the pronoun in 'it is the one by species' refers to the many, not the quiddity itself, and explains the technical terminology: the aspect of unity is called 'one in species' (wahid naw'i), while the many is called 'one by species' (wahid bi'l-naw') — a distinction made in Sharh Hikmat al-'Ayn.

الواحد بالفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing 'wahid bi'l-naw'' (one by species) with 'wahid naw'i' (one in species), using them interchangeably.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'one in species' refers to the aspect of unity itself (the species concept), while 'one by species' refers to the many individuals that are united by that species.

Checkpoint
When the aspect of unity is a part of the quiddity that is not the full commonality, what type of unity is it?
A) al-wahid bi'l-naw'
B) al-wahid bi'l-jins
C) al-wahid bi'l-fasl
D) al-wahid bi'l-'arad

Key Takeaway
The essential subtypes of al-wahid la bi'l-shakhs are determined by whether the aspect of unity is the entire quiddity (species), a shared part (genus), or a distinguishing part (differentia).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that 'one by species' means the entire quiddity of the individuals. Now, think of yourself: What part of your essence is truly shared with every other human? Is it your body, your soul, or something else? If you truly believed that this shared quiddity is the deepest reality, would you treat others differently? What specific action can you take today to honor that shared quiddity?
?
The passage distinguishes between the aspect of unity (wahid naw'i) and the many united (wahid bi'l-naw'). In your daily life, do you focus more on the unifying aspect (e.g., 'Muslim' as a category) or on the individuals who are united? Which perspective does Allah command you to emphasize? How can you shift your focus to the individuals without losing the unifying concept?

Accidental Unity and the Distinction of Accidents
Defines accidental unity and distinguishes two categories of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of accidental unity and the key distinction between accidents that necessarily accompany the quiddity and those that depend on existence.

Core Idea
Accidents of quiddity are inseparable; accidents of existence require actual existence.

The commentator begins by clarifying the scope of accidental unity—whether the predicate is essential or accidental—and then introduces a crucial distinction: some accidents belong to the quiddity itself, while others arise only after existence. This distinction is foundational for later discussions on attributes and their relation to essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of accidental unity
Note how the commentator expands 'predicated upon it' to include both essential and accidental predication, thus covering all types of accidental unity.
2
Identify the two types of accidents
Focus on the contrast: accidents of quiddity are present even without existence (as necessary concomitants), whereas accidents of existence require actual existence.
The author's example of parts unified in a single matter illustrates this.
3
Understand the looseness in phrasing
The commentator points out that the author's phrase 'or they inhere in a single matter' is imprecise because inherence belongs to the form, not the composite parts.
This shows the care required in theological language.

His saying: (i.e., predicated upon it, whether essentially or not, to include the two divisions). Then if it is intended by its being an accident of the quiddity that it is sufficient in its occurrence after existence, then these divisions are of the necessary concomitants (al-lawazim). And to that refers the author's expression, where he distinguished between the accidents of quiddity ('awarid al-mahiyya) and the accidents of existence ('awarid al-wujud) by saying: If absence from them were assumed, those parts resulting from division would not be unified, existing in a single matter, because the matter is one according to the one who affirms it, whether the parts are connected or not. This, then in his saying 'or they inhere in a single matter', there is a kind of looseness, because the inheren is the form, not those parts composed of hyle and form, so understand. His saying: (between that many and others) it should be understood by 'many' some individuals of the genus, not the totality thereof, otherwise there remains no meaning for 'others'. The fourth observatory — the fifth intention: on the divisions of the one. An accident that attaches to the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself (i.e., disregarding its external identities, and also its mental existence), for there is no role in that attachment for the specificity of either of the two existences, but rather for absolute existence. By an intrinsic matter it is more fitting than the one by an accidental matter, and it is more fitting than the quiddity itself. Contrary to the accidents of existence, and the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) will also explicitly state later in his saying: 'Because the investigation into what attaches to the quiddity is that it is among its necessary concomitants inasmuch as it is itself, etc.' And if it is intended by it that it occurs to the quiddity even with the involvement of another matter, then each of the three divisions is divided into the necessary concomitant (al-lazim) and the separable (al-mufariq), and it is apparent, because it is permissible that the occurrence be in external or mental existence, or both, conditioned by something separable from the quiddity. And his saying: 'Wherever it exists, etc.' does not necessitate the confinement of the accidents of quiddity to the necessary concomitants, as some have imagined, because the universality of places does not necessitate the universality of times. And know that the enumeration among the three divisions is rational, because occurrence cannot be without the existence of the subject of occurrence; so either it is in external existence only, or in mental existence only, or in both. The possibility of another division, such as occurrence with regard to both existences together, or occurrence with regard to the specificity of each of them not with regard to the absolute, is a delusion whose source is lack of reflection and attention to what the apparent expression might suggest. It was said that the quiddity... His saying: (i.e., disregarding, etc.) The intent of the interpretation is to repel what is objected: that the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself is nothing but the quiddity, so how can something attach to it? The summary is that what is meant by 'the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself' is not the quiddity disregarding everything else, even this aspect, but rather the quiddity disregarding its external identities. And since this much is sufficient for repelling, the author sufficed with it, and referred disregarding mental existence to the contrast. The commentator (may God sanctify his secret) added explicit mention of what is known from the contrast. His saying: (but rather for absolute existence) i.e., rather the entrance [cause] for that is absolute existence, i.e., any existence whatever, as his saying indicates. His saying: (but rather for absolute existence) i.e., rather the entrance is for it, and it is supported by what is said: The quiddity's requirement (iqtida') of something and its being qualified by it without regard to existence is inconceivable; for it is known by necessity that what has no existence in any respect cannot be qualified with the affirmation of something for it. So the meaning of 'necessary concomitant of the quiddity' is not that it is qualified by it whether it exists in either of the two existences or not; rather its meaning is that wherever it exists, it is qualified by it. For neither of the two existences has any role in the requirement, but rather the requirer is the quiddity with regard to its absolute existence. It is said, and in it there is discussion, because what is together with the cause does not necessarily have a role in causality; indeed, what equals the cause is not separate from it. And it has no role in... you see that the particularizing form is the cause for the particularization of hyle, while the hyle is the cause for the particularization of the form. Then the requirement is prior by essence to qualification, so the non-separation of the quiddity qualified by its necessary concomitants from existence does not necessitate the causality and the requirement. Unless it is said: If existence had no role in the requirement, then qualification would be valid with disregard for existence, because this qualification would then be a requirement of the essence, but the causality...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on accidental unity (al-wahid bi'l-'arad) and a detailed analysis of the distinction between essential and accidental attributes ('awarid al-mahiyya vs 'awarid al-wujud). The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'i.e., predicated upon it, whether essentially or not' to include both types of accidental unity. He then says that if we take 'occurrence to the quiddity' to mean that the quiddity is sufficient for its occurrence after existence, then these divisions (of the one) fall under necessary concomitants (lawazim). He references the author's distinction between accidents of quiddity and accidents of existence, giving an example about parts resulting from division being unified in a single matter, and points out a looseness in the author's phrasing regarding inherence.

Next, he clarifies that 'between that many and others' means some individuals of the genus, not all. Then the passage transitions to discuss the accidental attribute that attaches to the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself, disregarding both external and mental existence. The point is that the attachment does not depend on a specific existence but on absolute existence. The commentator distinguishes this from accidents of existence and cites the author's later statement about necessary concomitants.

The commentator then distinguishes two interpretations. If attaching accidents are considered without any external factor, they are necessary concomitants. If an external factor can intervene, then each of the three divisions (external, mental, both) can be further divided into necessary and separable. He refutes the idea that 'wherever it exists' implies only concomitants, because spatial universality does not imply temporal universality. The threefold division (external, mental, both) is rational, and any other division is a delusion.

Finally, the commentator addresses an objection: How can something attach to the quiddity in itself? The answer is that 'in itself' does not mean disregarding everything, but only disregarding external identities; mental existence is set aside by contrast. He then engages with a deeper discussion about iqtida' (requirement) and 'illah (causality). The argument is that the quiddity's requirement of its necessary concomitants is in respect of its absolute existence, not specific existence. Some object that if existence has no role, qualification would be valid without existence, but the commentator shows that this does not follow because what accompanies a cause may not itself be a cause. He gives the example of form and hyle mutually causing each other's particularization. The requirement is prior to qualification, so the inseparability of the qualified quiddity from existence does not entail causality. The passage ends mid-sentence with a counter-argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'accidental unity' only covers accidental predication, not essential.
Correction: The commentator explicitly says the definition includes both 'whether essentially or not', so both essential and accidental predication are considered.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what condition makes an accident a 'necessary concomitant' of the quiddity?
A) The quiddity is sufficient for its occurrence after existence
B) The accident occurs only in external existence
C) The accident is separable from the quiddity
D) The accident is predicated essentially

Key Takeaway
Accidental unity includes both essential and accidental predication, and the key distinction is whether an accident necessarily accompanies the quiddity or only appears after existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that some attributes are inseparable from a quiddity. Think of your own character: which of your traits do you treat as inseparable from who you are? Are you certain they are essential, or could they be accidents of existence that you have attached to your identity?
?
The author uses the example of parts not being unified without certain accidents. In your spiritual life, what 'accidents' (practices or states) do you think are necessary for your unity with Allah? Are you willing to let go of those that are merely accidental?

Logical Division of Accidents: Concomitant, Separable, and the Three Existences
Explains the logical division of accidents into necessary/separable and the three existential modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational division of accidents into necessary and separable, and the threefold classification based on existence (external, mental, or both).

Core Idea
Accidents are divided by existence: external, mental, or both; each can be necessary or separable.

After establishing the basic distinction between accidents of quiddity and existence, the commentator now presents a more detailed logical division. He explains that any accident can be either a necessary concomitant (lazim) or separable (mufariq), and that the occurrence of an accident must be in one of three realms: external existence, mental existence, or both. This division is rational and exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations of 'occurrence to the quiddity'
First, if occurrence does not involve an external factor, it is a necessary concomitant. Second, if an external factor can intervene, each division further splits into necessary and separable.
This distinction is crucial for understanding later debates on attributes.
2
Learn the threefold division of existence
The commentator says occurrence must be in external existence only, mental existence only, or both. No other possibility exists; any other is a delusion.
This is a rational (aqli) enumeration.
3
Refute the misconception about 'wherever it exists'
Some think the phrase implies only necessary concomitants, but spatial universality does not imply temporal universality.
The commentator uses a logical rebuttal.
4
Examine the objection and reply on iqtida'
The final part addresses whether existence has a role in the quiddity's requirement of its concomitants. The commentator argues that the quiddity's requirement is by absolute existence, not specific existence, and shows that what accompanies a cause may not itself be a cause.
Watch for the incomplete argument at the end due to cut-off text.

المدخل له ويؤيده ما قيل : اقتضاء الماهية لشيء واتصافها به من غير نظر إلى الوجود غير معقول، فإنه من المعلوم بالضرورة أن ما لا ثبوت له بوجه من الوجوه لا يتصف بثبوت شيء له فليس معنى لازم الماهية أنها متصفة به، سواء وجدت بأحد الوجودين أو لا بل معناه أنها أينما وجدت كانت متصفة به؛ إذ ليس لأحد الوجودين مدخل في الاقتضاء، بل المقتضى الماهية باعتبار مطلق وجودها قيل وفيه بحث، لأن ما مع العلة لا يجب أن العلية الا
His saying: (i.e., predicated upon it, whether essentially or not, to include the two divisions). Then if it is intended by its being an accident of the quiddity that it is sufficient in its occurrence after existence, then these divisions are of the necessary concomitants (al-lawazim). And to that refers the author's expression, where he distinguished between the accidents of quiddity ('awarid al-mahiyya) and the accidents of existence ('awarid al-wujud) by saying: If absence from them were assumed, those parts resulting from division would not be unified, existing in a single matter, because the matter is one according to the one who affirms it, whether the parts are connected or not. This, then in his saying 'or they inhere in a single matter', there is a kind of looseness, because the inheren is the form, not those parts composed of hyle and form, so understand. His saying: (between that many and others) it should be understood by 'many' some individuals of the genus, not the totality thereof, otherwise there remains no meaning for 'others'. The fourth observatory — the fifth intention: on the divisions of the one. An accident that attaches to the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself (i.e., disregarding its external identities, and also its mental existence), for there is no role in that attachment for the specificity of either of the two existences, but rather for absolute existence. By an intrinsic matter it is more fitting than the one by an accidental matter, and it is more fitting than the quiddity itself. Contrary to the accidents of existence, and the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) will also explicitly state later in his saying: 'Because the investigation into what attaches to the quiddity is that it is among its necessary concomitants inasmuch as it is itself, etc.' And if it is intended by it that it occurs to the quiddity even with the involvement of another matter, then each of the three divisions is divided into the necessary concomitant (al-lazim) and the separable (al-mufariq), and it is apparent, because it is permissible that the occurrence be in external or mental existence, or both, conditioned by something separable from the quiddity. And his saying: 'Wherever it exists, etc.' does not necessitate the confinement of the accidents of quiddity to the necessary concomitants, as some have imagined, because the universality of places does not necessitate the universality of times. And know that the enumeration among the three divisions is rational, because occurrence cannot be without the existence of the subject of occurrence; so either it is in external existence only, or in mental existence only, or in both. The possibility of another division, such as occurrence with regard to both existences together, or occurrence with regard to the specificity of each of them not with regard to the absolute, is a delusion whose source is lack of reflection and attention to what the apparent expression might suggest. It was said that the quiddity... His saying: (i.e., disregarding, etc.) The intent of the interpretation is to repel what is objected: that the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself is nothing but the quiddity, so how can something attach to it? The summary is that what is meant by 'the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself' is not the quiddity disregarding everything else, even this aspect, but rather the quiddity disregarding its external identities. And since this much is sufficient for repelling, the author sufficed with it, and referred disregarding mental existence to the contrast. The commentator (may God sanctify his secret) added explicit mention of what is known from the contrast. His saying: (but rather for absolute existence) i.e., rather the entrance [cause] for that is absolute existence, i.e., any existence whatever, as his saying indicates. His saying: (but rather for absolute existence) i.e., rather the entrance is for it, and it is supported by what is said: The quiddity's requirement (iqtida') of something and its being qualified by it without regard to existence is inconceivable; for it is known by necessity that what has no existence in any respect cannot be qualified with the affirmation of something for it. So the meaning of 'necessary concomitant of the quiddity' is not that it is qualified by it whether it exists in either of the two existences or not; rather its meaning is that wherever it exists, it is qualified by it. For neither of the two existences has any role in the requirement, but rather the requirer is the quiddity with regard to its absolute existence. It is said, and in it there is discussion, because what is together with the cause does not necessarily have a role in causality; indeed, what equals the cause is not separate from it. And it has no role in... you see that the particularizing form is the cause for the particularization of hyle, while the hyle is the cause for the particularization of the form. Then the requirement is prior by essence to qualification, so the non-separation of the quiddity qualified by its necessary concomitants from existence does not necessitate the causality and the requirement. Unless it is said: If existence had no role in the requirement, then qualification would be valid with disregard for existence, because this qualification would then be a requirement of the essence, but the causality...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on accidental unity (al-wahid bi'l-'arad) and a detailed analysis of the distinction between essential and accidental attributes ('awarid al-mahiyya vs 'awarid al-wujud). The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'i.e., predicated upon it, whether essentially or not' to include both types of accidental unity. He then says that if we take 'occurrence to the quiddity' to mean that the quiddity is sufficient for its occurrence after existence, then these divisions (of the one) fall under necessary concomitants (lawazim). He references the author's distinction between accidents of quiddity and accidents of existence, giving an example about parts resulting from division being unified in a single matter, and points out a looseness in the author's phrasing regarding inherence.

Next, he clarifies that 'between that many and others' means some individuals of the genus, not all. Then the passage transitions to discuss the accidental attribute that attaches to the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself, disregarding both external and mental existence. The point is that the attachment does not depend on a specific existence but on absolute existence. The commentator distinguishes this from accidents of existence and cites the author's later statement about necessary concomitants.

The commentator then distinguishes two interpretations. If attaching accidents are considered without any external factor, they are necessary concomitants. If an external factor can intervene, then each of the three divisions (external, mental, both) can be further divided into necessary and separable. He refutes the idea that 'wherever it exists' implies only concomitants, because spatial universality does not imply temporal universality. The threefold division (external, mental, both) is rational, and any other division is a delusion.

Finally, the commentator addresses an objection: How can something attach to the quiddity in itself? The answer is that 'in itself' does not mean disregarding everything, but only disregarding external identities; mental existence is set aside by contrast. He then engages with a deeper discussion about iqtida' (requirement) and 'illah (causality). The argument is that the quiddity's requirement of its necessary concomitants is in respect of its absolute existence, not specific existence. Some object that if existence has no role, qualification would be valid without existence, but the commentator shows that this does not follow because what accompanies a cause may not itself be a cause. He gives the example of form and hyle mutually causing each other's particularization. The requirement is prior to qualification, so the inseparability of the qualified quiddity from existence does not entail causality. The passage ends mid-sentence with a counter-argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'necessary concomitant of the quiddity' means the quiddity is qualified by it even when the quiddity has no existence at all.
Correction: The passage explains: 'ليس معنى لازم الماهية أنها متصفة به، سواء وجدت بأحد الوجودين أو لا بل معناه أنها أينما وجدت كانت متصفة به' – it means wherever it exists, it is qualified.

Checkpoint
How many rational divisions of accidents does the commentator identify based on existence?
A) Two: necessary and separable
B) Three: external, mental, and both
C) Four: essential, accidental, necessary, separable
D) Six: external necessary, external separable, etc.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are rationally divided based on existence into three categories (external, mental, both), and each can be either a necessary concomitant or separable depending on whether an external factor is involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the quiddity's necessary concomitants are not caused by existence itself. Consider your own relationship with knowledge: Do you seek knowledge as a necessary part of your being, or do you treat it as an accidental addition? How would your pursuit of knowledge change if you realized it is a necessary concomitant of your created nature, irrespective of external validation? Take one concrete step today to pursue knowledge as an essential part of your soul, not as a mere adornment.
?
The commentator warns against a delusion: thinking there is a fourth division of existence. How many times have you invented unnecessary categories in your own understanding of faith, complicating the clear teachings of the Qur'an and Sunnah? Identify one such unnecessary distinction you have held—perhaps between 'heart knowledge' and 'mind knowledge'—and write a resolution to abandon it, relying on the simplicity of the truth.
?
The lesson teaches that 'wherever it exists' does not imply necessity in all places. Think of a characteristic you ascribe to yourself as 'always'—like patience or anger. Is that truly a necessary concomitant of your soul, or is it separable and dependent on circumstances? How can you align your self-assessment with the reality of your changing states, and what does that demand from you in terms of humility before Allah?

The True One and the Priority of Unity
Classification of the one and priority of true unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of the one into types and the reasoning behind the priority of the true one and its unity.

Core Idea
The true one that admits no division is the most deserving of being called one.

In this lesson, the author lays out the fundamental categories of 'the one' (al-wahid). He distinguishes between the individual one that cannot be divided at all—the true one—and other ones that can be divided in some way. He then argues that the true one and its associated unity are prior to other types. The commentary explains why unity is inseparable from the true one, making its unity superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of the one
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the different types: the individual one (al-wahid al-shakhsi) that accepts no division at all is called the true one. This is contrasted with what accepts division in some respect.
The passage also mentions one by connection and one by aggregation.
2
Identify the three priority statements
Identify the three priority claims: (1) the true one is prior to what accepts division; (2) the unity that belongs to the true one is prior to other unities; (3) the one by connection is prior to the one by aggregation.
Think about why connection is more unified than aggregation.
3
Understand why unity of the true one is prior
Read the commentary explaining that unity cannot be conceived separately from the true one, unlike the point and the separable, where separation can be conceived even if impossible.
