Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument proves logical identity between part and whole, or that the judge's ruling is based on legal presumption.
Correction: The argument is about linguistic usage of 'غير' to indicate a separate number; the reply shows that 'other than ten' means any additional number, not the components of ten.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari evidence that part and whole are not considered 'different' (ghayrayn)?
A) If part were different, saying 'other than ten' would not imply five.
B) The Law says that parts are not independent entities.
C) Language allows no distinction between part and whole.
D) The judge's ruling would be impossible if part were different.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari claim that part and whole are not different is based on the linguistic rule that 'other than X' excludes only other numbers, not the parts of X.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat a 'part' of your life – like a small sin or a bad habit – as if it is separate from your overall commitment to Allah, convincing yourself it doesn't affect the 'whole'? What would change if you saw that part as fully integrated into your whole self, and thus demanded accountability?
?

The Argument About Attributes Being 'Other Than' the Described and Its Refutation
Al-Amidi presents an argument about attributes and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that attributes must be identical to the substance because otherwise a statement like 'There is no one in the house other than Zayd' would be false is invalid, due to the ambiguity of the term 'other'.

Core Idea
The term 'other' can mean numerically distinct individuals or conceptually distinct attributes – conflating them leads to fallacious arguments.

This passage from al-Amidi presents a subtle but important argument about the relationship between attributes and the described. Proponents of attribute identity use a linguistic test involving the word 'other' to argue that attributes cannot be distinct. However, the refutation reveals a crucial ambiguity in the meaning of 'other'. By understanding this distinction, you will gain clarity on a key point in kalam ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully, noting the structure: argument then refutation.
2
Identify the argument
Recognize that the argument claims that if attributes were distinct, saying 'There is no one in the house other than Zayd' when Zayd is knowledgeable would be false because knowledge is another entity.
This is the opponents' view.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation points out that 'other' here means other individual humans, not attributes. Otherwise, even Zayd's garment would be considered 'other', which is absurd.
4
Apply the distinction
Distinguish between numerical otherness (distinct individuals) and conceptual otherness (distinct attributes). The argument only works if we conflate the two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد السابع: الاثنان هما الغيران فوق العشرة . قالوا : وكذا الحال في الصفة والموصوف، فإنك إذا قلت : ليس في الدار غير زيد، وكان زيد العالم فيها، فقد صدقت. ولو كانت الصفة غير الموصوف لكنت كاذباً . ورد بأن المراد غيره من أفراد الإنسان، وإلا لزم أن لا يكون ثوب زيد غيره، وهو باطل قطعاً.
The Fourth Observatory - the Seventh Objective: The two are the different. Above ten. They said: The same applies to the attribute and the described, for if you say 'There is no one in the house other than Zayd' and Zayd was the scholar in it, then you have spoken truthfully. But if the attribute were other than the described, you would be a liar. It was refuted that what is meant by 'other than him' is other individual humans, otherwise it would necessarily follow that Zayd's garment would not be other than him, and that is absolutely false.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an argument and its refutation. It begins by stating the heading 'The two are the different' and then says 'Above ten', which may refer to a previous context. Then 'They said: The same applies to the attribute and the described'. The argument is: if you say 'There is no one in the house other than Zayd' and Zayd is knowledgeable (the attribute of knowledge is present), you are truthful. But if the attribute (knowledge) were something distinct from Zayd (the described), then the statement would be false because it would imply that the attribute is another entity in the house. The refutation follows: the phrase 'other than him' refers to other individual humans, not to attributes. Otherwise, even Zayd's garment would be considered 'other than him' in a similar sense, which is clearly false. Thus, the argument fails because it conflates two different meanings of 'other'.

Key Terms

الصفة والموصوف
غير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument shows that attributes cannot be distinct from the described in any way.
Correction: The refutation shows that the argument only applies to numerical distinctness of individuals, not to conceptual or logical distinctness of attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation in this passage?
A) The example only applies to separable attributes.
B) The phrase 'other than Zayd' means other individuals, not attributes.
C) The attribute is always identical to the described.
D) The argument is valid for all attributes.

Key Takeaway
The argument that attributes must be identical to the substance because otherwise a statement about 'no one else in the house' would be false fails because it conflates numerical distinctness of individuals with conceptual distinctness of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah's attributes that, if pushed to their logical conclusion, would lead to an equally obvious absurdity? Commit to examining one such belief today with the clarity this lesson provides.

Conceptual Distinction Without Mental Existence
The gloss argues that conceptual distinction does not require mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming a conceptual distinction between an attribute and the essence does not require accepting the theory of mental existence.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinction stands independent of mental existence.

This gloss clarifies a subtle but important point in Islamic theology. The early masters used expressions like 'not He and not other' to describe the relationship between attributes and essence. Some might think this implies a commitment to mental existence, but the gloss shows otherwise. Let's see the argument.

Guided Path

This is a methodological independence claim.
3

The gloss refers to the discussion of whether existence is superadded to quiddity. Understand that this point keeps the attribute-essence debate neutral regarding mental existence.

Two Things Cannot Unite: Metaphorical vs Real Union
Al-Ijji defines three senses of union: metaphorical via transformation/composition and real (impossible).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between metaphorical and real union and why real union between two distinct existents is impossible.

Core Idea
Union between distinct things is only metaphorical; true union is impossible without change or composition.

Al-Ijji opens the Eighth Objective with a necessary judgment: two distinct things do not unite in the real sense. He then clarifies that the term 'union' is used metaphorically in two ways—transformation and composition—but these are not true union. This lesson unpacks these definitions and the argument for impossibility, setting the stage for its application to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on union
Read the Arabic passage once through, then its translation. Focus on how Al-Ijji distinguishes the two metaphorical senses from the real concept.
Notice the terms 'istihalah' (transformation) and 'tarkib' (composition).
2
Identify the three concepts of union
The passage presents two metaphorical unions (by transformation and by composition) and one real union. List them and note the key characteristics of each.
Transformation involves change; composition involves joining; real union involves neither loss nor addition.
3
Understand the impossibility of real union
The real concept requires a thing becoming another without any change or composition, which is impossible for two distinct existents that can be separated.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it prevents misunderstandings about divine attributes and the relationship between distinct entities in kalam.
This principle is used later to argue that attributes are not posterior to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : الاثنان لا يتحدان وهذا حكم ضروري

[ المقصد الثامن : الاثنان لا يتحدان وهذا حكم ضروري ]

( الاثنان لا يتحدان)، الاتحاد يطلق بطريق المجاز على صيرورة شيء ما شيئاً آخر، بطريق الاستحالة أعني التغير والانتقال دفعياً كان أو تدريجياً، كما يقال : صار الماء هواء، والأسود أبيض، ففي الأول زال حقيقة الماء بزوال صورته النوعية عن هيولاه، وانضم إلى تلك الهيولى الصورة النوعية التي للهواء فحصل حقيقة أخرى حقيقة الهواء. وفي الثاني زال صفة السواد عن الموصوف بها، واتصف بصفة أخرى هي البياض، ويطلق أيضاً بطريق المجاز على صيرورة شيء شيئاً آخر بطريق التركيب، وهو أن ينضم شيء إلى شيء ثان فيحصل منهما شيء ثالث، كما يقال : صار التراب طيناً، والخشب سريراً، والاتحاد بهذين المعنيين لا شك في جوازه، بل في وقوعه أيضاً، وأما المفهوم الحقيقي للاتحاد فهو : أن يصير شيء بعينه شيئاً آخر، ومعنى قولنا : بعينه أنه صار شيئاً آخر من غير أن يزول عنه شيء أو ينضم إليه شيء،

موجودان يجوز الانفكاك بينهما، والباقي من إلحاقات المشايخ توجيهاً لكلامه، ومقصوده أن صفاته تعالى ليست متأخرة عن وجوده؛ لكونها مقتضى ذاته ،كوجوده فلا تكون ذاته تعالى فاعلة لها؛ لأن الفاعل يجب تقدمه بالوجود بالذات فلا تكون ذاته تعالي بالقياس إليها موجبا، ولا مختاراً، فلا يلزم شيء من المحذورات، كما أن ذاته تعالى ليس موجباً ولا مختاراً بالنسبة إلى وجوده، عند القائلين بزيادته. وكما أن الأربعة ليست بفاعلة لزوجيتها لا إيجاباً ولا اختياراً بل الزوجية مجعولة بجعلها .

قوله: (بطريق المجاز) فإن الشيء الأول لما كان باقياً في حالة الاستحالة والتركيب إما بجزئه أو بنفسه فكأنه اتحد بالشيء الثاني . قوله : ( شيئاً آخر) ذاتاً وصفة .

قوله : (أعني التغير إلخ) أي ليس المراد المعنى المصطلح أعني التغير التدريجي في الكيف، بل المعنى اللغوي وهو التغير مطلقاً . قوله : ( من غير أن يزول عنه شيء، أو ينضم إليه كلمة أو للتعميم أي لا يكون فيه شيء
The Fourth Observatory - The Eighth Objective: The two do not unite, and this is a necessary judgment. [The Eighth Objective: The two do not unite, and this is a necessary judgment.] (The two do not unite). Union (al-ittihad) is used metaphorically for something becoming another thing by way of transformation (al-istihalah), meaning change and transition, whether instantaneous or gradual, as is said: water becomes air, and black becomes white. In the first, the reality of water ceased by the removal of its specific form from its matter, and the specific form of air joined that matter, so another reality, the reality of air, resulted. In the second, the quality of blackness ceased from the thing described, and it was described with another quality, whiteness. It is also used metaphorically for something becoming another thing by way of composition (al-tarkib), which is that a thing joins a second thing and a third thing results from them, as is said: dust becomes clay, and wood becomes a bed. Union in these two senses is undoubtedly permissible, indeed it occurs. As for the real concept of union, it is that a thing itself becomes another thing. The meaning of our saying 'itself' is that it becomes another thing without anything ceasing from it or anything joining it. Two existents between which separation is possible... and the rest of the additions of the masters as an interpretation of his statement. His intention is that His attributes, may He be exalted, are not posterior to His existence; because they are requirements of His essence, like His existence, so His essence, may He be exalted, is not an agent for them; because the agent must be prior in existence and essence, so His essence in relation to them is neither a necessitator nor a free agent, so none of the undesirable consequences follow. Just as His essence, may He be exalted, is neither a necessitator nor a free agent in relation to His existence, according to those who affirm its addition. And just as the four is not an agent for its evenness, neither by necessitation nor by choice, but rather evenness is made by its making. His statement: (metaphorically) because the first thing, since it remains in the case of transformation and composition, either by its part or by itself, it is as if it united with the second thing. His statement: (another thing) in essence and attribute. His statement: (I mean change etc.) i.e., the intended meaning is not the technical term meaning gradual change in quality, but rather the linguistic meaning, which is change absolutely. His statement: (without anything ceasing from it or joining it) the word 'or' is for generalization, i.e., there is no thing in it...
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Eighth Objective, which lays down the necessary judgment that two distinct things do not unite in the real sense. The author first defines two metaphorical uses of 'union' (al-ittihad): (1) transformation (al-istihalah), where a thing changes into another through the loss of one form and the acquisition of another, such as water becoming air (substantial change) or black becoming white (accidental change); and (2) composition (al-tarkib), where two things combine to form a third, such as dust becoming clay or wood becoming a bed. The author notes that union in these two senses is both permissible and actually occurs. Then he contrasts the real meaning of union: that a thing itself becomes another thing without any loss or addition—i.e., without any change or composition. This is impossible for two distinct existents because they are separate and can be separated. The passage then shifts to a discussion of divine attributes, explaining that attributes are not posterior to the essence; they are requirements of the essence, not products of its agency. The essence is neither a necessitator nor a free agent with respect to its attributes, just as it is neither with respect to its own existence (according to those who affirm existence as additional). The commentary following explains the metaphorical sense: the first thing remains in some way (by part or by whole), so it is as if it united. The phrase 'another thing' covers essence and attribute. The phrase 'I mean change' specifies that it is not the technical term for gradual qualitative change but the linguistic sense. The final comment clarifies the phrase 'without anything ceasing or joining'—the word 'or' is for generalization, meaning no thing at all. The passage thus establishes the impossibility of real union and uses this principle to defend the Ash'ari view of attributes as not being caused by the essence in a temporal sense.

Correction: The passage shows that any union between distinct things involves either change (transformation) or composition; real union without change or composition is impossible.

Implications for Divine Attributes
Al-Ijji argues that divine attributes are not caused by the essence but are necessary concomitants, using the principle of impossibility of real union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine attributes are not caused by the essence and how the principle of impossibility of real union supports this view.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are requirements of the essence, not products of its agency.

Having established that two distinct things cannot unite in reality, Al-Ijji applies this principle to the relationship between God's essence and attributes. He argues that attributes are not posterior to the essence; they are necessary concomitants, just as existence is (for those who affirm it as additional). This lesson explains the argument that the essence is neither a necessitator nor a free agent with respect to its attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on divine attributes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Ijji connects the impossibility of real union to the attributes being requirements of the essence.
The phrase 'like His existence' is key to the analogy.
2
Identify the core argument
The passage argues that attributes are not posterior to the essence because they are requirements (muqtada) of it. Therefore, the essence is not an agent (fa'il) for them.
An agent must be prior to its act; attributes are not acts.
3
Understand the analogy with four and evenness
Al-Ijji uses the example of the number four not being an agent for its evenness, even though evenness is a property of four.
4
Apply the principle to avoid theological pitfalls
By denying that the essence is a necessitator or free agent for attributes, we avoid undesirable consequences such as temporality or multiplicity in the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : الاثنان لا يتحدان وهذا حكم ضروري

[ المقصد الثامن : الاثنان لا يتحدان وهذا حكم ضروري ]

( الاثنان لا يتحدان)، الاتحاد يطلق بطريق المجاز على صيرورة شيء ما شيئاً آخر، بطريق الاستحالة أعني التغير والانتقال دفعياً كان أو تدريجياً، كما يقال : صار الماء هواء، والأسود أبيض، ففي الأول زال حقيقة الماء بزوال صورته النوعية عن هيولاه، وانضم إلى تلك الهيولى الصورة النوعية التي للهواء فحصل حقيقة أخرى حقيقة الهواء. وفي الثاني زال صفة السواد عن الموصوف بها، واتصف بصفة أخرى هي البياض، ويطلق أيضاً بطريق المجاز على صيرورة شيء شيئاً آخر بطريق التركيب، وهو أن ينضم شيء إلى شيء ثان فيحصل منهما شيء ثالث، كما يقال : صار التراب طيناً، والخشب سريراً، والاتحاد بهذين المعنيين لا شك في جوازه، بل في وقوعه أيضاً، وأما المفهوم الحقيقي للاتحاد فهو : أن يصير شيء بعينه شيئاً آخر، ومعنى قولنا : بعينه أنه صار شيئاً آخر من غير أن يزول عنه شيء أو ينضم إليه شيء،

موجودان يجوز الانفكاك بينهما، والباقي من إلحاقات المشايخ توجيهاً لكلامه، ومقصوده أن صفاته تعالى ليست متأخرة عن وجوده؛ لكونها مقتضى ذاته ،كوجوده فلا تكون ذاته تعالى فاعلة لها؛ لأن الفاعل يجب تقدمه بالوجود بالذات فلا تكون ذاته تعالي بالقياس إليها موجبا، ولا مختاراً، فلا يلزم شيء من المحذورات، كما أن ذاته تعالى ليس موجباً ولا مختاراً بالنسبة إلى وجوده، عند القائلين بزيادته. وكما أن الأربعة ليست بفاعلة لزوجيتها لا إيجاباً ولا اختياراً بل الزوجية مجعولة بجعلها .

قوله: (بطريق المجاز) فإن الشيء الأول لما كان باقياً في حالة الاستحالة والتركيب إما بجزئه أو بنفسه فكأنه اتحد بالشيء الثاني . قوله : ( شيئاً آخر) ذاتاً وصفة .

قوله : (أعني التغير إلخ) أي ليس المراد المعنى المصطلح أعني التغير التدريجي في الكيف، بل المعنى اللغوي وهو التغير مطلقاً . قوله : ( من غير أن يزول عنه شيء، أو ينضم إليه كلمة أو للتعميم أي لا يكون فيه شيء
The Fourth Observatory - The Eighth Objective: The two do not unite, and this is a necessary judgment. [The Eighth Objective: The two do not unite, and this is a necessary judgment.] (The two do not unite). Union (al-ittihad) is used metaphorically for something becoming another thing by way of transformation (al-istihalah), meaning change and transition, whether instantaneous or gradual, as is said: water becomes air, and black becomes white. In the first, the reality of water ceased by the removal of its specific form from its matter, and the specific form of air joined that matter, so another reality, the reality of air, resulted. In the second, the quality of blackness ceased from the thing described, and it was described with another quality, whiteness. It is also used metaphorically for something becoming another thing by way of composition (al-tarkib), which is that a thing joins a second thing and a third thing results from them, as is said: dust becomes clay, and wood becomes a bed. Union in these two senses is undoubtedly permissible, indeed it occurs. As for the real concept of union, it is that a thing itself becomes another thing. The meaning of our saying 'itself' is that it becomes another thing without anything ceasing from it or anything joining it. Two existents between which separation is possible... and the rest of the additions of the masters as an interpretation of his statement. His intention is that His attributes, may He be exalted, are not posterior to His existence; because they are requirements of His essence, like His existence, so His essence, may He be exalted, is not an agent for them; because the agent must be prior in existence and essence, so His essence in relation to them is neither a necessitator nor a free agent, so none of the undesirable consequences follow. Just as His essence, may He be exalted, is neither a necessitator nor a free agent in relation to His existence, according to those who affirm its addition. And just as the four is not an agent for its evenness, neither by necessitation nor by choice, but rather evenness is made by its making. His statement: (metaphorically) because the first thing, since it remains in the case of transformation and composition, either by its part or by itself, it is as if it united with the second thing. His statement: (another thing) in essence and attribute. His statement: (I mean change etc.) i.e., the intended meaning is not the technical term meaning gradual change in quality, but rather the linguistic meaning, which is change absolutely. His statement: (without anything ceasing from it or joining it) the word 'or' is for generalization, i.e., there is no thing in it...
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Eighth Objective, which lays down the necessary judgment that two distinct things do not unite in the real sense. The author first defines two metaphorical uses of 'union' (al-ittihad): (1) transformation (al-istihalah), where a thing changes into another through the loss of one form and the acquisition of another, such as water becoming air (substantial change) or black becoming white (accidental change); and (2) composition (al-tarkib), where two things combine to form a third, such as dust becoming clay or wood becoming a bed. The author notes that union in these two senses is both permissible and actually occurs. Then he contrasts the real meaning of union: that a thing itself becomes another thing without any loss or addition—i.e., without any change or composition. This is impossible for two distinct existents because they are separate and can be separated. The passage then shifts to a discussion of divine attributes, explaining that attributes are not posterior to the essence; they are requirements of the essence, not products of its agency. The essence is neither a necessitator nor a free agent with respect to its attributes, just as it is neither with respect to its own existence (according to those who affirm existence as additional). The commentary following explains the metaphorical sense: the first thing remains in some way (by part or by whole), so it is as if it united. The phrase 'another thing' covers essence and attribute. The phrase 'I mean change' specifies that it is not the technical term for gradual qualitative change but the linguistic sense. The final comment clarifies the phrase 'without anything ceasing or joining'—the word 'or' is for generalization, meaning no thing at all. The passage thus establishes the impossibility of real union and uses this principle to defend the Ash'ari view of attributes as not being caused by the essence in a temporal sense.

مقتضى
فاعل
موجب

Correction: The passage argues that attributes are not effects; they are necessary concomitants, so the essence is not an agent for them.

Key Takeaway

The True Meaning of Union and Its Impossibility by Necessity
Defines true union and proves its impossibility by necessary rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true meaning of union as defined by kalam scholars, its two conceptual forms, and the rational proof that such union is impossible by necessity.

Core Idea
True union requires two things becoming one, impossible because their difference is essential.

Kalam often discusses concepts that seem obvious but have deep implications. One such concept is 'union' (ittihad). The theologians define it in a specific way to show that true union between two distinct things cannot occur. This passage from al-Mawaqif establishes the definition and then proves its impossibility using a necessary rational judgment. Let's explore how they argue this.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of true union
Read the passage and note that the author begins by stating that two things do not unite, and this is a necessary judgment. He then clarifies that the true concept of union is the primary understanding when the word is used absolutely.
Focus on the phrase 'المتبادر من الاتحاد عند الإطلاق'.
2
Identify the two forms of union
The passage presents two ways true union is conceived: (1) two distinct things (like Zayd and Amr) unite by one becoming the other; (2) one thing becomes another different thing. Notice the structural change before and after union in each case.
Think about why both forms are problematic.
3
Grasp the proof of impossibility
The author argues that the difference between two essences or identities is by essence (بالذات) and cannot be removed. This is a necessary judgment of the intellect. Understand that essential properties are inseparable.
Compare with other essential properties like 'heat for fire'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The impossibility of true union is not merely a logical possibility but a necessary intellectual judgment. Why do the theologians insist on this? Consider the implications for theological discussions about the attributes of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : الاثنان لا يتحدان وهذا حكم ضروري بعينه وإنما كان هذا مفهوماً حقيقياً؛ لأنه المتبادر من الاتحاد عند الإطلاق. وإنما يتصور هذا المعنى الحقيقي على وجهين الأول أن يكون هناك شيئان كزيد وعمرو مثلاً، فيتحدان بأن يصير زيد عمراً، أو بالعكس. ففي هذا الوجه قبل الاتحاد شيئان، وبعده شيء واحد كان حاصلا قبله والثاني أن يكون هناك شيء واحد، كزيد فيصير هو شخصاً آخر غيره، فحينئذ يكون قبل الاتحاد أمر واحد، وبعده أمر آخر لم يكن حاصلا قبله بل بعده وهذا المعنى الحقيقي باطل بالضرورة، وإليه أشار بقوله : (هذا) أي عدم اتحاد الاثنين حكم ضروري يحكم به بديهة العقل بعد تجريد الطرفين على ما ينبغي . فإن الاختلاف والتغاير بين الماهيتين، وبين الهويتين، وكذا بين الماهية والهوية اختلاف وتغاير بالذات، فلا يعقل زواله، يعني التغاير بين كل اثنين فرضاً مقتضى ذاتهما، فلا يمكن زواله عنهما كسائر لوازم الماهيات.
The Fourth Watch - The Eighth Objective: Two things do not unite, and this is a necessary judgment in itself. And this was a true concept only because it is the primary understanding from the word 'union' when used absolutely. And this true meaning is conceived in two ways: first, that there are two things, like Zayd and Amr for example, and they unite by Zayd becoming Amr, or vice versa. So in this way, before union there are two things, and after it one thing that existed before it. And second, that there is one thing, like Zayd, and he becomes another person different from him, so then before union there is one thing, and after it another thing that did not exist before it but after it. And this true meaning is false by necessity, and to that he pointed by his saying: 'This (i.e., the non-unity of two things) is a necessary judgment judged by the primary intellect after stripping the two sides as is fitting. For the difference and otherness between the two essences, and between the two identities, and likewise between the essence and identity, is a difference and otherness by essence, so its removal is not conceivable,' meaning that otherness between every two things assumed is a requirement of their essence, so its removal from them is impossible, like other necessary concomitants of essences.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the true meaning of union (الاجتماع الحقيقي) and proves that it is impossible by necessity. The author begins by stating that two things do not unite, and this is a necessary judgment. He then explains that the true concept of union is the primary understanding when the word 'union' is used absolutely. This true meaning has two forms: (1) two things, like Zayd and Amr, unite by one becoming the other, so before union there are two, after union one that existed before; (2) one thing becomes another different thing, so before union one, after union another that did not exist before. Both forms are false by necessity, as indicated by the author's statement that the non-unity of two is a necessary judgment of the intellect after abstraction. The reason is that the difference between two essences, two identities, or between essence and identity is essential and cannot be removed, just like other essential properties.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Union can occur by two things sharing the same identity or by one thing changing into another without essential change.
Correction: The author shows that true union involves two distinct things becoming one, or one thing becoming another, both of which require removal of essential difference, which is impossible.

Checkpoint

B) Because the difference between two things is essential and cannot be removed.
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of two things becoming one.
D) Because union only applies to non-physical entities.

Key Takeaway
True union is impossible because the essential difference between any two things cannot be removed; this is a necessary judgment of the intellect.

The Three Divisions of Two Things
Classification of any two entities into similars, opposites, and the different.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational categories for comparing any two entities according to Ahl al-Haqq and the distinction between essential and meaning attributes.

Core Idea
Two things are related as similars, opposites, or different based on essential attributes.

In the study of kalam, understanding how things relate to each other is crucial for arguments about God's attributes. This passage lays out the foundational classification of any two things into three categories: similars, opposites, and the different. The key is whether they share essential attributes. Let's read the author's precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the author in stating the three divisions.
2
Identify the three divisions
Find the three categories: similars, opposites, and the different. Observe the condition that distinguishes each: sharing essential attributes and possibility of coexistence.
The division is based on two criteria: sharing attributes and impossibility of coexistence.
3
Understand the definition of similars
Similars are defined as two existents sharing all essential attributes. Note the specific examples given for essential attributes of a human: humanity, reality, existence, thingness.
Essential attributes do not require an additional concept for description.
4
Contrast essential and meaning attributes
Meaning attributes (e.g., spatial occupation, temporality) require an extra concept beyond the subject. Essential attributes do not. This distinction is key for later theological arguments.
The passage ends by contrasting the two types of attributes.
5
Reflect on the classification's purpose
Ask yourself why this classification matters in kalam. It provides a precise vocabulary for discussing how created things relate to each other and to God.
This classification is foundational for later discussions about God's attributes and the Trinity in Christian-Muslim debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاثنان عند أهل الحق من المتكلمين ثلاثة أقسام، لأنهما إن اشتركا الصفات النفسية فالمثلان وإلا فإن امتنع لذاتيهما اجتماعهما في محل واحد من جهة واحدة فالضدان وإلا فالمتخالفان. أحدها المثلان وهما: الموجودان المشتركان في جميع الصفات النفسية. والمراد بالصفات النفسية ما لا يحتاج في وصف الشيء به إلى تعقل أمر زائد عليه كالإنسانية والحقيقة والوجود والشيئية للإنسان، وتقابلها الصفات المعنوية التي تحتاج في الوصف بها إلى تعقل أمر زائد على ذات الموصوف كالتحيز والحدوث.
The two according to the People of Truth among the theologians are three divisions: because if they share the essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-nafsiyya), then they are similars (al-mithlān); otherwise, if it is impossible for them to be together in one locus from one aspect by virtue of their essences, then they are opposites (al-ḍiddān); otherwise, they are the different (al-mutakhālifān). One of them is the similars, which are the two existents that share all essential attributes. And by essential attributes is meant that which does not require in describing the thing by it the conceiving of an additional matter beyond it, like humanity, reality, existence, and thingness for a human; and their opposite are the meaning-attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) which require in describing by them the conceiving of an additional matter beyond the described subject, like spatial occupation (al-taḥayyuz) and temporality (al-ḥudūth).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. It begins by stating that 'the two'—meaning any pair of things considered together—are divided into three kinds according to the Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox theologians). The division is based on whether they share essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-nafsiyya). If they do, they are similars (mithlān). If they do not share essential attributes but it is impossible for them to occupy the same locus from one aspect by virtue of their essences, they are opposites (ḍiddān). If they neither share essential attributes nor have impossibility of coexistence, they are different (mutakhālifān). The passage then defines similars as two existents sharing all essential attributes. It defines essential attributes as those that do not require conceiving something additional to the thing itself when describing it—like humanity, reality, existence, and thingness for a human. These are contrasted with meaning-attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya), which do require conceiving something additional, like spatial occupation and temporal origination. This classification is foundational for later discussions in kalam about how two things relate.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that similars share all accidental attributes as well.
Correction: The definition specifically restricts to essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-nafsiyya), not accidental ones.

Checkpoint

A) Opposites share all essential attributes.
B) Opposites cannot coexist in one locus from one aspect.
C) Opposites are not existents.
D) Opposites require an additional concept to be described.

Key Takeaway
The classification of any two things into similars, opposites, or different depends solely on whether they share essential attributes and whether their essences prevent coexistence.

Objection to the Threefold Division and Its Correction
The commentator objects to the threefold enumeration and proposes a correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the strict threefold division of any two things is logically flawed according to the commentator, and how it can be corrected by specifying the domain to accidents.

Core Idea
The threefold division fails unless restricted to existent accidents.

In the previous lesson, we learned about the threefold classification of any two things: similars, opposites, and different. However, this classification has logical issues. The commentator points out that it excludes mental entities and non-similar substances, and even the Necessary and possible. He refines the division by restricting it to accidents and noting that the first division is broader than the divided.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original claim
Read the passage and identify the threefold division being criticized. Note that the commentator begins by stating that restricting two things to three divisions is incorrect.
The original division is similars, opposites, and different.
2
Identify the objections
The commentator gives two main criticisms: first, the condition of existence excludes mental entities (al-umūr al-iʿtibāriyya); second, the condition of meaning in opposites excludes non-similar substances and also the Necessary with possible.
Non-similar substances cannot coexist in one locus, so they are not opposites.
3
Grasp the correction
The commentator says the division is not a strict logical division but a description of how two things can relate. If strict division is desired, restrict to accidents and note that similars can include substances.
The phrase 'according to the People of Truth' distinguishes this from other views (philosophers have four divisions, some theologians have two).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الاثنان إلخ لا يخفى أن حصر الاثنين في الأقسام الثلاثة غير صحيح، لأخذ قيد الوجود فيها، فالأمور الاعتبارية خارجة عنهما، ولأخذ قيد المعنى في الضدين، فالجواهر الغير المتماثلة خارجة عنهما وعن المتخالفين؛ لامتناع اجتماعهما في محل واحد، إذ لا محل لها . وكذا الواجب مع الممكن. وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن وجه الحصر الذي ذكره الشارح قدس سره غير صحيح، لورود المنع على قوله فالضدان وقوله وإلا فالمتخالفان فالوجه : أن يقال : المقصود أن الاثنين يوجد فيه الأقسام الثلاثة، وما ذكره الشارح قدس سره بيان لطريق حصولها . وإن أردت الحصر، فلا بد من تخصيص الاثنين بالأعراض، ومن القول بأن القسم الأول أعم من المقسم، لأن المثلين قد يكونان من الجواهر.

قوله : ( عند أهل الحق خلافاً للفلاسفة، فإنهما عندهم أربعة أقسام، ولبعض المتكلمين فإنهما عنده قسمان كما سيجيء .
His saying: ('The two, etc.' It is not hidden that restricting the two to three divisions is incorrect, because it takes the condition of existence in them, so mental considerations (al-umūr al-iʿtibāriyya) are excluded from them, and because it takes the condition of meaning in the opposites, so non-similar substances are excluded from them and from the different, due to the impossibility of their being together in one locus, since they have no locus. Likewise the Necessary (al-wājib) with the possible (al-mumkin). And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the aspect of restriction that the commentator (may his soul be sanctified) mentioned is incorrect, because objections arise against his saying 'then the opposites' and his saying 'otherwise the different.' So the correct way is to say: the intended meaning is that the two have these three divisions present in them, and what the commentator mentioned is an explanation of how they obtain. And if you want restriction, then it is necessary to specify the two as accidents, and to say that the first division is more general than the divided, because the similars may be from substances. His saying: (according to the People of Truth) in contrast to the philosophers, for with them they are four divisions, and according to some theologians they are with him two divisions, as will come.)
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-correction. The commentator begins by critiquing the classification just presented. He notes that the strict threefold division of any two things is flawed because it implicitly assumes both are existents, thereby excluding mental entities (al-umūr al-iʿtibāriyya). Furthermore, the definition of opposites includes a condition of 'meaning' (qayd al-maʿnā) that excludes non-similar substances (like different types of atoms) since they cannot share a locus anyway. Similarly, the Necessary Being (God) and the possible are not covered. He gives the example of non-similar substances and the Necessary with the possible as cases that fall outside the division. The commentator then proposes a corrected understanding: the three divisions are not an exhaustive logical division of all pairs, but rather a description of how two things can relate when they are of a certain kind. If one insists on a strict division, it must be restricted to accidents (aʿrāḍ) only, and even then the first division (similars) is broader than the divided (i.e., it includes substances). Finally, he notes that the phrase 'according to the People of Truth' distinguishes this from philosophers who have four divisions, and some theologians who have only two divisions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The threefold division (similars, opposites, different) applies to any two things without exception.
Correction: The commentator shows it only applies to existent accidents. Mental entities, non-similar substances, and the Necessary with possible are excluded unless restricted.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator identify as a flaw in the original threefold division?
A) It excludes mental entities.
B) It includes opposites where they should not be.
C) It fails to account for similars.
D) It contradicts the philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The threefold division of any two things is not universally exhaustive; it must be restricted to existent accidents for logical correctness.

?
The commentator shows how an assumption about a universal rule (the threefold division) is actually flawed when you examine its hidden conditions. What would it cost you to discover it is incomplete?
?
The passage points out that even a logical classification can exclude critical categories like mental considerations or the Necessary Being. In your own thinking, do you tend to overlook categories that don't fit neatly into your mental frameworks? How might you be inadvertently excluding God or transcendent realities from your reasoning?
?
The correction requires restricting the division to accidents. This demands precision and humility in accepting the limits of our concepts. Where in your life are you prone to overgeneralize or claim more certainty than your knowledge warrants? Will you take a concrete step today to qualify your claims more humbly?

Resolving Circularity in the Definition of Similars
Sanusi presents an objection to the definition of similars and shows two ways to resolve the resulting circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi addresses the potential circularity when defining similars as sharing all essential attributes, and the two proposed solutions.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided by distinguishing the respect in which similarity is considered.

The definition of similars as sharing all essential attributes raises a subtle problem: if similarity itself is an essential attribute, the definition becomes circular. In this passage, Sanusi presents the objection and two responses that rescue the definition by either excluding similarity or by distinguishing between the concept and its role as an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and find why the definition of similars might be circular.
The objector says similarity depends on itself.
2
Understand the first answer
The first answer excludes similarity from the set of essential attributes considered.
This is a straightforward solution.
3
Understand the second answer
The second answer uses a distinction: similarity qua attribute vs. similarity qua concept.
This is more subtle and avoids circularity by changing the 'title'.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why the second answer is philosophically richer and how it preserves the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في جميع الصفات النفسية قيل : ثبوت التماثل على هذا التقدير يتوقف على تحقق الاشتراك في جميع الصفات النفسية، ومن جملتها التماثل على ما صرح به بعيد، هذا فيتوقف التماثل على نفسه وأجيب تارة بتخصيص الصفات بغير التماثل وأخرى بأن التماثل يتوقف على التماثل، لا باعتبار أنه تماثل بل باعتبار أنه من الصفات النفسية، فيختلف العنوان، ويندفع الدور.
His saying: (In all essential attributes, it is said: the establishing of similarity (al-tamāthul) according to this estimation depends on the realization of sharing in all essential attributes, and among them is similarity as he explicitly stated, so this is far away; thus similarity depends on itself. And it was answered sometimes by specifying the attributes as other than similarity, and sometimes by saying that similarity depends on similarity, not inasmuch as it is similarity, but inasmuch as it is among the essential attributes, so the title differs, and the circularity is repelled.)
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of similars. The objection points out that if similars are defined as two things sharing all essential attributes, and similarity (al-tamāthul) itself is one of the essential attributes, then the definition is circular: similarity would depend on itself. The objector says this is 'far away' (baʿīd) i.e. problematic. The answer is twofold. One response is to specify that the essential attributes meant exclude similarity itself. Another response is more subtle: similarity does depend on itself, but not in terms of its own concept (that would be circular) but in terms of being one of the essential attributes. In other words, the 'title' or respect differs: what is being proved is similarity, but what is used in the definition is similarity qua essential attribute, not qua similarity per se. Thus the circularity is resolved.

Key Terms
تماثل
توقف
دور
صفات نفسية
تخصيص
عنوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of similars as sharing all essential attributes is inherently circular and thus invalid.
Correction: The circularity can be avoided either by excluding similarity from the essential attributes or by distinguishing between similarity as a concept and similarity as an attribute, thereby changing the respect.

Checkpoint
How does the second answer resolve the circularity in defining similars by all essential attributes?
A) By removing similarity from the set of essential attributes.
B) By stating that similarity depends on something else.
C) By allowing the circularity as harmless.
D) By distinguishing between similarity as a concept and similarity as an essential attribute.

Key Takeaway
The apparent circularity in defining similars through essential attributes is resolved by either excluding similarity or by distinguishing the aspect of consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever define something in a way that makes it depend on itself—like justifying a habit by the very habit? Sanusi shows that such circularity can be broken by changing the respect. What is one area where you need to apply this distinction between 'what you are defining' and 'the attributes it must have'? Commit to mentally rewriting that definition today.
?
When you find yourself trapped in a theological or personal deadlock, do you have the intellectual humility to consider that the problem might be in how you are looking at it, not in the thing itself? Sanusi's second response invites you to ask: 'Am I confusing the respect?' Try this right now: think of a disagreement or doubt you have, and ask yourself whether you are conflating two different respects. What changes?
?
The objector assumed similarity cannot be both an attribute and a relation. Yet Sanusi shows it can, under different respects. Is there a belief you hold that you assume must be one way or the other, but actually could be both? Let this lesson force you to re-examine that assumption with the same precision, and write down one specific way you will adjust your thinking.

Essential Attributes Defined and Distinguished
The commentary glosses the definition of essential attributes and contrasts them with meaning-attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defining characteristic of essential attributes and how they differ from meaning-attributes.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are derived from the thing itself, not from an external concept.

The commentator begins by clarifying the definition of essential attributes. He explains that an essential attribute is one that does not require conceiving something external to the thing being described; it is derived from the thing itself or a part of it. This immediately distinguishes it from meaning-attributes, which depend on an external concept. This lesson unpacks this foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening gloss
Carefully read the commentator's gloss on the phrase 'that which does not require...' and note how he explains derivation from the thing itself.
The key term is 'muntazaʿ' (derived).
2
Contrast with meaning-attributes
Identify the definition of meaning-attributes and their examples (spatial occupation, temporality). Observe that they require an external concept.
This contrast is central to the whole discussion.
3
See the confusion repelled
Understand how the correct formulation resolves a confusion some had about essential attributes requiring an additional concept.
The confusion stemmed from misidentifying examples like 'humanity' as meaning-attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ما لا يحتاج في وصف الشيء) أي توصيفه به إلى تعقل أمر خارج عن نفس ذلك الشيء، بأن يكون منتزعاً من نفسه، أو من جزئه كالحيوانية للإنسان، فما لا تكون منتزعاً من نفس الشيء صفة معنوية سواء كانت موجودة كالتحيز، أو معدومة كالحدوث. وبما حررنا لك اندفع التحيز الذي عرض لبعض الناظرين حيث قال : لا يخفى أن الظاهر من هذه العبارة أن تكون
هي التي وصف الشيء به إلى تعقل أمر زائد عليه كالإنسانية والحقيقة والوجود والشيئية للإنسان، وتقابلها الصفات المعنوية التي تحتاج في الوصف بها إلى تعقل أمر زائد على ذات الموصوف كالتحيز والحدوث، وبعبارة أخرى : الصفة النفسية تدل على الذات دون معنى زائد عليها والمعنوية ما تدل على معنى زائد على الذات وقال بعضهم بناء على الحال، وكونها زائدة على الذات مع كونها من صفات النفس

الصفة النفسية، ما لا تكون زائدة على ذات الموصوف، وحينئذ يتوجه أن مفهوم لفظ الحقيقة والشيئية والوجود كلها زائدة على ذات الإنسان وإن أريد أنها ما لا تكون مفتقرة إلى ملاحظة أمر خارج مغاير للموصوف، أي ما لا يكون إضافياً، يشكل التماثل؛ لأنه إضافي. وإن أريد أنه لا يعلل بغير الذات، فيشكل بالوجود، وإن أريد أنه لا يكون مغايراً للذات في الخارج يتناول سائر الاعتبارات .
His saying: (That which does not require in describing the thing) i.e. in describing it by it, the conceiving of a matter external to that thing itself, such that it is derived (muntazaʿ) from itself or from a part of it, like animality for man. So that which is not derived from the thing itself is a meaning-attribute (ṣifa maʿnawiyya), whether it is existent like spatial occupation, or non-existent like temporality. And by what we have determined for you, the confusion that occurred to some observers is repelled, where they said: It is not hidden that the apparent meaning of this expression is that it is that which describes the thing by the conceiving of an additional matter beyond it, like humanity, reality, existence, and thingness for man; and its opposite are the meaning-attributes which require in describing by them the conceiving of an additional matter beyond the described subject, like spatial occupation and temporality. And in another expression: the essential attribute (al-ṣifa al-nafsiyya) indicates the essence without an additional meaning upon it, and the meaning-attribute indicates an additional meaning upon the essence. And some said, based on the state (al-ḥāl), that it (the essential attribute) is additional upon the essence while being among the attributes of the self. The essential attribute: that which is not additional upon the described essence. And then it is pointed out that the concept of the terms reality, thingness, and existence are all additional upon the essence of man. And if it is intended that it is not in need of noticing an external matter different from the described thing, i.e. it is not relational, then similarity is problematic because it is relational. And if it is intended that it is not caused by other than the essence, then existence is problematic. And if it is intended that it is not different from the essence externally, then it includes all considerations.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-nafsiyya). It begins by glossing the phrase 'that which does not require...': describing something by an essential attribute does not require conceiving something external to that thing; rather the attribute is derived from the thing itself or a part of it (e.g., animality from man). In contrast, meaning-attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) require an external concept, like spatial occupation (requiring a location) or temporality (requiring prior non-existence). The commentator then notes that this formulation repels a confusion some had. He then presents another expression: the essential attribute indicates the essence without an additional meaning, while the meaning-attribute indicates an additional meaning. Some scholars, however, define essential attributes based on the concept of 'state' (al-ḥāl) and say the attribute is additional to the essence yet still an attribute of the self. A third definition is that the essential attribute is that which is not additional upon the essence. The commentator then points out difficulties with each interpretation: if we define it as not requiring an external notice, then similarity (which is relational) would be problematic. If we define it as not caused by something else, then existence (which may need a cause) is problematic. If we define it as not externally different, then it includes too many things. This discussion shows the complexity in pinning down the exact meaning of 'essential attribute'.

Key Terms
صفة نفسية
صفة معنوية
منتزع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some thought essential attributes are those requiring an additional concept (like humanity).
Correction: Essential attributes are actually those that do NOT require an external concept; they are derived from the thing itself.

Checkpoint
Which attribute is given as an example of a meaning-attribute in this passage?
A) Animality
B) Humanity
C) Spatial occupation
D) Reality

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are intrinsic to the thing itself, while meaning-attributes depend on an external concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as 'a believer', is that attribute derived from your own essence or from external actions and affirmations? How does this lesson challenge you to ground your identity in what is truly intrinsic?
?
Consider a time you misunderstood a core belief because you assumed an attribute required an external cause. How can letting go of that assumption bring you closer to a purer understanding of Allah and yourself?

Problems with Alternative Definitions of Essential Attributes
The commentator examines and critiques three alternative definitions of essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why several proposed definitions of essential attributes fail due to counterexamples like similarity and existence.

Core Idea
Defining essential attributes as 'not relational' or 'not caused' leads to contradictions.

After establishing the core definition, the commentator addresses other interpretations. Some define essential attributes as not requiring an external notice (non-relational), not caused by something else, or not externally distinct. The commentator shows each has a problem, using examples like similarity and existence. This lesson examines these critiques.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three alternative definitions
Read the passage and note three ways scholars tried to define 'not additional': non-relational, not caused, and not externally different.
These are introduced sequentially with 'إن أريد...'
2
Understand the first problem with similarity
If essential means non-relational, then similarity (which implies a relation) would count as essential, but it is not.
Similarity is relational, so it would be wrongly included.
3
Understand the second problem with existence
If essential means not caused, then existence (which may have a cause) would be excluded, but existence is considered essential.
Existence can be caused, so this definition fails.
4
Understand the third problem of over-inclusion
If essential means not externally distinct, then all attributes that are not separate entities would be essential, blurring the distinction.
This definition is too broad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

هي التي وصف الشيء به إلى تعقل أمر زائد عليه كالإنسانية والحقيقة والوجود والشيئية للإنسان، وتقابلها الصفات المعنوية التي تحتاج في الوصف بها إلى تعقل أمر زائد على ذات الموصوف كالتحيز والحدوث، وبعبارة أخرى : الصفة النفسية تدل على الذات دون معنى زائد عليها والمعنوية ما تدل على معنى زائد على الذات وقال بعضهم بناء على الحال، وكونها زائدة على الذات مع كونها من صفات النفس

الصفة النفسية، ما لا تكون زائدة على ذات الموصوف، وحينئذ يتوجه أن مفهوم لفظ الحقيقة والشيئية والوجود كلها زائدة على ذات الإنسان وإن أريد أنها ما لا تكون مفتقرة إلى ملاحظة أمر خارج مغاير للموصوف، أي ما لا يكون إضافياً، يشكل التماثل؛ لأنه إضافي. وإن أريد أنه لا يعلل بغير الذات، فيشكل بالوجود، وإن أريد أنه لا يكون مغايراً للذات في الخارج يتناول سائر الاعتبارات .

Meaning-Attributes vs Essential Attributes: Examples of Tahayyuz and Huduth
Commentary glossing terms and defending the definition of essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator distinguishes meaning-attributes from essential attributes using the examples of spatial occupation (tahayyuz) and temporal origination (huduth), and why describing a possible being by existence does not require conceiving the cause.

Core Idea
Meaning-attributes require noticing something external; essential attributes require only the essence itself.

This lesson unpacks several key glosses from the commentary that clarify the definition of essential attributes. The commentator uses concrete examples—spatial occupation and temporality—to show why they are not essential but meaning-attributes. He also addresses a potential objection about existence to further refine the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on tahayyuz and huduth
Notice the commentator explains why both are meaning-attributes: they require noticing a locus (al-ḥayyiz) or non-existence, unlike humanity which is derived from the essence itself.
This sets the foundation for distinguishing essential vs. meaning-attributes.
2
Understand the definition of essential attribute
The commentator defines essential attribute as that which requires no additional concept other than the attribute itself. He discusses the phrase 'indicates the essence' and 'without an additional meaning', noting that attributes indicating a part of the essence are included.
Pay attention to the distinction between indicating the essence itself vs. indicating with an additional meaning.
3
Examine the objection about existence
The commentator considers if describing a possible by existence requires conceiving the efficient cause. He denies this, saying existence in reality is from the cause but conceptualization does not depend on it.
This shows that existence is an essential attribute for possible beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كالتحيز) فإن التوصيف به يحتاج إلى ملاحظة الحيز والحدوث، فإنه يحتاج إلى ملاحظة العدم، وليس شيء منهما منتزعاً من نفس الإنسان مثلاً . قوله : ( تدل على الذات ) أي نفسه دلالة اللازم على الملزوم . قوله دون معنى زائد أي خارج عنها، أشار إلى أن ما يدل على جزء الذات داخل في الصفة النفسية. قوله : ( وكونها زائدة على الذات) فلا يكون منتزعاً من نفس الذات، فتحتاج في الوصف به إلى ملاحظة أمر سوى الذات، فلا يصدق التعريف عليها. قوله : (مع كونها من صفات النفس أما إذا كانت معللة بالصفات الحقيقية، فهي اخلة في الصفة المعنوية . قوله : ( ما لا يحتاج وصف الشيء به إلى تعقل أمر زائد قيل : أي غير هذه الصفة، وقيل: الكلام مبني على أن الوصف عين الماهية، وهو الأظهر. قوله : ( والوجود) فإن قلت : وصف الممكن بالوجود يحتاج إلى تعقل الفاعل الموجد، قلت : ممنوع نعم وجوده في نفس الأمر من الفاعل لكن لا توقف في التعقل . قوله : ( كالتحيز والحدوث) فإن الأول زائد على ذات الجوهر؛ لأنه باعتبار الجسمية، وتعقله . والثاني زائد على ذات الحادث؛ لأنه باعتبار العدم السابق وتعقله . واعلم أن عد الحدوث صفة معنوية مخالف لما في أبكار الأفكار حيث صرح في بحث المتخالفين في موضعين بأن الحدوث من الصفات النفسية. قوله : ( بناء على الحال ، وكونها زائدة على الذات من الأحوال ما يصح خلو الموصوف عنها، كعالمية زيد مثلاً، لكن الأحوال التي جعلوها من الصفات النفسية على هذا التفسير هي الأحوال اللازمة، كما سيشير إليه الشارح عن قريب .
His saying: (like spatial occupation (al-taḥayyuz)): for describing by it requires noticing the locus (al-ḥayyiz); and temporality (al-ḥudūth) requires noticing non-existence, and neither of them is derived from the essence of man, for example. His saying: (indicates the essence) i.e. itself, by the indication of the necessary to the necessitated. His saying: 'without an additional meaning' i.e. external to it; he indicated that what indicates a part of the essence is included in the essential attribute. His saying: (and its being additional upon the essence) means it is not derived from the essence itself, so describing by it requires noticing something other than the essence, so the definition does not apply to it. His saying: (while being from the attributes of the self) but if they are caused by the real attributes, then they are included in the meaning-attribute. His saying: (that which does not require in describing the thing by it the conceiving of an additional matter) it is said: i.e. other than this attribute; and it is said: the discussion is based on the description being identical to the essence, and this is more apparent. His saying: (and existence): for if you say: describing the possible by existence requires conceiving of the efficient cause (al-fāʿil al-mūjid), I say: denied; yes, its existence in reality is from the cause, but there is no dependence in conception. His saying: (like spatial occupation and temporality): for the first is additional upon the essence of the substance because it is by virtue of corporeality (al-jismiyya) and its conception; and the second is additional upon the essence of the temporally originated because it is by virtue of prior non-existence and its conception. And know that counting temporality (al-ḥudūth) as a meaning-attribute violates what is in 'Abkār al-Afkār', where he explicitly stated in the discussion of the different (al-mutakhālifān) in two places that temporality is among the essential attributes. His saying: (based on the state (al-ḥāl), and its being additional upon the essence from among the states that can be absent from the described, like the knowledge of Zayd for example; but the states that they made from the essential attributes according to this interpretation are the necessary states (al-aḥwāl al-lāzima), as the commentator will soon indicate.)
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a series of glosses on specific words and concepts in the definition. It first explains why spatial occupation and temporality are meaning-attributes: they require noticing a locus or non-existence respectively, unlike humanity which is derived from the human itself. Then it explains that 'indicates the essence' means the attribute itself indicates the essence, like a necessary implies its necessitated. The phrase 'without an additional meaning' excludes attributes that indicate a part of the essence, which are included in essential attributes. The commentator also discusses the phrase 'and its being additional upon the essence' meaning the attribute is not derived from the essence. He then addresses a potential objection about existence: describing a possible by existence does not require conceiving the efficient cause; its existence in reality is from the cause but conceptualization does not depend on that. Next, he clarifies that spatial occupation is additional upon the substance because it involves corporeality, and temporality is additional upon the originated thing because it involves prior non-existence. Importantly, he notes that counting temporality (al-ḥudūth) as a meaning-attribute contradicts the position in 'Abkār al-Afkār' where it is considered an essential attribute. Finally, he explains the interpretation based on 'state' (al-ḥāl): the additional attribute is like the state of being knowing, which can be absent; but the states that are essential attributes are the necessary ones (lāzima), which always accompany the essence.

صفة حقيقية

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why are tahayyuz and huduth not essential attributes?
A) Because they are derived from the essence
B) Because they require noticing something external
C) Because they are not real attributes
D) Because they are not known through reason

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are those that require only the essence itself to be conceptualized, while meaning-attributes require noticing something external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's existence, do you unconsciously tie it to a cause, like the creation around you? This lesson says conceptualizing existence does not require the cause. Is your heart at peace that Allah's existence is essential and self-evident, without needing external proof? If not, what would it take to truly internalize that?
?
Do you ever imagine Allah as being 'somewhere' or occupying a space? This passage teaches that spatial occupation requires a locus, which only created things have. How can you actively purify your belief so that you never, even in fleeting thoughts, assign a place to Allah? What practical step will you take today?

Consider one attribute you commonly use to describe Allah—like 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Powerful'. Is it possible that you understand it through a human lens, mixing it with created qualities? According to the lesson, essential attributes are intrinsically derived from Allah's essence. How can you ensure that your understanding of His attributes is not contaminated by external, human concepts?

The Disagreement on Huduth: From Meaning-Attribute to Essential Attribute
Gloss with a doctrinal note about huduth and explanation of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement about whether temporal origination (huduth) is a meaning-attribute or an essential attribute, and how the concept of 'state' (al-hal) relates to essential attributes.

Core Idea
Even though huduth seems to require non-existence, some authorities like al-Āmidī consider it an essential attribute.

This lesson examines a later gloss where the commentator repeats the examples of spatial occupation and temporality, but then notes a significant disagreement: in 'Abkār al-Afkār', huduth is classified as an essential attribute, not a meaning-attribute. The lesson also introduces the interpretation based on the concept of 'state' (al-ḥāl), explaining which states are considered essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on tahayyuz and huduth again
The commentator reasserts that tahayyuz is additional upon the substance because it involves corporeality, and huduth is additional upon the originated thing because it involves prior non-existence.
This reinforces the classification as meaning-attributes.
2
Note the disagreement about huduth
The commentator explicitly states that counting huduth as a meaning-attribute violates the position in 'Abkār al-Afkār' where it is considered an essential attribute. This is a notable difference between authorities.
Reflect on why huduth might be seen differently—perhaps because it is a necessary concomitant of originatedness itself.
3
Understand the interpretation based on 'state' (al-hal)
The commentator explains that when we say the attribute is additional upon the essence, it can be from among the states that can be absent, like knowledge of Zayd. But the states that are essential attributes are the necessary states (lāzima), which always accompany the essence.
This clarifies that essential attributes are necessary concomitants, not accidental states.

The Denial of Tamathul and the Response
Objection and response regarding tamathul impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against tamathul (likeness) and the response that distinguishes between essential and accidental attributes to defend the possibility of tamathul.

Core Idea
Perfect similarity does not require sameness in every respect; only essential attributes matter.

This lesson examines a logical objection against the concept of tamathul (likeness) and its rebuttal. The objector argues that if two things are identical in all respects they are not distinct, and if they differ in any respect they are not perfectly similar. The response clarifies that tamathul only requires identity in psychological (essential) attributes, not accidental ones, preserving the threefold classification of pairs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the logical flow of objection and response come through.
Notice that the objection uses two conditional statements.
2
Identify the two conditionals
Find the two 'if' statements in the objection: 'if they share in every aspect' and 'if they differ in some aspect'. Understand the conclusion that the categories of two are not three.
3
Understand the response
The response denies the second conditional. Ask yourself: why is it valid to say that two things can differ in non-psychological attributes while still being mutamathilayn (similar)?
This distinction is key to saving the concept of tamathul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الناس من ينفي التماثل، لأن الشيئين إن اشتركا من كل وجه فلا تمايز فلا اثنينية فضلا عن التماثل. أو اختلفا من وجه فلا تماثل، فلا تكون أقسام الاثنين عنده ثلاثة. والجواب : منع الشرطية الثانية، إذ قد يختلفان بغير الصفة النفسية مع الاشتراك في جميع صفات النفس.
Then among people are those who deny tamathul, because if two things share in every aspect, there is no distinction, so no twoness, let alone tamathul; or if they differ in some aspect, there is no tamathul. So, according to him, the categories of two are not three. The answer: denial of the second conditional, for they may differ in a non-psychological attribute while sharing all psychological attributes.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that some people deny tamathul (likeness) altogether. Their reasoning is a conditional: if two things are identical in all respects, they are not two distinct things, so they cannot be similar; if they differ in any respect, they are not perfectly similar. Therefore, the threefold classification of pairs is invalid. The response refutes the second conditional by pointing out that two things can differ in non-psychological attributes (like accidental qualities) while still sharing all psychological attributes (essential ones), which is sufficient for tamathul.

Key Terms
التماثل
الاثنينية
الصفة النفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any difference between two things prevents them from being mutamathilayn (similar).
Correction: Only differences in psychological (essential) attributes prevent tamathul; differences in accidental (non-psychological) attributes do not affect tamathul.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the argument that difference in any respect prevents tamathul?
A) Both conditionals are denied.
B) The second conditional is denied because difference can occur in non-psychological attributes.
C) The first conditional is denied because identical things can still be two.
D) Tamathul is impossible regardless.

Key Takeaway
Tamathul (likeness) is possible between two distinct things if they share all psychological (essential) attributes, even if they differ in accidental ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does the lesson's distinction between essential and accidental attributes challenge you to see beyond the surface? Will you intentionally seek shared essential qualities today?

What would change if you applied the tamathul principle to your judgments? What concrete step can you take to recognise essential likeness in someone you have overlooked?
?
If tamathul requires identity in all psychological attributes, what does that mean for your understanding of your own essence? Are you defined by your essential attributes (iman, character) or by accidentals? What would it look like to focus on cultivating essential traits rather than preoccupying with superficial differences?
